THE RED PAGE.
One ponders with a flickering mouth the com-
plication of motives which impels our young
authoresses to endorse their literature with their
marriage-lines. The initial irascible ‘ What the
deuce is he doing on board this galley ? ’ soon
gives place to flattered male curiosity. Is it a
self-adulatory ‘ Observe my brevet! ’ or a self-
abnegatory ‘ Lo, all that I have is thine ! ’ or
a mixture of half-a-dozen things—this common
emotion which determines the title-page announce-
ment of ‘ Kirkham’s Find, by Mary Gaunt (Mrs.
H. Lindsay Miller),’ and of ‘ Teens/by Louise
Mack (Mrs. J. Percy Creed),’ and of ‘Miss
Bobbie, by Ethel Turner (Mrs. H. R. Curlewis) ’’
* * * * ' ’
About ‘ Kirkham’s Find ’ there is little to ear.
■The author dribbles commonplaces over a vast
number of pages through which lacklustre char-
acters walk slowly and gossip about nothing in
particular. In ‘ Dave’s Sweetheart ’ Mary
Gaunt left room for hope that Bhe might
become something more than the average
fluent female penster. There is dramatic
power in that book, and, so far as the women are
concerned, a good deal of dramatic insight. But
‘Dave’s Sweetheart’ for the most part lacks in-
tensity. The incidents are lost in a flux of flabby
words. The author attenuates in ten sentences
an impression which a vigorous writer would
paint vividly in two. The collection of short
stories called ‘The Moving Finger ’ exhibited the
same fault without compensating virtue. And
‘Kirkham’s Find ’ touches the nadir of feeble
flatulency. Scenes and people are indeed photo-
graphed minutely, with feminine precision; but, as
the author’s mind has no depth or scope or origi-
nality wherewith to glorify them, the whole is
only a sorry small-beer chronicle. And the
average woman’s pre-eminence as a small-beer
ohronioler was already well established.
* * • *
‘ Teens ’ is Louise Mack’s second book to see
light—the first, ‘ The World is Round/ dates last
year. Then Angus and Robertson, who publish
‘Teens,’ have a sequel to issue in 1898; a
London publisher many months ago accepted a
collection of short stories ; and there was a serial,
‘ln An Australian City,’published in a Sydney
magazine. Add many verses and a little mixed
journal-work ; —and admire the lady’s industry !
• * * m
‘ Teens ’ is sub-titled ‘ A Story of Australian
Sohoolgirls ’ naturally for Australian school-
girls. It is written so well that it could not be
written better. The author has taken her own
memories, enhaloed with the charm of childhood,
the romance of girlhood, and set them on paper so
delicately and so skilfully that not one iridescent
tint seems lost from life. Thus to look into one’s
heart is easy ; thus to write is the hardest thing
imaginable. It is less hard for a woman, because
women remain physiologically and characteristi-
cally nearer to children—their minds retain always
something of the childish standpoint and outlook.
Many women bloom with childhood’s fragrance all
their lives. But the art to scent with this frag-
rance an atmosphere of words is exceedingly rare
and admirable.
» * * *
Somebody has said that ‘ to be beautiful, a child-
story need be neither sad, nor mad, nor bad. If
it is the work of an artist it may be as natural as
you please, as happy, healthy, and pure spirited,
and yet be a work of art.’ ‘Teens’ fulfils these
conditions. Like Longfellow’s Priscilla, it is
‘modest, and simple, and sweet.’ Only in the
‘happy ’ quality does it fail a little : it wants high
spirits. The author communicates the right im-
pulse in the right way, but does not quite arrive
at the force of childhood’s irresistible impulse.
Compared with ‘ Little Women/that permanent
standard of girls’-book all-but-perfection, ‘ Teens ’
has more cleverness and less vivacity. It is no
less natural than ‘Little Women,’ but it is less typi-
cal ; because—because Louise Mack seems to have
been a little farther from the normal girl-urchfn
than Louisa Alcott. The difference must be prac-
tically imperceptible, however, for a normal girl-
critic r ‘ght a B e gives assurance that both
Teens ’ and ‘Little Women’ are books ‘to hug
tc bed with.’
*****
Ethel Turner’s ‘ Miss Bobbie ’ completes her
round half-dozen volumes : she will soon need a
whole shelf to herself. Thanks to some years-ago-
written chapters, ‘when the heart was young,’
‘Miss Bobbie’ quite maintains the author’s ex-
cellent level. It is the funniest of her books :
perhaps some of the fun is impossible fun ; but you
have to laugh, anyway—the exploits of ‘Suds’
will be content with nothing less. And, if you
take Ethel Turner’s artificial child-fairyland for
granted, and don’t look her gift ogres in the mouth,
‘Miss Bobbie’ is pleasant and interesting through-
out. The author is always in the stranger chil-
dren’s country—her experiences must have been
made out of the things not given to anybody else
—but qualms are so few and small in comparison
with charms that it would be ungrateful not to
swallow them and look sweet.
* * * *
A cheaper edition of Kenneth Mackay’s ‘The
Yellow Wave’ is published. Nearly two years
ago it.was here mentioned as a valuable, memor-
able book—less for its literary than for its national
quality. It urges the truth that Australians will
some day have to fight for Australia, and that now
is the time to get ready. Withal, the story is
readable. British journals—* The Spectator, ■’* The
Academy/ and others—Draise it highly.
♦ * ' ~ * *
‘Darab’s Wine-Cup and Other Tales/ by Bart
Kennedy (Olliff) is a book just on the edge of being
memorable—and it might as well be in the abyss.
The author has a tremendous force of Celtic im-
agination, though it is not a very original force in
essence or effects. His style has rugged value ;
yet its crudity is continually close to anti-climax.
Noteworthy that the typically imaginative Irish-
man has rarely any sense of humorous incon-
gruity.
• * * *
Glittering Anthony Hope seems dead !
Read at his last new book an hour.
That Is the story, this its thread,
He penned the tale, but it lacks his power.
Beginning to languish, too, like the rest;
Much has been changed by his fame, I think:
By ravenous publishers opprest,
He drowns his genius in seas of ink.
It is not too late yet, Anthony Hope, •
You can redeem yourself, firm and true;
For good stars meet in your horoscope,
Your earlier characters live and woo,
Flirt, laugh, skirmish—fight as of old,
And bewitch us—this oannot be denied;
Toward ‘The Prisoner ’ and ‘ Dolly ’ no heart is cold,
They are fellow mortals—naught beside.
We love you, Anthony, all the while 1
There are better things yet to come, we hold ;
There is place and to spare for villain’s guile,
And your nonchalant heroes—overbold.
So hush—here’s a bit of advice to keep ;
We want your genius fresh from your hand.
There, that is your secret, don’t try to reap
Gold for mere trash—you understand !
From ‘ The Philistine.'
* * • *
America has now some storytellers who rank
with all but the very best elsewhere. And, what
is good to say, two or three of them seem emerg-
ing from the attenuated air of Boston culture
which has taken the red-corpuscles out of
so much American literature—seem well-pleased to
put nerve and strength into their work, heedless
of dogmatic fetishes and academic canons. For
one example, there is Hamlin Garland, a young
man still in the sunny thirties. His short stories
collected in ‘Main-Travelled Roads,’ in 1892,
describe some phases of American life as truly as
Henry Lawson’s stories describe some phases
of Australian; but Garland has breadth and
art to which Lawson has not yet attained.
Between ‘ Main-Travelled Roads ’ and ‘ Rose of
Dutcher’s Coolly/ issued last year, there was a
second book of short stories which does not seem to
have found an English publisher. (Australian
readers are far too dependent upon English
publishers : many excellent American books are
never seen in this country at all).
* * * *
The quality of ‘ Rose ’ is no whit better than
that of ‘Up the Coul6 ’ in ‘ Main-Travelled
Roads ’; she doubtfully leaves a stronger
impression than ‘Among, the Corn-Rows’; but
she fills a long story, and counts as a
greater effort, a more complete performance.
To give an idea of Garland’s literary stature,
it may be said that ‘ Rose of Dutcher’s
Coolly’ stands level with ‘Tess of the D’Urber-
villes.’ Garland, indeed, writes something in
Hardy’s manner, sensuously, keynoting in the
domination of the flesh. His ideal woman is
a magnificent creature six feet high, with bosom
and imagination, and hips and intellect, and
sweet blood and clear eyes (great stress on the
sweet blood and clear eyes), and a gait like a god-
dess. Likely enough Garland himself is an under-
sized little rat of a fellow, with a big head and no
physique worth mentioning—for that’s how things
usually go.
* » * *
Garland has not Hardy’s philosophy and Active
science ; but he has youth and fervency. ' Tess ’
wanted hot blood to make a masterpiece of her.
‘ Rose ’ is more simple and natural; she flows like
life itself, without an obvious motive ; and you can
feel Hardy’s motives all through his later works-
sources of profound artistic irritation. There is
no such trail-of-the-tract in Garland’s book.
If. Rose has a moral, she sheds it uncon-
sciously—it is her example, not her precept,
which urges every woman to go and be a magnifi-
cent creature six feet high. Some commenta-
tors have thought that Garland’s stress on
Rose’s sex —his presentation of the whole
woman, or something like her, without the
usual glosses and suppressions argues him
a woman himself. Nominally he is not; es-
sentially he may have a majority of feminine
elements. When it is generally recognised that,
in Weismann’s words, to every individual ‘the
primary constituents of both sexes are always
passed on from both sides/ there will be opened a
fine field of usefulness to the indeterminate pro-
noun se.
• * » *
Robert W. Chambers is another very capable
young American. His book of verse, ‘ With the
Band,’ has a touch of Kipling’s manner, but little
of Kipling’s strength. His novel, ‘ The Red Re-
public,’ is a pleasant, interesting story in the
second or third class, weaving a skein of romance
through the ugly days of the Commune. Inci-
dentally it shows once more how unneces-
sary it is for a lover to take out an in-
surance policy : he is bound to issue un-
scathed at page 350, or thereabouts : impavidum
feriunt shot and shell, Maxims and mitrail-
leuses. But artistically ‘ The Red Republic ’
is not so good as Chambers’s short stories —some
of which Poe would not be ashamed to sign. ‘ The
Maker of Moons/for example; or ‘The Messen-
ger.’ Then Chambers has another vein, an Am-
brose-Bieree vein, of which ‘ln the Name of the
Most High ’ is exemplar. And he has a third
vein, a vein of commonplace tenderness touched
with fancy, avoiding equally the mystic climax
and the warlike. Apparently he has not yet
settled which is his true line—if his versatility
will permit him to have one true line. But even
now both his promise and performance stand high.
* * * *
Surely there never was such a creative imagina-
tion as that of 11. G. Wells. It is easy to say
that his"stories are but science made fiction ; and
that, given the keynote, the tune plays itself.
But, apart from the fact that nobody but Wells
seems able to strike his keynotes, such an explana-
tion errs visibly. It is not the discovery, but the
development of his ideas that makes his talent so
astounding. The conception of a movement in
time, for example, akin to movement in space, is
not remarkably novel. Hundreds of people may
well have had it. But not one writer in a thousand
could utilise it at all—not one in a million could
utilise it as Wells has utilised it. Or take the
conception of an angel’s visit to earth—familiar to
everybody. What did everybody do with it till
Wells came and built his deiicious extravaganza ?
To be sure, there is nothing that is essentially
literature in all Wells’s work : his books are
merely brilliant tours de force —juggling feats of
fancy ; but they make uncommonly good reading.
Wells’s latest story, ‘The Invisible Man,’ is
just as clever as its predecessors. Most people
know that we see certain material objects because
they react to light in various ways—by reflection,
or refraction, or absorption, or by all together.
Well, the invisible man chemically causes such a
change in the molecules of his body that they
react to light precisely as common air does—so
that he cannot be seen any more than air can.
And he reckons he is going to have a good time—
seeing, for example, that he can walk into a bank
and help himself to sovereigns without anybody
being the wiser. If the theft was observed, all
the teller would see would be a handful of sovereigns
floating through the air ‘on their own’—and he
would probably be too astonished to protest
until too late. Unfortunately things don’t turn
out at all as the invisible man expects. England
is a cold country, for one thing; and he has to
wear clothes ; and when people see suits of clothes
walking round with nothing in them they natur-
ally make unpleasant remarks. By degrees the
man becomes an invisible Ishmaelite. For a
while he has the best of it—seeing that he is able
to trip up an opponent with an invisible foot, or
knock him down with an invisible fist; but in the
end he is hunted down and battered to death.
It’s a rattling story.
* * * »
One considers Hall Oaine with astonishment.
He worked up through more or less crude melo-
drama until in ‘ The Manxman ’ he reached the
strength of tragedy. That was indeed a fine
novel, epic in conception, noble in execution—
simple, true, and strong. And from ‘The Manx-
man ’ Hall Oaine drops all at once to the level of
‘The Christian’ false in art, in treatment
tawdry and hysterical. There is rarely such a
pulling of puppet - strings outside the penny
novelette. The characters, are fustian through-
out ; their talk is rant; their unnatural actions
are sawn to suit the author’s ridiculous precon-
ceptions. And a 50-000 edition of such a book
was sold at 6s. in a fortnight!
* * * *
Indeed, the book is well sauced to suit a pious
public. Cant alternates with lust, religious-mania
with sex-mania—which is just, since these are
obverse and reverse of the same medal.
♦ * * *
So the Nonconformist conscience is tickled now
by the spectacle of the Rev. John Storm de-
nouncing the worldliness of his ecclesiastical
superiors, and now by the spectacle of Glory
Quayle seducing a music-hall audience. Anon
religious-interest and sex-interest are combined,
and John, coming to kill Glory for the good of her
soul, is seduced to stay for the good of his body.
If George Moore, say, depicted such a scene so
coarsely, in the name of Art, there would be ter-
rible puritan outcry ; but Hall Caine is privileged
to be quite Biblical in his crudity, because he
writes ‘ in the name of the Lord ’—for much more
than the usual cash consideration.
* * * *
The book closes with stage-fire. John Storm is
killed by the Machine-God, and Glory Quayle
marries him on a death-bed of bathos. One is
sorry for John, who is an amusing fanatic, par-
ticularly when he offers to take Glory away from
the naughty musio-halls to a nice little islandful
of lepers in the South Seas, where they will read
Scripture to the lepers, and he will love her like a
sister. And she a red-haired girl too !
NOCTURNE—IN PROVENCE.
Did the vain garments melt in music from vour side?
Did you rise from them as lily flowers i’ the air’
-But you were there before me like the Night’s own
bride—
I dared not call you mine. So still and tall you were
I never dreamed that you were mine—l never dreamed
I loved you—l forgot I loved you. You were air
And music, and the shadows that you stood in seemed
Like priest* that keep their sombre vigil round a shrine-
Like somore priests that watch about a glorious shrine.
And then you stepped into the moonlight and laid bare
The wonder of your body to the night, and stood
With all the stars of heaven looking at you there.
As simply as a saint might bare her soul to God—
As simply as a saint might bathe in lakes of prayer—
Stood with the holy moonlight falling on you there
Until I thought that in a glory unaware
I had seen a soul stand forth and bare itself to God
A saintly soul lay bare its innocence to God.
Carman and Hovey.
A [?]
Before the writers of to-day a harder problem lies
Than that which stood before the men who’ve won tin
laureled prize.
When Shakespeare was a novice at the art of writing
plays,
He’d no such competition as the men of modern days;
For when on paper first he put his pathos and his wit,
The plays of William Shakespeare were of course as yet
unwrit.
And so it was with Milton, when he sought life’s richest
pelf,
He never had a foeman like his highly-honored self.
Nor was there ever on the shelves in Addison’s great day,
Another gentle essayist to tourney for the bay.
And wondrous-witted Thackeray, in battling for hisfame,
Was not confronted everywhere by his own cherished
name.
And so, ye critics, gently deal with those poor wights
who now
Are struggling for the wreath that fame puts on the
worthy brow ;
For it is true, past question, that the raoe is harder won,
Because of what these giants of the gloried past have
done. . . .
J. K. Bangs.

they have investigated further into the mysteries of
life, and penetrated deeper the secrets of disease,
than their quite worthy but less successful associ-

is lifelong, and their record right here in SYDNEY,
IS A SERIES OF PROFESSIONAL TRIUMPHS.
There are thousands upon thousands in this land
to-day who are praising their names, because
they have made their lives happy and brought
sunshine into their homes
m 3NIKU

Various Verses .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IN THE ATTIC.
What does it matter what they say
While there is the sunset, there the stars,
And over the city’s mistiness
The moon comes out of her silver bars.
And somewhere out of the sight of eye
The river runs through a low, long mist,
Under the bridge where the lovers cross
Into the fields for their evening tryst.
What does it matter ? Up and up
The mounting staircase twists and winds,
Till, see ! the starlight is almost touched,
The world that hates us is left behind.
Open the door with the rusty key,
Close and lock it, and enter in :
Straightway walk into Paradise
And let your time as a god begin.
Here in the attic all things fade
And dwindle into their own small size ;
Brain-fires burn when the coals go out,
And stars shine in with solacing eyes,
And weave a ladder into the room,
And wave and beckon until we dare
The first frail foot-hold. Then they turn
And veil their windows and leave us there,
Low and alone on the silver stair,
The attic window out of our sight,
The star’s gates hidden in mystery,
The shining ladder our only light.
Rolled in the mighty atmosphere
We stumble heavenwards bar by bar,
Through the midnights, till feet refuse,
And reel and tremble —and there’s the star.
What if the attic had not been !
A silver ladder would never dare
Down the stars to the basement world
Whose dirt would tarnish the shining stair.
Little it matters when the world
Tosses laughter against your mind.
Up to the attic’s loneliness !
Leave the world and the laugh behind
Open the door with the rusty key,
Close and lock it, and enter in ;
Straightway walk into Paradise,
And let your time as a god begin.
LOUISE MACK.
THEN LOVE ME!
If thou didst love me, all the hearts of men
Should be deep-stirred
With song’s divinest rapture—just as when
A full-heart bird
Is heard to thrill the very sky’s blue beauty
With wordless glee
Because his love has taught him song’s sweet duty
Of ecstacy.
So even I, with voice made sweet and loud,
And soft and clear,
Should drive with tones away earth’s grey care-
cloud,
And all should hear,
And hearing say : The birth of joy from Heaven
Is this, indeed—
This sound that spreads the sky-stretch ! As at
even
The sun’s death-deed
Doth color all the aether with Love’s roses
Of red, and gold
Of heart’s desiring, so this song reposes
In manifold
Delight on the horizon of the soul,
And speeds its fear,
Which was the grey. Ah, thus should hearts
be whole
Through you, Heart’s Dear !
Rosamond Ben ham.
THE “ETERNAL FEMININE.”
The sweet “ eternal feminine,”
Immutable in charming changes,
It sends queer wiggles down one’s spine—
Through Time and Space its magic ranges.
It’s swayed the world since earliest morn
Of Time, when Sex sprang into being ;
The male with sweetest yearnings torn,
The female, e’er enticing, fleeing.
Bear witness all our histories !
The blackest and the brightest pages
Are blazoned with its victories—
Its triumph through the rolling ages.
It breathes an incense-haunted air
That round its least belonging lingers ;
It lurks in gold or raven hair,
Thrills in the touch of slender fingers.
It conquered me a boy at school,
’Twas hid in someone’s shapely stocking;
Still, grown a man, I own its rule,
Its fetters there is no unlocking.
Ah me ! the flutter of a dress,
Or petticoat with lace upon it,
A bosom’s curve, a drooping tress,
A dainty face beneath a bonnet,
A little passing dubious glance—
And I, although I know I’ll rue it,
For some fair siren’s pleasure dance—
Each is a challenge to pursue it.
A challenge ever new and sweet—
I’d not resist it were I able ;
Nor would I lose its raptures fleet
To be the staidest sage of fable.
And sages, if all tales be true,
Not always might resist its power ;
They mostly met their Waterloo
In some more mortal hour.
Its charm, half earthly, half divine,
Is part of me I cannot sever ;
As temp’rance speakers say of wine,
“ Once taste it, you’re its slave for ever.”
So it will be until I die,
Nor death can change its power even ;
Unless our priests and poets lie
There are sweet Seraphim in Heaven !
ZADIG.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 20, 1897.
The 46 Iscariots.
The Bulletin has waited anxiously for
several weeks in the hope that some political
party in New South Wales would raise a
loud public protest against the treachery of
a majority of the Assembly to the cause of
Federation. So far, it regrets to see, no
protest worth calling such has eventuated.
Yet there never was an occasion in the his-
tory of the province when an outbreak of
devastating wrath was more called for. When
the Assembly, by a large majority, accepted
the Bill providing that 80,000 affirmative
votes should be required before New South
Wales could join the Federation, instead of
60,000 as previously agreed upon, it com-
mitted a disgraceful breach of faith, which
had not behind it one excuse that can bear
public inspection. It went back on its
agreement with the other provinces ; all the
provinces which prescribed any minimum
vote at all fixed it, as nearly as possible, on a
common basis, and required that the Federal
Constitution should be accepted by the
suffrages of, as nearly as possible, the same
proportion of the population. There might
be no written or spoken agreement, but the
uniformity of the arrangement showed that
there was, at all events, a tacit one, and
New South Wales deliberately proposes to
break it in pieces. The Assembly had no
mandate from the people for its action ; there
wasn’t even a semblance of reference to
the electors, who never once heard about
the matter until it was brought before Par-
liament. There was no change of public
circumstances to account for this sudden
reversal of the arrangement under which the
negotiations have hitherto proceeded. Except
that the Federal election had been held in
the interim, and sundry politicians who
hoped to sit on the Convention had been
wiped out by ridiculously large majorities,
and sundry oracles of parochial Bumbledom
had discovered, when the vote wai counted,
that their chance of a seat in an Australian
National Legislature was nil, and sundry
people who did get in did -so with much
greater difficulty than they expected, the
conditions are exactly the same as they
were a few months ago when the Assembly
decided that 50,000 affirmative votes would
be good and sufficient reason why New South
Wales should become a party to the Com-
monwealth. And absolutely no cause was
assigned for this amazing change of front.
* * » •
Still The Bulletin opines that there was
a cause. Some nine or ten months ago the
cornstalk Assembly, by a very large majority,
accepted the 50,000 minimum, recognising
that there was a fair chance to get 50,000
votes even on an abstract question like
Federation. Therefore, it is fair to assume
that it wanted Federation then. To-day
there is a large majority in favor of the
80,000 minimum which, judging by the ex-
perience of all past general elections, is an
impossible number, therefore it appears that
the Assembly doesn’t want Federation now.
So far as the drafting of the Constitution has
gone, New South Wales has got everything
it seriously demanded except the possession
of the Federal Capital, and that matter is
still open ; consequently the assumption is,
not that there is any change in the Federal
situation, but that there is a serious change
in the personal interests of the local politi-
cian. And in the first place it must be
remembered that Federation is a matter on
which the interests of the nation and those
of the common barn-door politician are
directly hostile. There will be room in the
Federal Legislature for about 36 New South
Wales representatives, so even if they are all
chosen from the two Provincial Houses,
about 160 members must be left behind in
obscurity. They will sink into mere glori-
fied aldermen with diminished power, very
little dignity, and probably with less salary
and perquisites than they now enjoy.
Therefore, while the people want Federation
for the sake of good government and pros-
perity, and for the making of an Australian
nation, the great majority of the politicians
—the 160 who will be left—have an immense
number of solid reasons for not wanting it.
* * * *
Until the Federal election came off no one
had any idea who would be left. There
never was before an election with the whole
province as a single constituency, and every
politician who was solid in his own constitu-
ency of Smith’s Curse probably reckoned
himself among the possibilities. The Federal
poll dissipated a great many beautiful
dreams of this kind. The fact that an
ex-politician headed the poll by a vast
majority, and that two men who were also
entirely out of politics (Messrs. Wise and
Walker) were among the ten, showed that,
while the very scanty supply of loaves and
fishes to be divided was not nearly enough to
go round, there was a serious danger that
even that limited provender would have to
be shared with outsiders. Quite a number
of robust little politicians were then enabled,
for the first time, to size themselves up as
they appeared, not in the eyes of one little
township, but in those of the nation at large,
and did it with disastrous results. And, as
a natural result, a good many humble but
vociferous road-and-bridge members and de-
partment trotters, who formerly thought they
had powerful reasons for helping Federation
along, discovered instead that they had very
good cause to keep it back to the best of
their ability.
* * * *
Further, there were five Premiers at the
Convention, and only one of them at the
most can be Premier of United Australia.
There were about 30 Ministers and ex-
Ministers, and probably 24 of them
must either stay behind among the com-
mon, unconsidered herd who are left,
or else get into the Federal Legislature
as mere private members. And the Conven-
tion proceedings doubtless gave some of
them a painfully unexpected indication that
they would be among the melancholy residue.
It is an uncharitable conclusion, but in the
utter absence of any other visible motive
the logical assumption is that the new depar-
ture, which promises to make Federation
utterly impossible in New South Wales, is
simply the revolt of the men who see that
they are going to be left. And the probable
reason why they didn’t revolt nine months
ago is that their bladder heads had not been
pricked they didn’t know till after the
election and the Convention that they were
going to be left.
* * * *
New South Wales is not quite the only
province which threatens to go back on
the national cause. South Australia is
dabbling with an amending scheme by which
the local Parliament is to have power to
alter the Federal Constitution before sub-
mitting it to the people, or to refuse to sub-
mit it to the people at all. If it carries out
this idea, and if it does alter the Consti-
tution, it will thereby make union impos-
sible ; there can be no Federation if
four provinces accept one Constitution while
the fifth accepts another and an entirely
different one. The Queensland Government
has introduced the Federal Enabling Bill
with the same proviso—but then no one
takes Queensland seriously in the matter.
And in any case New South Wales's breach
of faith is by far the worst. The other pro-
vinces are not essential to the Federation,
whereas it is expressly provided in the
Enabling Act that New South Wales is
essential ; therefore, their defection need not
break up the union completely, while the
defection of New South Wales must do so.
Moreover, the one proposal only gives the
Legislature power to block Federation with-
out committing it to any such course, and
it is quite possible that no bad results may
ensue. The other proposal —that accepted by
New South Wales Assembly—it is almost a
matter of certainty does block Federation
absolutely. Few persons seriously imagine
that it is possible to get 80,000 votes—
almost one-third of the entire number on
the rolls—on any abstract matter which
doesn’t affect the fiscal issue, or rouse up the
Orange, or Catholic, or Presbyterian, or
other religious vote ; which doesn’t wake up
the drink interest or the teetotal fanaticism,
which has no personal element in it at all,
and which is in no way related to the local
road, or bridge, or railway, or culvert, or to
the Labor question, or to anything else that
the community is accustomed to take an in-
terest in.
* * * *
The Bulletin trusts that the Upper
House will, for once, justify its existence by
throwing the Federal Amendment Act to the
winds. In all ordinary cases, this paper
holds that the Upper House should not in-
terfere, and that, for good or evil, the
people’s representatives should have their
way. But in this case the circumstances are
exceptional. The Assembly does not even
profess to have any popular mandate behind
it; it couldn’t possibly have one, as this im-
portant matter was not even dreamt of at
the time of the ’95 election. And, as the
matter is one on which the interests of the
nation are directly at variance with those of
the Assembly, the action of the latter body
is to be viewed with especial suspicion. The
Council would at least not be exceeding its
rights if it requested Premier Reid to appeal
to the people on the question and get a direct
mandate—or as direct a one as it is possible
to get in the chaos of a general election —on
the subject, and promised to pass the
measure with all possible speed as soon as
that mandate, or anything even colorably
resembling it, was forthcoming. If the
Council can’t do this much on so important
an occasion, it is difficult to imagine what
that body exists for.
On Behalf of the Widow.
Despite all the assurances of an optimistic
Premier, the fact sticks out conspicuously in
the new taxation proposals that the N.S. W.
Treasury is in desperate straits for money.
Premier Reid having solemnly pledged himself
not to tax the foreign importer, and to remove
the burden of import duties from the shoulders
of the people named Hoch and Bock and
Schmidt and Himmeliiochenstaufen, who
live by bringing in the thing that is made in
Germany, there is no resource but to pile up
new taxes on the local Cornstalk whose name
isn’t Schmidt, and w’ho consequently doesn’t
matter. Some of the new duties are unobjec-
tionable enough ; some are small and petti-
fogging and ridiculous ; some others are a sinful
tax on a very poor and distressed section of the
community. The stamp duties are mainly
remarkable for their beautiful comprehensive-
ness. Under the new regime there will be a
stamp duty chargeable on almost everything—
except the Sydney newspaper, which is still to be
carried through the post at the public expense,
and humped to the remotest part of the
country gratis by train and steamboat and
coach ; and as the Sydney paper would probably
turn and rend the Reid Ministry asunder if it
wasn’t carried gratis, the reason why its
wealthy proprietary will still be pauperised
out of the public pocket is, possibly, not very
far to seek. But, apart from the protected and
artificially coddled newspaper proprietor whose
industry will be subsidised as heretofore, there
will be a great change all round.
* * * *
Under the new order of things everybody
who swears by means of the familiar affidavit
to the truth of anything must put on a shilling
stamp. If he makes a declaration instead of an
affidavit he must also put on a shilling stamp.
If anybody in distressed circumstances wants
to sell or mortgage his four-roomed cottage
and goes through the necessary form of getting
it valued, and the valuation is £55, he must
pay for a ss. stamp. If the humpy is only
valued at £2l he must pay for a half-crown
stamp ; if it is worth £lO5 the law falls on him
for 10s. At the same time a valuation of
£lOO,OOO apparently costs only 205., so the poor
man falls in heavily as usual. If you get your
furniture or jewellery valued for any purpose
the stamp duty comes in. Again, there is
a stamp duty on repairs ; if the harmless,
necessary builder writes that he reckons it will
cost Bs. 3d. to repair the leak in the roof, that
involves Is. stamp duty ; and if he estimates
that the lean-to can’t be made really habitable
under £2l, the duty will be 2s. 6d. When
seven sanguine men enrol themselves under
the Companies Act to work some over-rated
quartz outcrop which ultimately comes
to nothing, the stamp duty will be £5.
A letter stating that 10 shares have
been allotted to you in the Lost Soul Gold-
mining Co. is to carry a threepenny stamp.
Every scrip, whether it represents 100 shares
in the Union Bank, value £2500, or 10 shares
in the Hungry Ghost mine, worth (at present)
one penny each, is to carry a threepenny stamp
also. If anybody’s house and furniture are
insured for £5OO, he is to pay Is. Bd. stamp-
duty on each premium ; and, if lie insures his
life for £l2OO, so that his widow may have 18s.
a week certain when he dies, the stamp-duty is
to be £2. If he insures for £6O, so as to pay
for his fuueral, the stamp-duty is Is. If lie
signs a proxy, so that someone can get up on
his behalf at the meeting of the society he is
insured in, and ask what the directors have done
with the money, the stamp-duty is 3d. If you
supply goods or labor, and take a P.N. (which
has to be stamped) because you can’t get cash,
and the P.N. is dishonored, and you lose both
the money and the goods, it will be necessary
to pay Is. for a stamp before you can take pre-
liminary proceedings against the defaulter.
These are a few of the things which will require
stamps of various amounts put on them when
the new order of things gets fairly started in
New South W ales, and the things with stamps
on them not above enumerated are legion.
* * * •
Most of these things, however, are only comic ;
it is in the new probate duties that the pathetic
element comes in. Under the proposed system
the rates of probate-duty are practically trebled,
or perhaps quadrupled; the 5 per cent, duty
begins when the estate of the late lamented is
worth £20,000, whereas it used to begin at
£50,000 ; and whereas 5 per cent, was formerly
the maximum tax, the highest rate levied now
is 10 per cent. In addition to this, whereas
estates under £2OO used to go free, there is now
no exemption whatever. With his usual in-
capacity for sticking to the unvarnished facts
of the case, Premier Reid recommended his
new tariff on the ground that it was identical
with the one that “ has been adopted for many
years in Victoria ” ; which is absolutely incor-
rect, as the merciful Victorian law exempts all
estates worth less than £lOOO, and lets off the
widow, children, and grand-children at half-
rates where the property to be divided is worth
less than £50,000. He also alleged that it was
identical with the Queensland scale, which was
equally inaccurate, as Queensland also lets the
widow and children off at half-rates. Likewise
he stated that his policy was the one adopted
in South Australia, which it isn’t, as South
Australia gives exemption up to £5OO in the
case of widows or children, and up to £2OOO it
lets them off at half-rates. Why Premier
Reid should have gone out of his way to make
these statements it is difficult to imagine.
Possibly it was mere ignorance, or possibly it
was owing to that ingrained habit of inaccuracy
which makes it safe to bet at all times that his
figures are wrong and that his facts won’t stand
inspection.
* * * *
In The Bulletin’s opinion the proposal to
abolish all exemptions in the case of probate
duties is a monstrous inhumanity. This world
is a cold place for the widow at all times. And
Australia is a colder place for her now than it
ever was before. Even the sober, industrious
and economical artisan can seldom leave over
£2OO worth of property behind him when he
dies, in the present state of the labor-market.
And £2OO worth of property only means a
wooden cottage of four rooms and a kitchen—a
cottage standing on six wooden stumps, with a
shaky fence in front and a creaking gate and
an-eighth of an acre of suburban desola-
tion for a back-yard ; also, some plain furni-
ture, the old man’s clothes and his
silver watch, and the remnant of his last
week’s wages. His widow, as a rule, has three
moderate-sized children; the artisan, whose
sole, or almost sole, amusement is getting mar-
ried, seldom has less than three. She has lost
her only source of income, and has to pay
funeral expenses and medical charges as well,
and to go out and hustle in a world where
women’s work is scarce and horribly ill-paid to
boot. And Premier Reid— the man who
climbed into office on the assurance that he
was going to shift the burden off the poor and
deposit it with a thump on the broad back of
the rich—proposes to levy an extra tax of £2
on the bereaved woman and the orphaned
family. Even the direst necessity for taking
off Dibbs’ duties on sporting ammunition,
playing-cards, plate, carriage-cloth, engravings,
scented soap, fireworks, Brussels carpet, furs,
opera-glasses, gilt-mouldings, top-hats, pianos,
organs, jewellery, lace, oil - paintings, per-
fumery, silks and the like, can’t justify this
proceeding ; and the urgent need to subsidise
the Sydney press by carrying its wares free at
the public expense, so as to keep it from turn-
ing upon and rending the Government, is no
sufficient excuse for such a mode of money-
raising.
♦ * * *
In The Bulletin’s opinion the Victorian
exemption of £lOOO is none too large. Where
the estate of the deceased only pans out for
£lOOO, and it is the sole support of his widow,
and possibly his young family as well, that
sum, however liberal it may look by comparison
with the means of most widows, only amounts
to a provision of 15s. per week at the utmost as
interest goes nowadays, and for the first year it
is proposed to take one-fourth of this as taxa-
tion ! If Premier Reid regards a lone woman
with 15s. a week as a fit subject for special
taxation, then his ideas about how to take the
burdens off the poor and pile them on the rich
are built after a curious fashion. It is that
politician’s proudest boast that he has done
more towards making Sydney a free port than
was ever done before, and apparently he has
made it so free that no one can do anything
while he is alive without putting a si amp on it,
and that no one can die outside the Benevolent
Asylum without a heavy duty being levied on
his surviving relatives. And even the blessed
privilege of knowing that the foreign-made
goods of Himmelhoch and Schmidt and
Schroder now' come in free and unhindered
may cost, at this rate, a great deal more than
it is worth*
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Majority of Six.
The Maoriland Government survived the latest
motion of censure by a majority of only six.
Many Governments have lived all their lives on
a credit balance of no more than six, but com-
pared with the majorities which the Seddon
Ministry used to have, the figures look like the
beginningof disaster. Certainly there was alarge
desertion among its followers, and now that the
big Government following has once shown signs
of disintegration it will probably never again be
quite the solid affair it was. The motion of
censure which so nearly upset the Liberal Cabi-
net was directed against Seddon personally, for
his action in holding a paid billet (salary alleged
to be ,£IOOO a-year) as Maoriland representative
of a British mining syndicate. It was claimed
that when Seddon, as representative of the
syndicate, appealed to Seddon as head of the
Government for some concession or other,


some very stinkful things might happen—
though it was not alleged that anything
of a stinkful character had happened up to date.
The censure motion was not moved by Captain
Russell, the Opposition leader, probably
because Russell is mixed up with a big land
and mortgage co., and to ask the House to
throw out Premier Seddon because he belonged
to a gold-mining co., and put in Premier Rus-
sell, who belongs to a big land-grab co., instead,
would have looked a trifle ridiculous. But
though one of Russell’s understrappers acted in
his stead, the result of the motion, had it been
successful, would almost certainly have been to
put Russell (and his land-syndicate) in office.
Anyhow, the narrow majority is the first
definite sign that the country is beginning to
grow weary of the old Ballance Seddon Govt.,
as it grows weary of everything in course of
time. The good works that this Govt, did
are many. It saved the Bank of N.Z., and
thereby averted an appalling panic. It created
a State Land Bank and gave cheap money to a
community which was formerly ground
down by usury. It compelled factory and
workshop owners to give their employes
light and air and a reasonable amount
of comfort. It shortened the hours of labor ;
minimised the sweating evil ; widened the
franchise ; and burst up many big estates, and
supplied farms for a multitude of small settlers.
It shifted a lot of taxation off the poor and on
to the very rich. It was the first Government
that ventured to attack the curse of usury,
to seriously attempt an Old-Age Pension system
—now nearing completion—and to deal with
the Midland Railway incubus. And, at the
worst, its finance' was a hundred times more
honest than that of any other Maoriland
Government for 25 years back, and its record
of Democratic legislation is vastly better than
that of any other Government that ever existed
in the Seven Provinces. Above all things, it
has succeeded. The industries of Maoriland, as
shown by the number of persons employed in
manufactures, have gone ahead far faster dur-
ing the last four years of Socialistic legislation
than those of any other province. Maoriland
is the only province, barring Westralia, which
has no accumulated deficit. It has a remark-
able increase of population and a tolerably
regular surplus, and its great dilapidated bank
is now safely out of the mire. But very little
is heard of these things now. Maoriland poli-
tics mostly centre at present round Joseph
Ward’s insolvency, and Seddon’s syndicate
billet, and the exorbitant bill which he sent in
for his Jubilee expenses, and the fact that
Minister M'Kenzie’s sons bought one of the
B.N.Z. estates, and other personalities. More-
over, the Seddon Government is slowly failing
through old age; the country gets weary of every-
thing and everybody in time, and nothing on
earth could prevent the mana of that long-en-
during Ministry from decay. It has done such
great things that it has to keep on doing greater
things all the time to maintain its reputation,
and if it can’t do them it will probably be thrown
out before very long to give place to the old
Tory crowd, which never did any great things
whatsoever. Probably Richard Seddon is
wise in his generation in hanging on to his
rather shady syndicate billet. He has toiled
incessantly as a Minister for seven years, and
run the country for nearly four years, at less
than half the salary given to the man who only
runs a bank. Also he has occupied a position
which leaves very little chance of saving money,
and his billet is liable to desert him at any
moment, and he is an old man with no profes-
sion to fall back upon. So he hangs on to his
little syndicate job as something to fall back
upon when the country has ejected its idol
and Richard of Kumara is wanted no longer.
Sensible man !
A Huge Blunder.
Never was the want of a strong Democratic
party, with a strong leader, more conspicuous
in N.S.W. politics than in the Lucknow strike
debate. The Government promised to do its
duty by opposing the Wentworth co.’s demand
for exemption. All the troubles—including the
dilapidated state of the mine, which necessarily
followed on the partial cessation of work—arose
through the co.’s attempt to cut down wages to
a miserable figure, and its insistence on a
degrading and unlawful authority to search the
houses of its employes, and under these circum-
stances the Government had every right, as the
co.’s landlord, to appear by counsel and argue
that its tenant had no claim for special con-
sideration. It lacked the courage to carry out
its purpose, still the purpose was good.
And the Opposition, instead of censuring
it for not carrying out its good intention,
actually tried to censure it for having any
good intention at all. The Labor-party must
have voted against the Government if the com-
plaint had been that it didn’t carry out its
commendable purpose; when the plaint was
that the Government, in addition to acting
badly, should have meant badly as well, every
democratic vote had to go with the Reid
Ministry. A tactician like Parkes would have
blown the Reid crowd to the winds with such
a case, and an honest democrat who had no
tactics at all would have done likewise.
Premier Reid is a chronic blunderer of a very
bad kind, a shuffling mediocrity, a man of half-
measures, and unfinished purposes, and broken
promises, and evasions, and failures, and in-
tentions that go askew, and muddle and mis-
representation. But he is the one-eyed man in
the kingdom of the blind, and his one eye is
bland and glassy, and always fixed on the main
c hance, so he prospers.

The Dead Baby-Farmer and the Live
Abortionist.
Writes a man who knows what he is talking
about: —
Dear Bulletin,— With reference to Mr. Sydney Maxted’s
contention, in Sydney Daily Telegraph of Nov. 2, that
local child-protective legislation has produced excellent
results in the diminution of infanticide, let me point out
that this evil cannot—any more than any other anti-social
practice—be eliminated merely by repressive legislation.
Statistics doubtless do show a marked diminution in
recorded cases since the passing of the Children’s Protec-
tion Act of 1892. But this may merely prove that more care
is taken to prevent detection, and that the further-
reaching evil of abortion is more prevalent now than for-
merly. Before the Act became law it was the baby-farmer
who flourished ; now ifc is the abortionist who waxes fat,
and the hospitals are crowded with the victims of mal-
practice. The hospital authorities are forced by circum-
stances to become accessories after the act, because both of
the urgency of the cases and the impossibility of gaining-a
conviction in 999 cases out of a thousand, they are forced
to grin and bear it. Humanity affords the grim example
of an inherited appetite developed out of all proportion
to the necessities of race-propagation, and accompanied
by ever-increasing difficulties in the way of maintaining
the “ inevitable consequences. ” Results: bagnios, pro-
miscuous immorality, illegitimacy, infanticide. Repress
post-natal infanticide—and the vendor of pills for female
“obstructions” and the manipulator of the deathful
darning-needle reap a golden harvest. In this respect, at
all events, Kipling may fairly be reversed. “We may
lighten the curse of Evk when we’ve lightened the curse
of Adam ! F.
The Dishonored P.N’s.
The present N.S. W. Parliament is now almost
ended, barring a very brief session, which is
promised about April, to deal with Federal
matters, and most of Premier Reid’s promissory
notes have come back marked N.S.F. Of the
democratic measures which were promised, or
half-promised, at the beginning of the Reid
Government, almost everyone has dropped into
peaceful oblivion. The State Bank is never
heard of now ; neither is the State Land Bank,
or the State monopoly of paper-money. The
Land for Closer Settlement law, which was to
burst up the big estates and settle the fertile
eastern districts with a multitude of small land-
holders, has perished by the wayside. The
Local Government Bill fell off the vehicle some-
where, and broke its unobtrusive neck. The
Reform of the Upper House never even-
tuated. There is no absentee - tax after
all. The Land-tax, which was once expected to
be a democratic measure, proved to be a mere
revenue-raising device which strikes the strug-
gling agriculturist just about as heavily as it
does the speculator and monopolist. The
Income-tax, instead of being graduated to lay
the chief burden on the man of wealth and on
the idle man who lives upon his accumulations,
is another mere revenue device which grinds the
large and the small man very much alike. The
Reid Government will go to the country with
hardly one democratic action to its credit. A
strongly democratic Opposition, which declared
itself willing to do all the things the Ministry
has left undone, and which had a good enough
record to convince the country that it would do
them, could sweep the polls at the 1898 election
and send Reid and Sunday Brunker into
obscurity ; but, unluckily, as Reid sinks into
somnolent Toryism Lyne tries to go one
better by being more Tory than Reid, and
between the two there seems to be no hope
anywhere. And yet, when one considers how
the Reid combination has sold the democracy,
and how shamefully it has gone back on Federa-
tion, and views the ever-increasing pile of its
dishonored political P.N’s, it seems incredible
that some big, brainy democrat can’t be found
to rise to the occasion and create a new party.
The spectacle of the Freetrade Tory party and
the Protectionist Tory party clawing for office,
and the Labor-party—the one avowedly demo-
cratic organisation in the Assembly—drifting
along to no good purpose, is far from edifying.
The Far-West Aboriginal.
The Westralian Government is making another
vigorous effort to get at the local aboriginal.
When W.A. got a Constitution in 1889, it was
provided that the dispossessed western nigger
should be placed under the care of an indepen-
dent board, and that 1 per cent, of the revenue
should be set aside as a fund for the education
of the rising aboriginal, and for the support of
the old toothless autochthon as he goes down-
hill to the grave. Last year this fund ran to
nearly .£30,000 —probably about £1 per annum
for each nigger, to cover education, medical
attendance, pauper relief, old-age pensions, &c.,
for the entire race, and the salaries of the men
who looked after them ; and it was felt that
such a sum was wholly extravagant. The in-
dependent board, which was to watch over the
aborigine and be a father to him, was duly
appointed, but it had no machinery for doing
its work, and was practically a dead-letter. The
administration was really done by a Parlia-
mentary official outside the board, and by this
means the money was kept under the control
of the little Forrest ring. Also, there is no
doubt that the aborigine was looked after. In
no Australian province was he ever flogged so
vigorously or conscientiously or regularly. He
was captured and run in with manacles on him,
and apprenticed at practically no wages, and
lots of hard work, to the Government’s friends,
and they spared no expense in the matter of
flogging to keep him in the way he should go.
It was no uncommon thing to see the police
bringing 16 of him chained together in one lot
to be civilised and enlightened at the cost of
the fund, and the W.A. Government did its
duty by him all it knew. All the same, it re-
gards the £30,000 a year as unjustifiable extra-
vagance, so a bill is now before Parliament to
abolish the Board and reduce the vote for
whaling the original possessor of the soil to
£SOOO a year. For all the good that the native
ever got out of the existing arrangement the
grant might as well be reduced to £SOOO, or
even a great deal less, and the British Govt.—
which thought it was doing a great and humane
act when it insisted on the 1 per cent, system
and the abortive Board—has been wheedled into
upsetting the aboriginal’s apple-cart. Aprevious
negotiation revealed the fact that the British
authorities were inclined to stick to the original
conditions, and the only time when the loyal
Westralian Government and Parliament got
right out of its usual grovelling habit of mind,
and denounced John Bull, almost in so many
words, as an interfering old fool, was
when that preliminary attempt at spoiling
the native was coldly received. This time
it is cabled that the scheme will probably get
through. As £SOOO a year will fully cover
all the care and education that the native ever
got or is ever likely to get, the arrangement
seems quite unobjectionable. In fact, the
native will probably be rather better off under
the new system than the old one. The Govern-
ment, with a large, fund to expend on him, had
to make a pretence of doing something, so its
various officials harried the miserable heathen
from pillar to post, and made Ids li f e a misery
and his death painfully suddtn With a greatly
reduced vote the black man may possibly get a
little rest.
A Roebourne (W.A.) jury has just returned
the customary verdict that the nigger died from
natural ‘causes. It was the usual story. The
indentured heathen (indentured means being
bound for a long term of years to work almost
any number of hours per day, for any kind of
food and lodging, and under any conditions, at
no wage worth mentioning) ran away. He was
brought back and walloped with a stick—this
fact was admitted by all the witnesses—by his
humane boss, and he promptly died. Two other
natives belonging to the same station, and also
liable to be walloped, naturally testified that
the deceased didn’t seem to be absolutely
pounded to death ; if The Bulletin was an
aboriginal on a Westralian station it would
probably be equally careful about its evidence.
A medical man testified that the remains, when
he saw them, were so much decomposed that the
cause of death was unascertaiivable —the de-
ceased was buried unobtrusively on the station,
and it was not till two months later, when he
was in a safe state of decay, that the law dug
him up and inspected him. Then the jury
acquitted the accused right off, the general
feeling being that the thrashing was rather
calculated to prolong life than otherwise, and
that the lamented nigger died chiefly from
insufficient walloping, combined with the wrath
of God at his conduct in bolting from a loving
master. The Westralian aboriginal’s habit of
dying from purely natural causes immediately
after being struck in a merely nominal fashion
by his kind master is simply unaccountable.
Stipendiary and other magistrates in inviting
lay persons, as Mr. Smithers did in a Sydney
court, the other day, to take a seat on the
Bench during the hearing of cases, follow, it
must be admitted, an example set by several
Supreme Court Judges. But example and copy
are both bad, and some day an accused will
focus public reprobation on this peculiarly
brassy brand of courtesy to “ distinguished ”
visitors. The Bench which tries an accused
should be composed of Justices or other
accredited ministers of the law—and of these
alone. Monocled globe-trotters or petticoated
relatives of the presiding Judge have not alone
no right to the seats of judgment, but actually
detract from the impersonal character of the
Blind Goddess. If is Justice plus a brother-
in-law, a maiden aunt or a globe-trotter,
which, in such circumstances, looks down on
the prisoner in the dock. A prisoner may
properly resent being made this “ show ”
of ; he may claim that the Bench, like the jury-
box, shall be reserved for the parties directly
associated with the study of his guilt or inno-
cence. It is hoped that some day a prisoner
will have the nerve to demand that companion-
able magistrates and Judges shall dismiss from
the Bench their opera-box attachments.

One of the bitterest accusations brought against
the present Maoriland Government is that
when Treasurer Ward’s hopeless overdraft had
practically wrecked the Colonial Bank, it made
an arrangement by which that institution was
taken over by the State-managed B.N.Z. The
charge is only another proof that any stick is
good enough to beat a dog with—especially if
the dog is not so young as it used to be. The
B.N.Z. bought up the Colonial’s business at a
valuation made by non-political experts, and
the bargain, judged by the increased profits
since shown by the former, was a reasonably
good one. The price received for its business
saved the Colonial from very much worse
disaster ; if it hadn’t sold out but had waited to
go broke in the ordinary way there would have
been no business to sell, and the Colonial would
have lost the money, and there would have
been no profitable connection for the B.N.Z. to
buy, for it would have been scattered to the
winds. Everybody knew or guessed that the
Colonial wanted to sell its business because it
had been struck by somebody’s overdraft. The
sale didn’t save Ward from exposure ; in fact,
whatever the intention might be, the actual re-
sult was that it rather hurried on the exposure
by winding up the Colonial a year or so earlier
than might otherwise have happened. The
Government couldn’t well state in advance that
the Colonial wanted to sell because it was prac-
tically ruined, largely through the overdraft of
Treasurer Ward’s co„ ; while the sale was uncom-
pleted, it could hardly blast other people’s pro-
perty on the strength of information which was
more or less confidential. Apparently the whole
theory is that it shouldn’t have made a pur-
chase which has turned out very well, and
should have let the Colonial make a disastrous
smash, or should have allowed its business to
be absorbed by some foreign institution, simply
because Treasurer Ward owed it a lot of
money. Whether there was any ulterior motive
or not, the transaction was apparently con-
ducted on absolutely honest lines, and the
Colonial Bank squabble is only another evidence
of the small-mindedness and parochial character
of Maoriland politics.

Another movement, supported by Melb. Age,
has been begun to secure the coining in Aus-
tralia of all silver and bronze called for in
Australian currency. Nowadays the right is
worth having ; last year the Mint made a profit
of 117'73 per cent, on the silver coined, said
profit reaching for the year J>476,866. Half-
promises have hitherto been made ; but at that
the matter stands. The grounds taken by the
Imperial bosses are chiefly these : —Silver isn’t
a coin ;it is a token. It cannot be held as a
reserve; hence it would be dangerous to coin
beyond requirements. The Bank of England,
having its finger on the financial pulse of the
kingdom, alone knows when silver tokens are
required, and on its representation alone are
any minted. By the way, the silver minting is
pretty frequently done away from the Mint
altogether. Also by the way, there is but one
British Mint in the world ; all the rest, in
India, Australia, and elsewhere, are merely
branches of it. In Australia, silver-distribu-
tion is arranged by the Colonial Treasurers, and
not by the banks.
‘ Harry Monck,” to The Bulletin :
Although this constant yabber about Kanaka slavery in
Queensland is in some few instances not far from truth, it
is still greatly exaggerated, in the interests of a body of
men who wish for a monopolv of the islands for their own
purposes. Five years as a Govt, agent in the South Seas
convinced me that slavery and depopulation are carried
out in a most systematic manner on the islands, without
the “ boys ’ leaving their native shores. One instance :
Roats went ashore in answer to a signal-smoke. I took
off two boys, explained agreement, and signed them on
as recruits. Following morning comes a letter from mis-
sionary, demanding boys back, as I had taken them with-
out his, their parents’, or their own permission. As this
amounted to a direct charge of kidnapping, I replied that
I would retain boys, and lay his letter before Govt, on hiy
return. On investigation in Queensland, the boy's said
they had worked for missionary long periods—one over a
year and only received a few sticks of tobacco for ser-
vices. These boys thoroughly understood their Queens-
land agreement, and were only anxious to get out of mis-
sionary clutches, and gain some recompense for their work.
Again, if this slavery exists in Q., how account for the con-
stant return thither of old hands from the islands ? I have
had as many as 30 old hands amongst 70 recruits. Have
also had in one trip seven boys return at once after being
up to their villages seeing their relatives and giving away
their boxes. There is, no doubt, a high Kanaka death-
rate in Queensland, but if a census could be taken and a
death-rate struck on the missionary islands, the figures
would surprise church-goers. Two reasons for "this.
First, the missionary' has not sufficient confidence in the
God he preaches to believe that all things are for the
best, but at once proceeds to put his alleged converts
into clothes—men, shirt and trousers ; women, a sort of
dressing-gown. Latter arrangement probably from doubt
of his own innate purity in so hot a climate. The effects,
however, are disastrous to his disciples, as they never
remove the togs till they fall off (take ’em as part of the
Gospel), turning in after tropical rains and fishing on the
reefs, wringing wet, to sleep in the hot ashes strewn over
the floors of the village houses. Pneumonia, rheumatism,
and consumption are natural results. Second cause
of depopulation per missionary is that islands like
Aoba, Malo and Santo in the New Hebrides are simply
immense brothels, frequented by trading vessels and
other craft with usual results of disease and death. The
labor vessels’ crews are not offenders to any extent in
this way. The heavy bond entered into by ship-owners
against illegal traffic with natives, the sharp look-out of
the Govt, agent, and the knowledge by the crew that dis-
covery means barring-out from the trade, act as sufficient
deterrents in the majority of cases. Those islands which
are not yet missionary-ridden, and where a white man
interfering with a native woman is liable to be shot, or
tomahawked quick and lively, are not, I venture to say,
losing much population, though there may be recruiting
for Queensland going on. I think I have advanced enough
to prove that Queensland is not responsible for all the
slavery and depopulation of the South Seas. That the
boys are well looked-after in Queensland the recent en-
deavor to shift the Polynesian Inspector in Bundaberg
will prove. Fact is, there has been too much talk by
missionaries and their supporters about slavery' in
Queensland and consequent depopulation of islands, and
too little talk (of the right kind) about a body of men
whose hypocritical prudery and insane methods are
jeopardising the lives of hundreds annually in the name
of Christianity. If the pagans must be christianized, let
them be inoculated in the nude, and, if this is more
than missionary virtue can stand—well, why mission-
aries at all?

Apropos of The Bulletin’s recent statement
that the constantly bragged-about “influx”
into N.S.W. from the adjacent Protectionist
provinces is all a myth, an infuriated cor-
respondent writes to state that this paper is a
liar, and that something awful will happen to
it when it dies. By way of reply to this unduly
excited person, it tenders the following figures :
The N.S. W. statistics give no details of arrivals
or departures by sea from or to the other pro-
vinces, these being lumped in with the influx
from Europe, Asia, Africa, and America; but
it seems a fair thing to assume that the arrivals
and departures by sea will be in about the same
proportion as those overland. As regards the
overland movement, the three adjacent pro-
vinces—Victoria, S.A., and Queensland —are
reckoned in together, so it is necessary to take
them all in one lot, with the following result,
so far back as the information goes :
Arrivals Departures
Overland. Overland.
1891 ... 50,775 49,685 Gain 1090
1892 ... 62,050 62,734 Loss 684
1893 ... 45,175 49,207 „ 4032
1894 ... 42,809 45,442 „ 2633
1895 ... 52,795 57,125 „ 4330
1896 ... 68,860 57,690 Gain 1170
312,464 321,883 Loss 9419
These are the Government Statistician’s figures,
and the Registrar-General agrees with him
except as regards 1896, in which year the latter
authority declares N.S.W. lost 1801 people in-
stead of gaining 1170. But, even on the most
favorable computation, the figures are very routrli
upon the old, dreary, pig headed lie about
the “ influx ” of people from the adjacent pro-
vinces to escape from the horrors of Protection
and enjoy the blessed sunshine of Fiee-raid.
The Shoddy press of Sydney has apparently
entered into a tacit conspiracy to bolster up the
Foreign-trade system by large, copious, off-
hand statements about the wondrous prosperity
of the province. Among its pet fictions are the
Treasury surplus which the State Statistician
persistently shows as a chronic deficit ; the pro-
fitable condition of the N.S.W. railways, des-
pite the fact that the Government Auditor
shows a huge deficit against them—the largest
save one in the Seven Provinces, and one which
runs even the largest one very close ; the in-
creased revenue, which is steadily declining ;
the influx of population from the adjacent pro-
vinces, which pans out in cold figures as above ;
the steady growth of the capital, which has
13,600 less inhabitants than it had two years
ago, and a good many other things. The Free-
trade press of N.S.W. is a great deal too much
like the Chinaman, who has spent 3000 years in
considering how much greater and wiser and
more enlightened and civilised he is than any-
body else in the whole world, and who has been
left behind while he was wrapped in considera-
tion.

The battle of ’9B still rages with un-
diminished force in Sydney, and the peculiarity of
it is that here, as in the “ ould dart” itself, we
have the local M'Carthyites, the local Healyites,
and the local church-party at loggerheads. The
church-party want the Irish Rebellion of 1708 to
be “ commemorated ” in the orthodox manner, the
proceeds to go towards erecting an altar in St.
Mary’s ; the M'Carthyites want Irishmen, mostly
Catholics, to “commemorate” in a moderately
sympathetic manner the efforts of misguided
patriots, and devote the proceeds of the com-
memoration, also the proceeds of St. Pat. ’s Day,
towards a Celtic Cross at Waverley ; the Healyites
maintain that it is necessary to justify '9B as a
constitutionally-proper movement, so I hat citizens
of all nationalities, classes and creeds u ho are free
to admire true patriotism, no matter by whom
shown, should be invited to “celebrate” the event

the vital points of the expensive exhibition phono-
graph combined with a low price. This new
“ talking machine,” costing only =612, will soon be
found in every house in the country. The manager
of the Edison Electric Parlor (162 Pitt-st., Sydney)
reports a strong demand.*

At Sydney Royal George Rignold—who
came back rather unexpectedly —has been running
“ Tommy Atkins ” with less good fortune than
that vigorous drama deserves. The point about
“Atkins ’’ which appeals to the writer’s soul is
Diver’s wicked captain, with three wives and a
scheme for marrying a fourth. And he likes
Diver especially where he goads his second un-
wedded wife to suicide, and then thoughtfully
retires so that she may drown herself without
being embarrassed by his presence. He feels that
the lady wants to drown herself, and that the river
is handy, and that he is in the way, so he remarks
that it is a nice, wet river, and that the lady will
make a beautiful corpse, and steps aside. No lady
who really wants to drown herself cares to do it
when there is a man looking, for she is liable to
come up ungracefully feet-first several times before
she is quite dead. “ Atkins ”is a realistic drama,
partly because the wicked Diver doesn’t repent
in his last moments, but dies full of gunshot and
blanky; and because the hard-hearted father
never relents at all, but tells his erring daughter
to drag her illicit infant to the ends of the earth,
and his last hope is that she will be
wet and miserable all her life; also be-
cause the curate jumps on his black coat
and enlists; while the good, mild young
man gradually falls into gin and begins to come
home incoherently at a late hour, with a lump of
mud the size of an eagle on the tails of his moral
black coat, and ask passers-by to lend him a
fresh-laid keyhole so that he can get in. Taking
one consideration with another, “ Tommy Atkins”
is a good deal out of the ordinary dramatic rut,
which is a valuable feature in its character. One
of the good points about it is Miss Lilian
Wheeler’s rendering of the bad captain’s second
wife, and one of the worst is George Rignold’s
abandonment of his old part to take up
that of the curate, formerly played by Pliinmer.
Rignold can’t look in the least like a young,
fresh curate, while Plimmer did.

** * *
To-morrow (Friday) night King Hedley’s
wild Amurrikin horse will fall into the tank at the
Lyceum for the last time. His departure will be
regretted by many, for he was an honest, con-
scientious horse, and he went in with a mighty
splash, while King Hedley hung on to the broken
bridge with one hand, and fired at Green Bear, or
Pale Dog, or some other Indian, with the other.
There was a whole lot of hair-raising Indian in the
“ Prairie King,” and the air was full of scalp, and
Maud Williamson, in her male hunting suit, was
a very nice spectacle, and greatly run after by
Pale Cat and Blue Horse and other red men, who
wanted her to add to their harems. The fact that
she was nearly drowned in the tank the first night,
and had to be fished out, pallid and insensible,
with her wet brown hair in her mouth, helped to
advertise the drama considerably. Also, there is
room to say some kind things about Ethel
Grey’s Mexican adventuress, who wore the huge
feathered hat of a sinful life, and was somehow
or other in alliance with Pallid Mule, the Indian, in
his designs on the scalp of the King whose horse
fell into the tank.
** * *
Grattan Riggs, an almost-forgotten hero
of ye olden time, who was much in favor with
a bygone generation—the one that burst for its
many sins and drifted into gaol or the soup-
kitchen in 1893—is due at the Lyceum on
Saturday, when “ The Irish Detective” will be re-
vived. Riggs was a broguey man of might when
Australia used to know him, and as the Irish
drama has had a blessed rest of late years there
ought to be a show for both of them, if they
don’t stay too long.
** * *
Sydney Palace, which has many strange
openings and shuttings, and whose gilded portal
bangs on the heels of one disastrous enterprise
after another in amazingly quick succession, is to
try its luck again on Saturday. It starts with a
new musical comedy, of which Hoyt, of “China-
town ” celebrity, is part author, called “ Binks,
the Photographer,” and the co. includes George
Lauri, Marietta Nash, Addie Conyers, and other
good names. Therefore, if the palatial gate bangs
on the sudden exit of another frost, the chances
are that it will be “ Binks ” and not the company
that is to blame. Probably the ill-luck of the
Palace is only based on the fact that there is far
more, show in Sydney now than a very poor and
hollow city can support. Sydney shows are
divided into three classes at present: those where
business is moderate, those where it is bad, and
those where it is simply awful. One house where
it was worse than awful closed some time ago.
** * *
At Her Majesty’s, Sydney, the haggard
waif with the dreadful coff is nearing his end —
in fact he is booked to cough his very last
to-night (Thursday). Taking one consideration
with another, his lingering lung has lasted longer
than this paper expected. When it first heard
him, a good nwiy weeks ago, it diagnosed him as
a dreadfully bad case, and reckoned he would
bark himself into the cemetery within seven
days, and the fact that he only expects to blow
off the last fragment of his lung to-night shows
great endurance. The Bulletin will regret this
fateful melo-shriek, for the sake of Lily
Titheradge’s fine rendering of the other vago-
bond—not the one with the expiring lung. Miss
Lily makes a nice, healthy, clever boy—a round-
faced, ordinary boy, who looks real; and the
stage boy who is a girl very seldom looks par-
ticularly real. Also it wili regret it for the
sake of the company in general, which is a
remarkably good company, and it even has a
certain sorrow at parting with the boy-girl with
the blighted lung. He looks so spectral, and
there is such a tremendous bark inside such a
very small and hazy boy, that it suggests a lion
running wild in a paper bag, and the writer has
dropped in many a night with a half-formed
expectation that the boy would burst, and the
cough come out and rush about loose on the stage.
But somehow it never happened.

Her Majesty’s will be closed on Friday
while the management gets its superfluous clothes
off and makes ready for a great sprint with “ A
Royal Divorce” on Saturday. Napoleon will be
played as heretofore by Julius Knight, the man
who was falsely accused of having legs that were
not absolutely straight when he was an Early
Pagan in the “Sign of the Gross.” It is antici-
pated that the view of Napoleon’s motley army
trailing horribly homeward through the snows on
the frozen road from Moscow, and dropping its
toes on the road, will be a valuable hint for people
who think of going to Klondyke this winter.
Otherwise the “Royal Divorce’’ is mainly his-
tory gone mad and wild discrepancy in uniform.

The Biograpli co., which Harry Rickards
first presented at Sydney Palace, and then at the
Criterion, and which has latterly been crowded
into the Tivoli along with the regular Tivoli show,
departs this week. Still there will be enough
talent left at the latter house to keep the pro-
gramme going as usual.
* * * *
Elsie Lander, with her “Demon Gold”
company, opens at Brisbane Royal, on the 26th,
with “ Kitty, the Queen of the Mines.” In
America this piece is stated to be drawing crowded
houses—in what part of America The Bulletin
knoweth not, neither has it any idea as to the size
of the house that is alleged to be crowded, and it
knows absolutely nothing about the circumstances
of the case. Therefore the above statement may
be taken either fresh or corned. Anyhow, there
is much thrill and shock in “ Kitty,” and the co.
includes some names that the public seems to have
heard before.
* * * *
The Melb. show market is somewhat de-
pressed since the races came to an end and the
cricket-match went along to Sydney. Public
taste having grown tired and critical, “The Gay
Parisienne ” will run for only one more week, and
not several more weeks, at the Princess’s. Some
of the incidents between the songs fall very
heavily upon the tired population, especially the
mock-trial incident, which is about the saddest
specimen of libretto that ever gained admittance
to the first act of a financial success. If it were
not an essential feature of the plot the manage-
ment would, no doubt, cutout the mock trial, and
fill the gap with ballet, or any other airy irrele-
vancy, in which case “The Gay Parisienne”
might just as well be played backwards on alter-
nate evenings. The performers continue to do
their best for this fragile musical farce, and the
announcement of last nights excites new interest
in the dresses, and scenery, and general fittings of
a payable concern.
* * * *
At Melb. Royal, the bill seems to undergo
a more or less decided change every Saturday.
The present drama is “ Arrah-na-Pogue,” which,
in its old age, depends a good deal upon Maggie
Moore’s broad, effective treatment of “ Wearing
of the Green.” All the other points of Bouei-
cault’s long-lived narrative have lost their early
freshness. The heroic rebel who lurks in the hay-
loft, and the gallant O’Grady, and the crouching
informer, and the finest pisantry in the wurrld
are all of them less new and original than they
used to be. Likewise the bloodthirsty British
major, and the broth of a bhoy who would lay
down his life for the young master, and Patsey,
and Tim, and the faction-fight, are institutions
that begin to pall upon representatives of the
press as time rolls on. But “ The Wearing of the
Green ” has the same old thrill in it when Maggie
Moore turns her soul full on at the meter, and for
the sake of that traysonable ballad-singer The
Bulletin looks kindly at “ Arrah-no-PogUe,” not-
withstanding sundry shortcomings in the tail-end
of the production.
* * * *
At the Opera House somebody, no doubt,
will be coming along to console people for the tem-
porary loss of Wootwell, a joyous, unconventional
humorist, who constructs absurd songs to suit
himself, and is altogether amusing, as a rule. The
vertical pole people depart from Australia this
week, leaving a space for the “ Matchless Luci-
fers.” Silence reigns once more at the Alexandra,
and the Bell Ringers have duly retired from the
Athenseum Hall with a profit, but Cosgrove’s
hopeless co. still struggles along at the Cambridge
for want of something better to do elsewhere.
* * * *
It appears that the Gladstone Wright
Concert Direction, or bureau, or whatever the
Grand Old Man’s distant relative may call him-
self in his business capacity, has secured the Xmas
holiday dates at Melb. Exhibition Building, and
intends to fix up some concerts on a scale of un-
rivalled, &c., &c. The Xmas dates have, of late
years, always been plums for W. J. Turner, who
gave very respectable concerts in a rather rough
style. If the new management wants to shine
it had better start by scrubbing the floor and
putting a little bit of frill on the proceedings.
