from the perennial show at the Opera House.
Several of the names on the bill, including the
Biograph, are quite new to Melbourne, whilst
some of the others, such as Phil Newbury and
Miss Lilian Tree, are dear to memory. Also, the
prices are popular, and seeing that the Bijou is
the best of ‘‘pitches ” for a variety-cwm-concert
eo., the season should hum.
At Poverty Point.
“ Colborne-Baber, who has had a few good chances in
London, pines for the sun of Sydney,” writes a Bulletin
correspondent from London. “ Lardelli seems to be
doing better since he moved to Eastbourne, where he has
the advantage of family connections. Beatrice Griffiths
played for Hugo Gorlitz, at Steinway Hall, to-day (8/10,97),
which means that there is a likelihood of her meeting
Paderewski—now shorn of his locks.”
“Ada Crossley sang in ‘ Elijah,’at the opening of the
great Birmingham Musical Festival, on Tuesday last,”
writes a Bulletin correspondent by last mail, “ the soul-
ful Wagnerian, Madame Marie Brema, singing second to
the Gippslander. Albani, Edward Lloyd, and Andrew
Black were the other principals. Twenty-four critics and
hundreds of musical enthusiasts went down from London
for the occasion, and from the press Ada got stacks of
praise. Hundreds of people were unable to secure seats
for ‘ Elijah,’ no less than 120 applicants being ‘ out’ at the
first ballot for places. Albani, who embraced the Cross-
ley at the Chelsea Barracks Colonial Concert, a couple of
months ago, followed that overture by an offer for the
Canadian’s Australia tour. A.C. wasn’t ‘ taking any,’ and
prefers the prospect of doing Australia ‘on her own.’ She
went to Paris for her Birmingham Festival frocks, and
looked truly chic in them.”
Kowalski is back in London after a successful concert-
tour in France. There is a possibility of his returning to
Australia in two years. He is now engaged on a comic
opera on a Greek subject, with a French libretto.
Flora Graupner, under the gn y skies of London, is not
recognisable as the mercurial little fairy of Austral comic
opera. The Bulletin regrets to hear Flora seems in-
clined to melancholy.
While Melba tours the English provinces her young
sister remains in Paris to learn French. The brother,
Ernest Mitchell, is studying composition in Paris with
Joachim, the phenomenal voice not having come up to
anticipation. Joachim has a niece in London who devotes
herself to German diction, and at her Tuesdays during
the season one meets crowds of Australians.
From London : “Mrs. Potterand Kyrle live in a charm-
ing place, Blenheim House, St. John’s Wood, where they
are ‘at home ’ every Sunday afternoon. Even before the
most intimate friends they address each other as ‘ Mrs.
Potter’ and ‘Mr. Bellew.’ The latter’s father was in-
cumbent of St. Mark’s Church, which stands quite near
to their Abbey Road home. At the present moment Cora
is about equally dividing her interest between the London
critics —who were really nice to her—and the fluctuations
in the Mt. Lyell market. She bought Mount Lyells at
£6.”
Bertha Rossow gets a show at the first of Vert’s three
morning-concerts at St. James’s Hall. If they go, Vert
will inaugurate a series in opposition to Boosey, who trod
on his corns. The rival contraltos, Ada Crossley and
Clara Butt, sing together at the first of the new Boosev
Ballad concerts.
Notes and small change, from an Australian globe-
trotter : “ Tom Haydon, for so many years secretary of the
V.R.C. is writing a book entitled ‘ Sporting Reminis-
cences ’ for Bliss, Sands and Co., who are so satisfied with
the advance sheets they have paid a good price down.
The old-’un is going strong, and is as popular as ever.
Miss Ethel is not at present too well, the monotony and
whirl of two much ‘ Circus Girl ’ easily accounts for that.
The much-aspersed Australian ‘ ham-fat ’ A. G. Paulton
has done well in his first engagement—‘La Perichole’—
being hailed as ‘ a clever young actor,’ though it is to be
hoped his success will not depopulate Poverty Point.
Miss Pattie Browne, in ‘ White Heather,’ at Drury Lane,
does well, and, with all respect to an English provincial
paper, looks well. The ‘ atrocious rag,’ with more
audacity than decency, says, ‘ In the cycling scene Miss
Pattie B. wears a rational suit of white serge, and looks
extremely nice in it so long as she stands up wit h her face
to the audience. But any change of position seems fatal
to grace.’ Which means— — ”
Mascagni, composer of “ Cavalleria Rusticana,” lately
made a more or less genuine attempt to commit suicide.
His one famous opera, created him the hero of the hour ;
then came comparative failures and loss of celebrity, and
the genius who had been happy enough as an unknown
struggler, began to think that life wasn’t worth living.
Business at London Duke of York’s has been improving,
The Bulletin hears. After the first week Musgrove de-
cided to sell the lease, but matters changed for the better
in a day or two, and he refused the offer and pluckily
resolved to hold on. As far as “ Francillon ” goes, it is to
the play, not the players, that the London people object.
Some members of the cast are decidedly antagonistic to
Mrs. Potter, for all that.
Tom Fitzgerald, of circus fame, told a friend of
the writer's last week that his firm had cleared over £BOOO
by their show in the West, during the last five months.
Henry Bracy starts the “ Beggar Student ” at Bendigo,
under the MacMahon management, and is going to run a
season of provincial opera. Bracy is a good actor and a
good singer, and looks gorgeous in his Polish togs, but
owing to something—probably the want of hard business
instinct—his own attempts at management didn’t make
him absolutely a billionaire. In fact rumor has it that it
only required six men to carry the profits home after his
Melbourne season.
The marks of the feet of the critics are deep on the
otherwise beautiful cuticle of Mrs. 8.-Potter. William
Archer said the nastiest things possible. Then A. B.
Walkley in the Speaker : “ She gives us a mincing,
affected minx without a shed of dignity, or any sign by
which Francillon is to be distinguished from the very
type of woman for whom she was deserted by her hus-
band. She talks of her matronly instincts, but we notice
the audacity of her dicolletage.” Likewise George Ber-
nard Shaw in the Sat. Review : “ She seems, as far as I
can judge, congenitally incapable of a genuine impersona-
tion. But she has coached herself into a capital imitation
of a real French actress playing the part, which she
thoroughly understands. Saving one or two lapses into
clowning for provincial laughs, her performance is not a
bad specimen of manufactured acting.”
“ ‘ A Marriage of Convenience,’ at Haymarket Theatre,
is a most, effective little play, with excellent situations,
bright dialogue, and picturesque dresses (period, Louis
Quinze),” writes a Bulletin man from London. “ The
story turns on the parties to a mariage de convenance
falling in love with each other after marriage. There are
but two women in it, played by Winifred Emery and
Adrienne Dairolles, the former a consummate comedienne,
and the latter a clever French soubrette, who speaks
English very well. Mr. Cyril Maude is a comedian who
would be appreciated in Australia—quiet, refined, yet
effective to the last degree. The theatre is perfect,
orchestra hidden, yet perfectly audible; no horrible
tuning of instruments ; at the appointed hour the music
just begins; no bells rung, or beams bumped on the floor
—when the music ends the curtain simply goes up, and
the play starts ; programmes free, no shrill-voiced people
selling ‘book-o’-th’-something-or-other.’ Altogether no
friction or nerve worry. It was delightful.”
An Australian Abroad : “ Met Ellen Terry at Boulogne,
the other day, in a cab, with a great poke bonnet, hu ,|r e
goggles on her eyes (she suffers terribly with her eyes,
poor thing), and a gigantic cauliflower in her hand, which
she was taking back to Folkestone with her. She was over
for a day’s rest while the company was at Eastbourne.
Premier Reid slangwhanged Lyne for
taking advice on the Financial Statement from an
outside financial authority: “ Why did you not
go to the Auditor-General instead of to this
precious city-accountant?” yelled the new P.O.
But, only a Parliament or so ago, Reid presented
to the House a statement from three private
bankers, supposed to be corroborative of one of
his previous financial statements; and, when
asked why he had not used the services of Govern-
ment officials, retorted that he took the course any
honorable man should take.
Louis Staunton, of Penge mystery (alleged
woman-starving) notoriety, having completed his
20 years imprisonment, is coming to Australia
under his own name, because lie says he has
nothing whatever to hide or to be ashamed of.
The omission from Sydney Daily Tele-
graph of any report of the now-celebrated Naran-
dera child-flogging case, was, it now transpires,
caused by the negligence of their local correspon-
dent, with whose services, The Bulletin is in-
formed, the paper has dispensed.
The vital point in the amended regulations
for prevention of smuggling and illegal whisky
milling in N.S.W. is that, whereas the informer
had formerly to depend for reward upon a share of
any money-penalty that might be paid, he will
now, in addition, be secure of a fixed remunera-
tion, even where the offender elects to take it out.
The alteration should be a death-blow to the
private still.
The present session of Bananaland Parlia-
ment has been about the dullest on record —even
after making allowance for the antics of Mat.
Battersby. Government supporters outside com-
plain of the masterly inactivity of the Tozer
regime ; Labor-men and democrats growl because
they never seem to get any “ forrarder ”; and the
public at large wants to know what harm would
be done if the Palaver House were shut up alto-
gether.
Explorer Ernest Giles, wlio died the other
day at Coolgardie in very poor circumstances, is
—so the telegraph-man alleges—to have a costly
State funeral. When he was alive, the same
W.A. Government gave him a billet in an office
at =£lso a year—equal to about 355. a week in
Sydney or Melbourne. The S.A. Government
went one better, and gave him nothing at all, yet
Giles did a lot to open up these two provinces in
the old days.
The Maoriland Old Age Pension scheme is
getting fairly afloat. By way of a beginning, the
Government proposes to grant an allowance of
<£lB a year to the venerable inhabitant of 65 or
upwards whose income is less than £36 per
annum. It is a very small beginning certainly,
but better things may come of it later on, and
meanwhile his 30s. per month will enable the very
ancient patriarch to die a little more decently
than heretofore.
With reference to a paragraph in our issue
of October 16, relating to the marriage of Mr.
Muddit Allie, a Hindoo licensed hawker, well and
favorably known in the Moama district of N.S. W.,
The Bulletin learns with regret that certain
statements therein contained are totally incorrect.
As a matter of fact, Mr. Muddit Allie was married
by a Church of England clergyman at Rochester '
(Vic.), and his honeymoon was spent at the resi-
dence of a respected citizen of Carlton (Vic.). The
statement regarding police enquiries is quite un-
true. Mr. Muddit Allie has been a resident of
N.S. Wales for upwards of six years, and The
Bulletin is assured that both in business and
privately he bears an irreproachable reputation.
N.S.W. Marine Board decides that Piggy
had to save the Kameruka passengers because the
skipper was drunk; and that officer and another
—who is alleged not to have taken proper steps
to save the vessel—are called upon to show cause,
&c. Considering that, if the other officer had
bearded the captain, Things would have Hap-
pened—possibly he would have lost his billet—that
individual seems to have been in a mighty tight
place. It’s the story of the lost victoria
over again—a cat jumping to perdition while the
mice ponder how on earth to hang the bell round
her neck. Incidentally the Marine Board—not
reformed yet, by the way—“deprecated” the
look-out man being taken away to get ashes up!
If the Marine Board is really as green as it looks,
it probably does not know that there is hardly one
small steamer on the coast whose look-out man is
where he ought to be all the year round and all
night through.
The principal Yic. missionary of the Bible-
in-Schools party invariably informs his audience
of flabby pietists that the reason why it would be
“most unfair” to leave the Bible question to a
referendum is that “we have not time to educate
the people up to our standard of thought.” Then
the dear brethren, with the soulful desire for
things spiritual gleaming in their eyes, and a yearn-
ing for the next bun-struggle gnawing at their
innards, whack the floor hard with the gamp of
faith in token of approval. Yet it is difficult to
see what the bun-crowd has to complain of. It
had the people educated up to its standard for
many years, and it foolishly let them slide down
again—or slide up, whichever it may be. And, as
to not having time to educate them up again, it
has all the time there is. And if all the time
God has made isn’t time enough for it, how much
time does it want ?

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The slump in the N.S.W. revenue goes on con-
tinuously. For October the receipts were £704,285, or
a fall of £54,382 as compared with Oc f ., 1896. For the
first four months of the present financial year the re-
venue was £2,819,458 or £220,640 less than the revenue
for the four corresponding- months of last year. Premier
Reid estimated that this year’s revenue would be £40,000
more than last year's, and as a-third of the year is now
past, and instead of an increase there is a huge drop, the
Treasury has a lot of leeway to make up. The expendi-
ture up to date is £3,221,984, so the deficit is already
£402,526. Last year at the same date the deficit was only
£262,368, so in this respect also things are a lot worse
than they were in 1896. The 1896 revenue was less than
that for 1895 mainly through the abolition of Customs
duties, which at least furnished a decent explanation of
that particular falling-off. The 1897 revenue is so
far less than that for 1896 because there is a
slump in income - tax, customs, railway revenue,
tramway revenue, land revenue, school fees, water
and sewerage receipts, and miscellaneous takings—in
fact, there is a drop almost everywhere signify-
ing all-round depression. Under these painful conditions
the affable bungler who promised that his Freetrade
policy would make N.S.W. jolly and fat is engaged in
desperate efforts to avert yet another deficit. New
taxation on a large scale is promised, and the old policy
of charging ordinary expenditure to loans i 3 going ahead
more vigorously than ever. The outlay charged against
revenue in the last four months is £80,482 less than that
for the corresponding four months of 1896, and the out-
lay charged against loan funds is £88,706 more. The
beautiful similarity of these two sums is interesting. In all
these provinces there is no law to define what may or
may not be legally charged to loans; an unscrupulous
Treasurer with a good majority behind him may debit
any darned thing he likes to posterity so as to make his
accounts look decently square. And G. Reid is a genius
at this sort of work.
* * # *
The Reid Government proposes to economise
some more by hacking another slice off the road and
bridge vote. The following are the amounts paid out of
revenue by various recent Governments under this head ;
1890 Parkes ... £805,309
1891 „ ... 997,678
1892 Dibbs ... 889,633
1893 „ ... 706,563
1894-5 (1| year) Reid £830,654, or
per annum ... 553,770
1896 „ ... 666,863
1897 (estimate) ~ ... 550,000
The saving is partly effected by letting things slide and
mostly by shunting expenditure into loan account.
Premier Reid had the gall to bitterly accuse his predeces-
sor of starving the road and bridge vote to make a bogus
surplus. Yet, no one of late years ever starved this vote
as Reid himself has done. As a rule, when Reid accuses
anybody of doing anything it is a fair assumption that the
other fellow didn’t do it and that Reid did it himself.
* * * *
The splendid upward movement in the Victorian
revenue struck a very bad snag in October. For the first
quarter of the financial year the income was £39,857
ahead of the corresponding quarter of 1896. Then
October showed a fall of £46,525 as compared with
October, 1896, which leaves Turner £6668 worse for the
four months than he was at the same date 12 months ago.
That sound in the distance like a stampede of wild oxen
is the Knight of St. Hilda jumping on the glossy hat he
bought in London.
* * * *
Last year Premier Turner gave notice of his
intention to convert £2,250,000 of Victorian 4 per cent,
stock into 3 per cents., thus saving £ 22,500 a year inter-
est. All of it save £120,000 worth has since been con-
verted, and, by way of raising the money to pay off
this little remnant of the old stock, Turner proposes
to float a loan of £120,000 in Melbourne at 3 per cent,
with a minimum, it is alleged, of £lO2 10s. If he succeeds
it will be a great operation ; no Government has ever .yet
got over £lOO for 3 per cent, stock in Australia, and very
few have got that. Still, as the Vic. 3 per cents, are now
selling at £lO3 10s. in Melbourne (now-a-days one must
have a heap of money to be able with perfect safety to
cease work) it seems certain that there will be no diffi-
culty in getting £lo2los. for the new issue. Most of the
Victorian 4 per cents, were floated in the days of Duncan
Gillies’s boom, and the biggest part of them, unluckily,
don’t fall due till between 1913 and 1920. Considering
that the money could be got now at 3 per cent, if these
awful securities were only convertible, this is a truly
pleasant thing to look forward to. Victoria now pays on
its whole public debt £430,000 a year more than the pre-
sent market rate—which is a considerable price to pay for
defunct booms.
* * * •
The Vic. State Land Bank’s latest issue of 3 per
cent, bonds ( £18,100) only induced the public to subscribe
£775, the Savings Bank, as usual, taking the balance at
par. Evidently there is something wrong with the
arrangements. The Government’s 3 per cent, securities
are worth £lO3 10s. in the open market; the 3 per cents,
of the State Land Bank, which should be about as good
security, are almost unsaleable at £lOO. Probably the
whole trouble lies in the fact that the latter are repayable
at any time, while the former have a fixed currency. The
public doesn’t yearn after an investment which is liable
to hand the money back at any moment. By-and-bye
though the date is still far ahead—the Savings Bank will
have taken all the Land Bank bonds it can hold, and then
it will be absolutely necessary to give the latter a fixed
currency of 20 years or so to induce the public to put its
money in them.
* • * *
Queensland revenue for October (.£279,225) was
£12,619 less than that for Oct., 1896, and for the four
months of the financial year that have already elapsed
the receipts are fully £86,000 less than for the corres-
ponding four months of 1896. Consequently, things look
very, very blue for the Nelson-Byrnes-Tozer combination
and the worst-mortgaged of all the Seven Provinces
appears to be slithering rapidly to perdition.
* * * *
Tasmania’s revenue for October (£88,051) is an
increase of £8044 on that for Oct., 1896, and, with 10
months of the financial year gone, the receipts are now
£32,380 better than they were at the same date last year.
Westralia’s revenue for October (=£219,706) is
£35,040 more than for Oct., last year. Up to the end of
Sept., there was a decline of £25,821, but the unexpected
boom of October leaves things £9219 better for the four
months.
• • * •
The boom in A. J.S. Bank shares departed al-
most as suddenly as it arrived. In August, these shares
(£7 10s., paid up) sold at 2s. 9d. In October they hustled
up gaily to Bs. 9d. and by the 15th November they were
quoted sellers, ss. ; buyers, 3s. 6d. At the latter date, 4J
months of the half-year were gone, and about that time a
vague impression generally gets loose in the atmosphere
as to how the balance-sheet is going to turn out. The
.mpression for the moment is, apparently, that it will
turn out not too well. As The Bulletin pointed out last
half-year, when the appalling loss of £53,000 was an-
nounced, the reduction of deposit interest wasn’t nearly
sufficient to cover the gap, and the only things which
seemed likely to put the A. J.S. straight were some good
seasons, good wool prices, a revival of business, and a
general looking up of things. And, judging by that
usually reliable barometer, the State revenue, instead of
looking up things are, so far, remaining down “ some-
thing awful.”
* * » *
Premier Nelson has taken decisive action in the
matter of the Q.N.B. report and the Gentleman Who is
Away. The investigation committee-men mildly pro-
posed that the Government should give them £2OOO or
£3OOO so that they might take a trip to Europe and per-
sonally examine MTlwraith in his flowery haunt at
Heidelberg, the matter being indefinitely hung up mean-
while. _ Nelson instead got a resolution through the
House indemnif3’ing the committee from any legal conse-
quences which might ensue through the fury of the
Gentleman Who Is Away or any other person, and
directed it to send in its report straightway, merely
indicating what passages were to be taken with a grain
of salt pending any possible explanations from the
eminent and wrathful absentee in Germany. So there
should be some light upon the Q.N.B. mystery shortly.
• * * •
The Colonial Sugar Refining Co. made a profit
of £85,301 for the half-year just ended, which is about its
usual figure, and pays away £85,100 in a 10 per cent,
dividend. All the same, there seems to be some bad
trouble ahead for this big' and hitherto prosperous con-
cern. It reckons that its N.S.W. business will close in
1899, as by that time the progressive reduction of the
sugar-duty will have about extinguished the cornstalk
cane industry. Then it will have to sell its N.S.W. mills
and land for what they will fetch, and remove its
machinery, or, if that weighty property won’t stand the
cost of taking down, freight, and re-erection, sell the
latter for old metal; and the chairman alleges that the
loss will be something like £450,000. As the co. has only
£419,000 of reserves with which to meet this loss,
it seems likely to make a big inroad on capital,
and if Chairman Knox is really serious in his estimate,
the Colonial Sugar Co. needs to cut its dividend down to
7 per cent, or less—s per cent, would be better—and start
preparing for the wrath to come. For a co. which sees
all its reserves and apparently part of its capital on the
verge of going bung to pay away practically all its profits
in a 10 per cent, dividend looks like raging insanity.
The United Insurance Co. (Sydney) shows a
profit of £9952 for the year ending 30th Sept., as against
£11,466 last year and £10,668 in 1895, so, though the profit
is very handsome, it is hardly so good as it used to be. A
12J£ per cent, dividend absorbs £8528 ; £lOOO is added to
reserve, making it £54,000 ; and the remainder, with the
previous balance, leaves £10,576 to carry forward. The
United has also a re-insurance reserve of £12,500 ; a re-
serve of £2OOO for equalisation of dividends ; and one of
£lO,OOO to cover possible depreciation of investments,
making £89,076 of accumulations in all; and its solidity
seems beyond all question.
* * * *
The Householders’ Co-operative Stores, Limited,
now being floated in Sydney, should fill a big want. The
city has already one prosperous co-operative institution,
the Civil Service society, but it deals almost solely in
eatables and drinkables. The new concern is to include
not only these but also meat, fowl, fruit, the unobtrusive
cauliflower, pastry, crockery, cutlery, bedsteads and
bedding, linen, coal and wood, tobacco, music, &c., and
to be, as nearly as possible, a universal providery. It
has secured a huge, six-storey warehouse adjacent to
Sydney G.P.0., and the only thing required to make it a
very huge success is good management and a reasonable
amount of luck.

rent.
* * * •
The Sydney Lager-bier Brewing Co. Limited
(capital £80,000) expects to be in full blast during the ensu-
ing hot weather. The co. will no doubt have the blessing of
the other breweries, but its promoters look to finding a
field of their own in a huge family trade, instead of cut-
throating with the “tangle” factories. The new beer,
for which there is surely a huge opening, will contain no
more than 3.10 percent, alcohol, and will be sold bottled
at about 6s. per doz.; in small barrels fitted with syphons
it will be vended at about 6s. per 2L(gals.
* * * *
Melb.’s Manchester-lane is distraught with a
huge supposed Customs fraud. Such a wide frontier line
of judgment is necessarily allowed to officials that there
is a perpetual fight between softgoods houses to steal a
point without actual dishonesty, the exact range of which
is most difficult to define. Much depends on valuation
and tricky invoicing. An official may possibly be in the
swim, as alleged at present, but consider how difficult is
appraising when a bonnet may be worth two guineas this
summer and 2s. the next. Look again at the advantage
gained by a firm that sneaks in a big line and spreads it,
at a cutting price, all over Victoria with its commercial
travellers.
» * * *
A certain business man had the misfortune, a
few years ago, to be robbed of £4Boin gold and notes, the
proceeds of the sale of his stock to a stranger whom he
had met casually in the street, but whose name or address
he could not recollect. The poor victim, of course, had
to meet his creditors, who accepted 2s. 6d. in the £. How
misfortune runs in some families is shown by the afore-
said victim’s brother having been robbed lately of
£260 in almost the same way, and his brother-in-law, the
day afUr, having been burned out and his books and cash
destroyed entirely. Their unfortunate coincidences are
arousing much sympathy in certain circles, and it is
anticipated that something practical will be done for the
two unfortunate creditors at the meetings this week.
* * * *
Dominion James’s disinclination to file his
shovel led to a warrant of attachment for contempt being
issued last week. The £l7 creditor refused settlement of
his claim in full, and fainted in court when the warrant
was issued. There seems to be a great deal more in
James’s coyness and his bitter attack on the Judge, than
meets the eye. The real facts of the case would make
interesting reading indeed.
Yvon ‘N.S.W. Chief Justice Darley, in
reply to a deputation the other day, said that he was in
favor of shortening- the time allowed for pleading in
actions for debt and simple contract, but did not approve
of verifying pleas on oath. Of course, the adoption of
the latter course would mean the abolition of the present
technical system of common-law pleading now obsolete in
England and in all the Australias but N.S.W., which
stands alone in its adherence to this absurdity of the
past. The difference between the old system and the new
is simply that in the latter a man may state his cause of
action or his defence fully and in plain language ; and it
is a plan which, in N.S.W. Equity Court, has always
worked satisfactorily. Under the other you must couch
your claim in highly technical formulae—in which, if you
make the slightest error, you are liable to be bowled over
by a demurrer or something. Also, your pleas or answers
to the declaration must strictly conform to artificial and
antiquated rules. Instead of saying what you have got to
say in short, you must answer non-assumpsit, nunqvam
indebitatus, or some such impossible gibberish. N.S.W.
is only behind England and the other Australias in
this important matter because she has not yet got rid
of the superstition that law can’t be good unless it’s unin-
telligible.”

Melb. : “ What galls the repentant Federal

* * • •
Genial “Jacob,” the stalwart Dane who fell
dead on his way to business, the other morning, owing to
an unsuspected “ pound of suet ” round his heart, was a
well-known figure in Melb. metropolitan banking-circles.
“Jacob’s” duty was to keep the door of the clearing-
house when the boyish exchange-clerks entered with their
bags, not to escape again till everything was balanced.
The lads, impatient to get away, often tried to slip past,
but the door-keeper was a match for any number of them,
and in consequence had come to be regarded by budding
bankers as something of a Herculean hero.

The American-cwm-French silvermen’s proposals
to England looked cheeky enough ; but no doubt much
reliance was placed on the unrest in India. At the
middle of August London Daily Telesrapii, apropos
something else altogether, said : —“ Amongst some of the
most loyal and influential of our Indian subjects there is
a very strong feeling of dissatisfaction resulting from the
persistent fall in the price of the metal in which the
savings of the people are invested. . . We as a nation
cannot afford to ignore the fact that the native losses,
with silver at its present depreciated level, must be enor-
mous, and that discontent was bound to show itself in the
country sooner or later. The wonder is that it has not
maJ° itself evident before.”

» * • •
The day is coming evidently when “ baby
batteries will no longer monkey with the mother-lode ”in
Cobar, and Premier Reid’s statement that there is plenty
of local capital awaiting investment in N.S.W. mines will
be verified or disproved. The prospectus of the Cobar
Chesney Copper and Gold Mining Co. shows that a work-
ing capital of £65,000 will be knocked out of a total
capital of £200,000, of which £lOO,OOO is offered for sub-
scription in Australia. This mine, Melbourne-managed
for the past eight years, has been a frost to the extent of
some £25,000 having been dribbled away without any
results, save to leave the property in some sort of shape
for the future proprietors. The recognition that that
which Callahan, general manager of Lake View Consols,
called “ the mother-lode of N.S.W. ” is a huge gold and
copper lode underlying gold-bearing gossan, and that
these distinct classes of ore should be treated together by
smelting is a radical departure from the old system of tin-
pot batteries alone. Trewenack’s (of Lake George) report
is very sanguine, and is, perhaps, the more reliable in
that he and W. J. Collins, late manager of the Wentworth
Proprietary, made an unsuccessful effort to secure the
property Randolph Adams, late of the Sulphide Cor-
poration’s Broken Hill Central mine, is equally certain of
future profits. Thomas Longworth, one of the successful
tributors of the Great Cobar mine, and the manager of
that property, is convinced that the Chesney lode is the
same as that he is so successfully working, and predicts a
successful future if properly handled. Louis Janin, jr.,
who has made several examinations, predicts that a profit
of £90,000 a year will be earned on the scheme proposed.
The prospectus of this company occupies page 28 of this
week’s Bulletin.
* * * *
Ross, Ross and Co., financiers, 352 Collins-st.,
Melbourne, want freehold and c.p. securities for any part
of £lOO,OOO at about 4 per cent. Owners wishing to bor-
row should write this firm, who make no charges if securi-
ties are not accepted, and do not control wool or stock
sales.*
* * * *
N.S.W. Premier Reid has got into a tight
corner by saying N.S.W. mines were independent of
British capital, that for good mines plenty of money was
available within the province, and that they all knew the
sort of mines usually floated in London as well as the sort
of men who generally took them there. A wild whoop has
gone up from the N.S.W. Chamber of Mines, and several
Bull representatives are shrieking' for retractation,
an( * other things from the Premier. That part
of the Premier’s declaration referring to men and mines
is gospel truth, and in accord with what The Bulletin
has preached for years. If the Premier will promise to
make use of it for the good of the mining industry, the
writer will furnish him with a list of N.S.W. mines floated
in London, also of the bounders who floated them. Alto-
gether some hundreds of thousands were paid in cash to
the promoters ; hundreds of thousands of working capital
have since been spent in machinery and development on
the mines, but not one of this list, ever paid a shilling in
divs., and several have long been closed down. Others
are languishing from the want of gold in them-or in

Gundagai (N.S.W.) :—“ Machinery totalling
about 300 tons is coming by special train from Victoria
for Booth s United gold-mines. It was recently placed
on the Melb. market, when all the shares were taken up
in a few hours. It is now understood that an offer has
been cabled from London offering £60,000 cash and
60,000 fully-paid shares for the property as it now stands,
with £20,000 working capital.” If so, N.S.W. mining is
looking up in London, with a vengeance.
Watch for a movement in B.H. Norths —not
immediately, perhaps, but shortly. The theory that the
mine gets the continuation of the Junction shoot is being
promulgated again, and there is talk of a new deep shaft
being put down to tap it.
* * * •
British B.H. announces for the half-year a profit
of just over £15,000 —which a Is. div. consumes. Its
level, at present metal prices.
* « * •
B.H. South looks unmistakeably bare of div.-
paying ore; recent prospecting hasn’t helped it on much.
The workings in this mine are, measured along the out-
crop, a mile and five chains from Block 12 of the
B. Prop., where the fire is ; yet. the South men were, in
the first week in November, driven out of the deep work-
ing's by a heavy flow of water, which it must be sup-
posed comes from the floods still being poured on the fire
which was “ practically out” two months ago. Certainly
the fire isn’t gaining; but it is still costing a pretty penny
to keep it under control.
* * * •
N.S.W. Govt. Geologist Pittman, before W.A.
Mining Commission : He had no doubt the supplv of water
in the mines would diminish. The experience of all mining
districts pointed to that. He did not think there was the
faintest possibility of an artesian supply. He considered
the Kalgoorlie lodes were really auriferous dvkes, but he
was distinctly favorable to the opinion that they would
live in depth. From samples of Kanowna cement he had
seen in Sydney, he then thought it was sedimentary rock
or desert sandstone, but, since inspecting the deposit, he
admitted that he was quite wrong. It was a mineral de-
posit, and there was a great probability that large deposits
of deep alluvial gold would be found in the near future
about the fields. The prospectors, so far, had only
scratched over the surface—had been working false-
bottoms, in fact—and he would be greatly surprised if the
finding of deep-leads at Kanowna was not repeated in
other places, for there they had the facts and evidence of
deep alluvial mining.
* * *
Kalgoorlie wire: “Rumored that a very rich
strike has been made at Reison’s Reward, one of the Pad-
dington Consols mines floated by Mr. Kaufman. No
official information is available, but it is said that the lode
is rich in free gold, besides massive sulphides. Mr.
Kaufman’s luck is so good that the Wealth of Nations is
now a rich mine, but without water.” When were the
public ever told that the Wealth of Expectations was any-
thing else but a marvellously rich mine? Why lias it
been made poor—to attribute' its richness to Kaufman's
luck? Surely if Kaufman’s luck puts gold in all the
mines he handles it ought not to stop at water ; hence
the writer now expects to hear that the Wealth of Nations
has been flooded to such an extent as to wash all the ore
away, leaving nothing but the pure gold behind, and to
hear the incident attributed to Kaufman’s luck.

The Coolgardie correspondent of the Mining
Standard “ Bayley’s Consols battery starts this week,
with a large supply of payable ore in sight. This mine
should give its holders good returns before Christmas.”
Yes! Under 7dwt,s. to the ton. “Londonderry: With a
good supply of water and plenty of batterv-power, it will
yet give a good return to the holders.” Hitherto, there
has been an idea that a supply of gold was wanted to give
a good return to the holders, inasmuch as it takes a
10,000 oz. cake to pay one little shilling div. ! “ The White
Feather Reward has cleaned 430 tons, giving a return of
2400 z. With a strong body of stone and the new battery
almost ready, this mine will soon be among the dividend-
payers.” Great Scott! It has been crushing over two
years, and failing to pay divs. on l£oz. stone, of which it
crushed many hundreds of tons. How is it going to pay
at SOZ. ? “ The Golden Bar still continues the same con-
sistent returns.” Then follows an apology for avery poor
return. Calls are the most consistent thing about the
Golden Bar. There are other similar pars., but enough
have been quoted to show how well-trained this gent, is to
represent a mining journal.

A PARALLEL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Life’s a gold-rush to the Nor’ward,
Through the famine and the cold.
Crowding millions crushing forward
In the fearsome fight for gold ;
Swarming down Life’s Klondyke river
Not a single thought flung back
Co the weaker ones for ever
Sleeping on the Klondyke track.
With their labors bent and broken,
Furrowed with their hopes and fears,
Bearing for their toil’s love-token
Grey hairs gathered from the years ;
When they come to count the tally
Will they win so much at last ?
—Lone graves in life’s Yukon Valley,
And the world still crowding past!
WILL H. OGILVIE

COGMAG
Wigs or no wigs is likely soon to be a
keenly-debated question in N.S.W. Supreme
Court. One Judge has said that he will go in his
own hair while the hot weather lasts, and the
example is sure to be followed by a batch of coun-
sel. “But,” asks another Judge, “ where will
the innovation stop?” The cautious man has
evidently been terrified by a glimpse of the Full
Court sitting in its undershirt.
* * * »
The difference of opinion among N.S.W.
Judges as to whether they can, under the local
law, hear divorce cases in camera, or with such
studied pre-arrangements as amount to in camera,
will get an airing soon, as the so : divorced wife of
an influential politician has determined to apply
for alimony, holding that the court proceedings
were null and void and that her status as wife is
still legally unimpaired.
CRICKET IN CHURCH.
(In some of the Anglican churches about Sydney the numbers of the hymns are displayed in large figures
on cards on the wall.)
TOM (just awaking from, a mild night mare in which he dreams that the Australian XI. are
playing All England XI., and that six of them are out for Iff —to his wife): “Ah,
that’s better! Who made the 112, my dear —Gregory or Giffen?”
Were a public register (similar to that
which records the names of the regular police)
kept of the names of the informers in the secret
pay of the Vic. detectives, not a few fashionable
gentlemen in society, who are supposed to have
“ private means,” would lack the many invitations
to dances, &c., with which they are now favored.
* * * *
A certain private lady detective, in answer
to an advt. a little while ago, called upon a lady,
who offered her liberal terms to watch her hus-
band and find out why he did not come home very
regularly. The next morning the d., as instructed,
waited near the lady’s house to identify the man
she was to watch. That job was never completed,
inasmuch as a moment’s view of the hubby re-
vealed to the d. the fact that he was the gentleman
under whose protection the lady d. was then living
and had been living for a year.
* * * *
Member : “ The decadence of certain
bong-tong Melb. public-schools was strikingly illus-
trated at the England v. Vic. match, when the
members’-reserve was infested with a swarm of
youths from the ‘ highest-class ’ schools. The
blasphemous and bestial yarns and conversation of
most of them —particularly of one gang who sat in
the pavilion taking little or no notice of the play-
shocked and disgusted all within reach of their
loud and unlovely voices. And they wore the
colors of their several schools, and bellowed out
teachers’names, in order, presumably, to advertise
their superior education ! ”
* * * *
One Australian Governor bars races and
the theatre—his penchant is concerts of the
“ Messiah ” order. H.E. and family roll up re-
ligiously to orchestral shows- and though they
“ never part,” they are always saluted with “ God
Save the Queen ’’—the audience standing like
dumb-dog sycophants. Not content with this,
“ Govt. House ” had the front to ask the manage-
ment recently if it could arrange for the audience,
at the conclusion of the show, to remain seated
while the Governor and suite passed out. There
is, however, a limit to all things human—and the
management said No.
* * * *
“ The Australian ‘ Naicest ’ will, no doubt,
ere this have copied the latest London society
expression ‘Rather fun,’” writes a Bulletin
scribe from the Big Smoke. “It is in general
use here, and seems to have ousted ‘ Quaite so ! ’
from the field. Instead of saying ‘Quite so’ you
simply say, ‘ That was rather fun,’ or ‘ That will
be rather fun,’ pronounced ‘ Rahthah fen,’ of
course. It embraces almost as wide a region of
meaning as a French shrug, and implies all sorts of
praise of various divergent events. For instance,
a concert, a ball, a cricket-match, a divorce-case, a
bicycle-accident, a picnic, a continental tour, a
supper after the theatre, a new play, nay, a pro-
posal to do anything, or a description of anything
done might all be ‘ Rahthah fen ! ’ ”
To-day every
bootshop in Mel-
bourne has its
Micawber stand-
ing patiently on
the kerb. This
poor wretch keeps
a keen watch on
the doorway, and,
the momenta pur-
chaser emerges,
he steps forward,
and, touching his
hat with a grimy
forefinger, humbly
requests the favor
of the old boots.
In nine cases out
of ten Micawber
scores.

The back-
blocks is essenti-
ally a land of con-
tradictions. At
Cloncurry, the
heart of a great
pastoral district, the residents have just
been furiously howling because they’ve had
no milk for three weeks and the butcher was
unable to supply them with beef for days at
a stretch. If it hadn’t been for tinned-dog
and condensed-milk the settlement would
almost have perished.

behavior. Police-court. Boy charged with

but his smoke was that vile that the public
couldn’t pass. It proves me case. If yer
Worship will ax him to smoke one ”
Fined ss.
Some distinctly precious survival of the
past has written to S.A. Register to say that—
the drought in the North is the direct intimation of
the Divine displeasure at the low esteem paid by too
many public men to Bible morality, and at the trans-
gressions of the community generally ;
and actually proposes that —
the settlers in the distressed districts join together
and offer as a sacrifice one animal in their flocks for
each family, as a tribute of humility and confession
of dependence.
The Stone Age is evidently yet with us in spots !
* * * *
In one N.S.W. Govt, department there is
grievous tribulation over a recent edict sternly
forbidding fruit-eating during office hours, and
announcing that any future offender will be
“dealt with.” What will be the precise fate of
the delinquent is thus left open to speculation,
and the ukase itself is obviously based upon the
fiction that the gentlemen of the department have
something else to do than suck oranges and nibble
bananas.

on board a mailboat, effusively : “Oh ! captain ;
I've been reading Nansen’s ‘ Farthest North ’ it’s
perfectly delightful! I like it ever so much better
than ‘ Little Lord Fauntleroy ’ ! ”

“ Aft er dark, ’’ writes an Australienne to The
Bulletin, from France, “ Parisian cyclists, male
and female, carry Chinese lanterns instead of
lamps. These many - colored lights more easily
attract the eye of the careless pedestrian, and add
considerably to the general glitter and glare of the
city.”
* * * ♦
“ Excessive zeal ” is what a Sydney magis-
trate calls the systematic police-perjury which
charges nearly all the drunks brought before him
with having been also “ disorderly.” The chief
desire of the experienced toper is to get home as
unostentatiously as possible, and, the more
“ tanked ” he is, the less capable he is of statutory
“ disorder ” ; but, as he is also the less capable of
remembering afterwards what actually took place,
the “ disorder ” charge has hitherto been a soft
thing for the false-swearing copman. It may be
true, as a Sydney police-officer retorted the other
day, that there are “not so many policemen in
gaol as lawyers ” —but what about the ought-to-be ?
* * * *
A man of many parts is an ex-Vic. bank-
clerk now located on the Westralian goldfields.
He had a “book” on the A. J.C. Derby, and, while
waiting for wires with results of race, he was
asked to read the burial-service on a just-deceased
woman. He did it. Then, after attending to his
“ book, ” he acted as chairman at a nigger-minstrel
show ; then he refereed a fight—and fell in him-
self. Bookmaker, parson, minstrel, and referee.
A good all-round man.

In a certain N.S.W. township the office of
coroner became vacant through removal to another
district of the P.M. (who had theretofore per-
formed the duty). The P.M., thinking that the
billet would not be unacceptable to a smart young
townsman whom fortune had not been treating
too kindly, offered to recommend him to the
authorities, and did so. When the matter came
before the bench of magistrates, only this one
nomination had been received, but one J.P., a
business rival of the nominee, raised objections to
the appointment of such a YOUNG man, and one
who was not a magistrate. The other beaks said
nothing, and the presiding magistrate, after wait-
ing a considerable time for further nominations }

At Gov. Buxton’s hops no cards, no
smokes, and no drinks are allowed—except “ soft
tack” and claret. Yet the evangelical viceroy-
draws big divs. out of Truman, Hanbury and
Buxton’s London brewery.
* • * *
In a certain M.L. town is a publican who
is an enthusiastic fisherman. On the first day of
this season he landed a fine trout, which in the
wisdom of his generation he forthwith despatched
to Lord Ranfurly. His Ex. thereupon signified
that he would come along shortly for some fishing
and would be pleased to have the company of Mr.
A. When the Governor did turn up he sent an
A.D.C. to patronise A.’s pub. to the extent of 36
gallons of beer, a case of whisky, ditto of brandy,
and so forth, to the tune of d;2l in all. A., who
was with the vice-regal party, chuckled to him-
self at the lovely investment he had made with
that trout, but when he got home he nearly
fainted with horror to find that his barman knew
nothing of such a splendid turn-over. The A.D.C.
had made a mistake, and a rival Bung had col-
lared the spoil !

* * * *
The law’s enlightenment : At a recent
Sydney inquest, where the corpse was plainly the
result of a 30ft. fall down a ship’s hold, the jury
returned a verdict of “ death from natural causes”
—all the witnesses of the accident having gone
to sea in their vessel whilst the injured man was
under hospital treatment.
* * * *
Ballarat puts forward a loud claim as the
site for the Federal capital. Ballarat is a hand-
some, healthy town, and the abundance of lamp-
posts in the main streets increases its suitableness
as a legislative centre. People who have seen
politicians returning late from banquets will
readily understand. Those numerous Ballarat
statues are not without their advantages, too, as
was illustrated by the case of a Melb. man of some
note on the occasion of a vice-regal visit to the
garden city. The metropolitan personage was
seen wandering from statue to statue in the early
moon-lit hours, and, when interviewed by a sym-
pathetic policeman, was affectionately embracing
the feet of the poet Burns. “ Never met sush a
sochiable lot of (hie !) fellrsh in me life (hie !);
never ! ” he said as the law gently led him away.
“EGO.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
So old I am, I laugh to scan the days
Of childhood’s folly, passing in a maze
Of Fame and Love, till Age awakes to find
These are but dreams, and youth is left behind.
A smile, a sigh, a sob, and they are gone,
Fled with the past, and Age stands still, alone,
While in the shades of the Invisible
Are but the outer gates of Heaven and Hell.
ALICE M'KINNON.

Dear Bulletin. —“ What we
eat! ” Down Belmore Markets, last
Saturday morning, I noticed a Chi-
naman sitting on a heap of cabbagee
—nothing between his clothing and
the vegetables. When the Chow is
driving to market, he invariably sits
on the cabbagee, with—perhaps—a
sugar-bag between his dirty nether-
garments and the provender. Also,
on a North Sydney boat, one day, I
saw a Chow breathing on apples
and then polishing them with a
dirty rag. The shine produced on
the apples hawked by Dagoes in
the parks is also due to saliva and
elbow-grease. The common white
inan is too beastly lazy to spit on
his apples and polish ’em with his
handkerchief.—D.
Re “ What we eat,” a story is
going round the English press.
Two workmen meet. “What are
you goin’ to have for dinner a
Sunday, Bill?” “ Dunno ; missus
alius looks after that.” “What do
you say to a nice bit o’ pork ? ” “O,
I dessay we might.” “Well, now,
we’re goin’ to kill our pig, an’ I’ll
sell you a loin cheap.” “ All right,”
said Bill, “send it over.” Satur-
day night came, but no pork, so Bill
sent his son over for it. When he re-
turned, without the meat, “Where’s
the loin of pork ? ” said his father.
“ Can’t have it,” returned the boy;
“ they ain’t goin’ to kill the pig,
’cos he’s gettin’ better.”

•** * *
Mayor M‘Eacharn, of Melb., astonishes
the local drunks and larcenists by figuring as
Chief Magistrate at the City Court in his gorgeous
robes of office. For years past the metropolitan
Mayors have refrained from decorating the Bench,
and the novelty of M‘Eachern’s appearance at the
right hand of Panton, P.M., is understood to
please the Court and do no harm. It will give an
effective touch of color to the preliminary hearing
of murder-cases, anyway.

The ocher day a johnnie rode up
fco a Maitland pub., hot and dry. “Aw !
landlord, vewy hot! I’ll —er —have a
whisky-and-soda—Schweppe !” Johnnie
helped himself very liberally, drank, and
tendered sixpence. Boniface looked at
it, spun it, spat on it for luck, pulled out
a threepence and handed it, with “Change,
sir!” “Aw! only threepenny drinks
heah. Nevah mind the change—l’ll cut
it out.” He poured out a stiff nobbier,
and this time the landlord handed him a
penny. “Aw! what’s this?” “Rebate,
sir. We always allow a rebate on large
quantities.” “Aw, I see—you’re a wag.
Don’t you find it expensive ? ”
* * * *
A popular Sydney medico, noted
for his good nature, was a little too kind-
hearted a few nights ago. Returning
homeward, near midnight, he encountered
a month-married friend, who had been
imbibing. Knowing where he lived, the
doctor guided him home, and rang the
bell. The door at once flew open, and a
white-robed figure clutched the benedict,
shook him, and dragged him inside. The
doctor waited to see no more, but turned
away. Half-way down the street, a clatter
drew his attention once more, and he
beheld his friend, shot forth catapult-
fashion, and land in a heap on the pave-
ment. When he bent over the sobered
wreck, it looked up at him and gasped,
“Wrong house—don’t live here —moved
lasht week.”

Melb. : “A young lady, just mar-
ried in Sydney, is daughter of a rich and
worthy widow, known as Queen of Pork-
opolis, in a Melb. suburb devoted to pork
and bacon-curing. She was mainly in-
strumental in building the finest local
church, known as ‘the cathedral.’ A
preacher was got one Sunday morning
quite ignorant about the pork business of
the locality. He dilated on the miracle of
the devils being driven into the herd of
swine, and justified it because of the
diseases engendered by pork-eating.”

Normanton (N.Q.) Circuit Court
was adjourned recently to enable a Croy-
don solicitor to attend and defend certain
aboriginals accused of murder. Solicitor,
however, didn’t arrive—the Crown fee was
too small to attract one. Mr. Justice
Power didn’t think much of solicitors who
would not defend men tried for their lives.
Considered “the profession was getting
low when it was reduced to a monetary
one.” This was really fine! But why
shouldn’t the Crown pay a decent fee in
tsuch’ cases?
The real Mackay—The Australian Horse.
* * * *
An enterprising thief actually stole a law-
yer’s boots from under the solicitors’-table in Melb.
City Court the other day. The lawyer had taken
his hoof-covers off to ventilate his corns, and when
he wanted them they were gone. The Buli.etin
has no sympathy with the sufferer ; the ordinary
layman who dared to take off his boots in Mr.
Panton’s court would get two years for contempt.
The “ explanation ” of his conduct, which
Bairnsdale schoolmaster Langier gave to Yic.
Public Service Board was very funny reading at
Melb. City court last week and even the highly
expurgated edition published in the dailies had its
humorous aspect. In it Mrs. Langier asserted
that the girl be is now charging with perjury was
just the sort of character he wished to incorporate
in a book he contemplated writing, and, on his
realising this, he set about to “study” the young
lady, which he did by moonlight in a paddock,
where, so he said, she frequently made
attacks on the virtue of the said Langier. But
Langier pursued his investigations purely in
a scientific spirit, and the fifth act
was never played ; although Langier naively
remarks that “everything else” took place
between them. The strangest revelation of
all was that Mrs. Langier, the wife of the
schoolmaster, was kept fully posted by her hus-
band as to the progress of her husband’s “ psycho-
logical studies,” and apparently made no demur
through alleged faith in Langier. She did, in-
deed, on one occasion pen a note to the torrid
girl threatening divorce against her husband, and
mentioning that “ the moon had disclosed the pre-
vious night’s studies ”to her enquiring eye. She
added that her husband had made a confession,
full and particular, if not true, for she re-
peated details of a most complete character and
told the young lady that she was welcome to
her husband’s heart and hand “both of which had
been much polluted during the past six or seven
months.” Mrs. Langier added that “ I will never
lower myself to be associated with one who be-
lieves that variety is the spice of life.” The last
day the case was heard this letter was just being
questioned upon when Langier stepped behind the
witness and ordered her to be careful. P. M.
Panton promptly sent him to gaol for 24 hours for
contempt, and a long argument followed as to
whether the case should go on and the sentence
date from the rising of the court. Just in the
middle an indiscreet individual arrived and men-
tioned that Ranjitsinhji was batting, and the
court was adjourned in 1 l-ssec. The girl was
able to prove by the evidence of the two medical
witnesses that her virtue survived all the con-
tention.
• • * «
This case recalls an old comedy, named
“ The Dramatist.” Yapid, the hero, gets into all
sorts of feminine intrigues, to obtain pabulum for
waiting. _ The young lady thought Mr. Langier
was making love, while he was really only conduct-
ing experiments. When he got to, say, the 17th
chapter of his novel, “Innocent Adultery,” or
whatever it was, he found his physiology ran
short. Lord Nozoo had got the bewitching Mrs.
Jelly bag into the Park, among the ornate
aristocratic benches, but somehow the writer
had not the hang. So. in a perfectly innocent,
and merely Marcus-Superbus - and - Mercia way,
just stage fashion, he had to lead his lady-model,
his fellow-school-teacher, beneath the red gums,
stringy-barks, or whatever happened to be there-
abouts, and talk some stage-love, with a trifle of
stage-pawing, after which he would rush off to his
desk and scribble away with all the fury of a
Zolaa. But the poor actress in the concern took
it for genuine. The novelist said to himself :“ I
must go out with the model to-day and get the
correct physiological relation of Lord Nozoo’s arm
to Mrs. Jellybag’s waist. Then the phenomena of
the kiss. Let me see. Does the wicked earl im-
plant it on the mouth, the cheek, or the neck ?
Does the lady-novelist similarly experiment on the
male bird ? ”

The ‘ Home ’ mentioned at least strives to preserve
the individuality and independence of tne aged
poor. Each old body can spend his (or her) Bs. a

Henniker Heaton’s proposal to travel the
feawlinies with a team of British M.P. cricketers
has a decidedly himperial appearance on close
inspection. The team will, in the first place,
assert the greatness of the “dear old mother
country ” by wallopping their Canadian and Aus-
tralian opponents in one innings, more likely than
not. Also, they will probably promote further
“loyalty” by the splendor of their clothes and
mannahs, and every time the captain of the dis-
tinguished visitors returns thanks or proposes the
health of the beaten team, he will lay himself out
to tighten those silken bonds. Moreover, the
triumphs of the All-Hingland Parliamentary
Eleven will furnish another Tory argument against
payment of members, and we will be treated to
invidious comparisons between the successful ex-
Etonian bowler who sits in the House of Commons
gratis, and the lamentable Australian Labor-
member who was “dismissed for a duck.” If H.
Heaton, M.P., succeeds in bringing a team to tho
wickets, the British Lion should score heavily.

Dear Bulletin, —Are you taking on jealous
men? I know an old Murrumbidgee boundary
rider who’s wedded to a very young woman. He
gets up before daylight every morning and care-
fully sweeps all round his hut, and when returning
at night closely scans the ground for tracks ! His
hut is 18 miles from anywhere.—6 xB.

That pretty little scandal re indecent
assault in a photographic studio in Charters
Towers has resulted in the dismissal of the detec-

sleuth-hounds of the law who worked up this
pretty little ease, and almost incited the comnji-
sion of the crime, received an awful showing-up in
the Police-court at the hands of John Hoolan,
J.P. Public indignation was too much even for
the torpid authorities in Brisbane, and a public
enquiry was held resulting as above-mentioned.

THE PRODIGAL’S REPLY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Some time ago Bob crossed the foam
And sailed for Cool-gar-dee,
And when our hard-up boy came home,
No wealthier was he !
We saw him—at “The Rising Sun,”
And o’er an “s.-and-b.”
We asked if Dad his prodigal son
Was mighty pleased to see?
“ Perchance now you have been, old chap,
So long—and far—away.
The Guvnor’s put the wine on tap,
And killed the calf to-day?”
The Prodigal laughed a weary laugh
Whilst sadly answered he :
“Dad didn’t kill no fatted calf,
But d d near slaughtered me ! ”
