A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, November 16, 1897.
Mr Dear Mtee—
Mrs. David, wife of the coral-boring Professor,
interestingly lectured the Victoria Salon on
Thursday afternoon on her adventures in the
Pacific Isles, dwelling particularly on dress, love,
and cookery among the Ellice Islanders. She
brought with her many examples of native gar-
ments —the whole wardrobe was easily carried in
a small hand-basket—and exhibited her little
daughter as a living picture “ faa Funafuti.”
(For fear any reader should feel shocked at such
goings-on at a ladies’ club, I hasten to add that
Miss David is only seven.) The “reform dress,”
a hideous print “ lava lava ” or kilt, and large
square bib back and front, is a lamentable descent
in beauty from the pre-missionary flower-and-leaf
garlands. Mrs. D’s audience laughed over a
specimen of a fashionable island hat—the first
missionary told his female flock to cover their
heads in church, so they all copied Mrs. Mis-
sionary’s best tile, and the unlovely style with
variations still prevails. When a girl falls in love
in the Ellice Group, she doesn’t let concealment
prey on her dusky cheek, but tells her love by
shaking a lighted torch over her victim’s back and
literally scorching him with passion’s fires ; or else
she coyly shies cocoanuts at his head. Two
students accompanying the scientific party were
the objects of such pointed attentions from native
belles that all the scientists passed one lively
week dodging flying cocoanuts. Funafuti girls
can aim straight. Infanticide is almost as common
as it is in civilised Sydney, but is practised by
system, every second child being destroyed. The
primitive Malthusianism arose from a fear that
the food-supply wouldn’t support a rapidly grow-
ing population.
They say the earl—the bogus Hardwicke—
became engaged to a certain squatter’s daughter
on the strength of his blue blood. When the
downfall came some kind friend rang up the
lady’s family to ask if congratulations were to be
offered on the felicitous event. And a loud “No ”
thundered through the ’phone like the report of
the one o’clock gun at Dawes Battery.
Many wheel women pedalled themselves on to
the crieket-match. Spinning along the road they
didn’t, as a rule, look lovely. Bikeresses don’t
beauty much in costume. They flash past shop-
windows too quickly to be able to see their reflec-
tions. The shop-window is the pedestrian woman’s
best friend, and helps materially to keep her tidy.
Another reason for the cycling dress being still a
mere compromise with ugliness is that you can’t
sit still on your machine in front of a looking-
glass and get a back view in a hand-mirror. If
that oould be accomplished almost every wheel-
woman would jump off her jigger with a yell of
despair. For it is the back view that is so
appalling. Up-to-date woman is decidedly not
made for the machine, and there is money await-
ing the inventive person who will make a machine
to fit the woman. Two wheels, a pair of tongs, a
stony-hearted saddle, and an onion-net—combined
—can’t ever be fixed up into an ideal car of Venus.
Members of the unlucky “ Bunch of Keys ”
touring co. were in town for a few days before
leaving for Newcastle. The ladies were all worked-
up to hysterical pitch, and the men depressed.
The trip was a long drawn-out chapter of acci-
dents. A tragic incident happened the day before
the fire at Bourke ; the co., which had put up at a
hotel on the river bank, saw a boat containing five
persons capsize before their eyes; four were
rescued with difficulty, but the fifth, a girl of 19,
was caught by a snag which had to be dynamited
before the body was recovered. Next night the
theatre was burnt down, the men’s dressing-rooms
suffering most. G. Lauri and W. Gourlay lost
much property, and the latter had the coat partly
burnt off his back ; the remains of the garment he
keeps as a souvenir. Next afternoon, having
moved on to their next town with a faked variety
show to fill in advertised dates with, they had
another fire-alarm—the hotel outhouses were des-
troyed. At Orange, through the upsetting of a
kerosene-lamp behind the scenes just before the
curtain rose, they had scare No. 3. At Dubbo
they arrived too late to play. From Orange to
Bathurst they travelled by a goods-train, which
crawled and shunted and kept the travellers grill-
ing in the fierce heat all day. Last horror of all,
Mrs. Lauri and Miss Gourlay had as companion
in the sleeper from Albury an invalid being taken
by her husband and nurse to a Sydney hospital,
and the poor creature’s moans kept them sympa-
thetically awake all through the last wretched
night of their ill-fated tour. Business was good
through it all.
Two well-known Australian surnames figure in
recent mail-news. Capt. R. C. Stephen, son of
Mr. Sept. Stephen, M.L.C., of Sydney, was
married in London on Sept. 29th to Miss Edith
Mary Osborne, of Elsternwick, Melb. Our Naicest
people, through intermarriage, will soon become
one vast family party.
This cricket-adoring population turned out in
thousands for the first day of “ the match,” and
on Saturday there was standing-room only (and
not much of that) in all the reserves and stands.
The back of the new ladies’ stand was fringed five
deep and even the little side balconies, from which
nothing can be seen, and all the stairs were masses
of muslins and millinery. Two records were put
up during the game: the first day’s temperature
topped the Nov. heat average, the second day had
the largest “gate” recorded at S.C.C. There
was no promenading on Saturday. Early comers
who had secured seats wisely sat in them all the
time, and went without afternoon-tea rather than
lose their places ; or else they employed obliging
men of their party as tea-room scouts, who darted
about in the crowd doing balancing-acts with full
tea-cups and piles of cake. It was worth the
money to see the crowd, and thousands of women
went out solely for that purpose, or else to behold
“Ranji.” Feminine interest in the players was
certainly absorbed by that well-boomed alien.
His every catch was applauded from the ladies’
reserves, and when he went to keep the wicket,
kid gloves were split in enthusiasm. Any merely
white player might crouch behind the sticks with-
out a hand-clap, but Ranji, “ the Prince,” was the
hero of the day with the title-worshipping woman-
kind. On .Saturday it was a cruel disappointment
to his admirers that his batting career was over
before lunch, and he was behind the scenes all the
afternoon. The men went out to see cricket, the
women to see each other and Ranji. By-the-
way, “ the Prince ” is the only player who doesn’t
turn up his sleeves. I wonder why 1
. The big-hat nuisance was as annoying in the
tightly-packed stand as it is in theatres. I was
amused at one woman, who was loudly denouncing
an “ Ich dien ” bunch of plumes on a cart-wheel
of white straw, which blocked up the view in
front of her, while the grumbler herself had on
her own head a funnel of pink ribbon, with chiffon
puffing out of it like smoke, which effectually
eclipsed the whole game from the man just behind
her. On the front lawn seat a scarlet parasol
glared and blotted out the universe to a large and
irritating throng of baek-seaters, who murmured
angrily about the lady “ with the umbreileru p, ”
until someone.remonstrated, and the bull-enrager
came down with an irate snap. The Governor’s
pen was filled every day, but sacredly curtained
off.above the railing ; all one could see were the
twin points of Capt. Fielden’s moustache, a healthy
fruit-orchard on the hat of Mrs. Sketfington-Smyth
(how Thackeray would have loved that name!), and
a pair of soiled lavender gloves waved by Hon.
Dorothy Brand in the background. Aides Free-
man-Thomas and Shaftesbury came overland for
the match, at which they wore straw hats sporting
the Australian colors. Aides, luckier than abo-
rigines, are railway dead-heads all the time, and
travel on the nod even when they go a-pleasuring.
On Monday, one of the sights of the stand was
Jakoff Hambourg industriously acting waiter, and
carrying tea to a whole ladies’ school, while the
fickle damsels looked over the top of their deposed
idol’s head to gaze at Ranji.
The Cri. was crowded on Monday night, when
the pupils of Miss Dunn, of Petersham, took the
stage, and gave an exhibition of fancy dancing in
aid of the Lewisham hospital. The juveniles did
all their steps and whirlings with great accuracy,
and the audience applauded enthusiastically!
I er *J. a P s the quaintest item of the evening was
the Lancers by a troupe of performing babies, who
toddled through all the manoeuvres without a
single breakdown—more than can be said of most
sets of Lancers at an adult ball. Miss Dunn’s
rose ballet was a prettily-posed arrangement of
small children in the background, while energetic
infants of a larger growth skipped in the lime-
light. The. children stepped out in all the dancing
in such a lively way, and entered into the spirit
of the thing so thoroughly, that it wouldn’t be
surprising if an 'epidemic of cellar-flaps, cart-
wheels and hand-springs were to attack the youth
of this city. Mr. Hawthorne, M.L.A., sponsored
the show and spoke a piece, remarking that the
juvenile dancers will probably whirl again for
charity in the suburbs.
Mrs. H. Booth (wife of the Harmy Com-
mandant) is banging the big drum in Sydney on
behalf of the saffron-haired brigade. Her method
is the “sisterly-sensational.” All patronising and
stand-off-for-I-am-holier-than-thou ” schemes of
reaching “ the fallen ” are strictly tabooed. It is
worth remarking that this up-to-date hail-fellow-
well-met-slap-on-the-back style of Salvation was
invented and used with immense success by
Mrs. H. Booth’s really grand and brainy mother-
in-law— the Mrs. Booth (the “General” is only
her widower). The best description of Mrs.
Booth s style of dealing with London street-
walkers is written by an authoress whose
book is a sealed volume to the Respectable.
It shows that Mrs. B. had that intuitive
gift of sympathy which amounted to genius. Her
followers copy her ; the big drum and the glaring
ad. and the sympathetic tea-fight remain, but
genius is incommunicable.
A correspondent writes noting some mistakes
in my account of the Limerick family. The
family tree of that aristocratic house was, it
seems, never transplanted in Victoria, the second
Lari of L. being in N.S.W., where he served Gov.
Bourke in a clerical capacity, and in Norfolk
Island where his son, the lately-deceased Earl,
was born in ’44. This son was an earlier “ Aus-
tralian contribution to the House of Lords ” than
the newly-ennobled Egmont, as his turn came in
66, on his brother’s death ; as an English peer he
sat as Baron Toxford (a baron is two rungs lower
in the social ladder than an earl); a younger
brother of his, Mr. E. S. Pery, is now settled in
N.b.W. Another county of Limerick family,
that of “ Guillamore,” was in Australia at one
time very much down on its luck. The late Paget
S.tandish O’Grady (4th Viscount), before he got his
title, was driving bullocks in Victoria in 1860.
The air is full. of rumors concerning the
morals of a very high, titled, Sydney personage
who is reported to have got himself into trouble
over the charming wife of a wealthy Sydney resi-
dent,. and will, it is said, play a very prominent
part in some divorce proceedings. Fabulous sums
are mentioned as having been offered by him to
square things, but, so far, it is stated, the
impugned person is not to be “squared.” Now,
I have it on the best authority that the whole
story is a malicious lie—originated, no doubt, by
certain dear friends of the lady, who is now
travelling with her husband and is on the best of
terms with l\im.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“At last week’s tea-drinking at the Austral
Salon, the hostess (Mrs. Theo. Hagens) entered a
protest against the way in which Salonites who
entertain at the above fashionable coterie waste
their substance in riotous cream-cakes and soft-
drinks. Mrs. A. gives an ‘afternoon,’ and pro-
vides hot-house flowers and printed tinted pro-
grammes ; Mrs. 8., in her turn, must go one
better, and so turns on an expensive caterer and
hired performers; consequently the impecunious
Mrs. G. retires abashed, and shunts her turn alto-
gether, and the long-suffering committee have to
stand tea all round on the off-Monday. Ever
since the Salon started, the determination of one
woman to outshine another has been the rock
ahead in the club’s course. Why not make a rule
limiting outlay—as is done at political elections —
and thus make the result of the struggle depend on
Taste, and not on Cash ?
“The Gay Parisienne No. 2, Ada Reeve, has
some new and original frocks which don’t quite
come up to the glitter of those of Ada No. 1. The
first-act fascination consists of rose-pink chiffon
and rapturous brocade ; another is gaily striped
like a sugar-stick ; the third unbuttons behind sud-
denly and mysteriously, and Julie Bon-bon emerges
as a‘coon’ in white with scarlet sash, and sings
the great hit of the piece, ‘ Susie Toosie,’ a haunt-
ing, tuneful ditty in the minor, miles ahead of the
ordinary, inevitable ‘ coon ’-song. Why must
there always be a love-lorn black-boy in every
show, now-a-days ? Comparing the two Gay
Parisiennes—they are very much alike—A.
Willoughby was the more ‘gay,’ but A. Reeve is
the more ‘ Parisienne.’
It must be remembered that Ada 11. created
the part, whilst Ada Willoughby just copied and
improved upon the business of Ada 11. The “Gay
Parisienne ” was much observed at the Anglo-
Ranji Victorian Cricket Match in a blue gown
with terraces of biscuit-colored lace running down
it, a blue hat with a paddock of cornflowers in it,
and a blue silk parasol. The shrug of the shoulders
acquired for the purposes of the part has developed
into a mannerism. Ada shrugged all over the
grand stand reserve.
“Compare dear little Julie Bon-bon and her
Frenchy skirts (at the Princess) to the bloomered
Mayor’s daughters, shapely ankles and all, and
confess that old mother Eve chose wisely, when
confronted with the responsibility of fixing femi-
nine fashions for all eternity, she preferred petti-
coats to pants. Of course, there are skirts and
skirts, and, for that matter, pants and pants.
Place before your mind’s eye, for instance, a
clothes’-line, grotesque with bifurcated flannelette
monstrosities, and at the same time recall Julie
Bon-bon in a whirling upheaval of black and
cerise. Alas, sisters ! between such as these there
is a great gulf fixed. Somehow, Mons. Auguste
at the Princess makes one think of a Jap. more
than of a Parisian; but Japs, are called the
Frenchmen of the East, aren’t they ? So no doubt
the family likeness is O K.
“At time of writing, Melb. girls were still cricket
mad. Thanks to glorious weather, the M.C.C.
grounds really looked gayer, more frilly and
feminine, on match-days, than Flemington of
late. For that matter, lots of race-clothes cropped
up again, beautified by sunshine, which showed
up the colors, flushed the wearers’ cheeks, and
brought out the coy tints of their hair. Besides,
feminine, cricket-enthusiasts—as a class, pretty
young girls, daintily rather than magnificently
dressed—are fully as ornamental as your typical
she race-goer, the smart society matron, strident
of voice as costly of toilette. Needless to mention
the hero of the hour! The local ‘Nicest,’ bless
you, are not half democratic enough to copy
English crowds in pet-naming their idol ‘ Ranjy.’
You hear ‘the Prince’ on one side, and ‘the
Black Prince' on t’other, till you feel bored.
“ Why do women go to a cricket-match ? Fifty
per cent., go to see the sights, and as every second
woman is a ‘sight’ in the eyes of her critical
sister, all are gratified. The other 50 per cent, go
to be seen by the cricketers. The attendance of
women at the M.C.C. ground during the four
days of the big match was enormous, and the
cackle ! —it would have savaged a penguin, and
that is the most patient and philosophical creature
I know of. After a casual survey of a non-
descript flannel-clad figure at the other end of the
ground: ‘ ls that the Prince? Isn’t he nice?’
‘ Yes, dear, there is something different about
him. Blood dors make a difference, you know.’
And it was Harry Trott after all! Later on :
‘My ! isn’t that a little fellow at the sticks now ?
Does he countone, I wonder?’ Wouldn’t Graham
feel pleased ? Again, when Ranji. was batting :
‘ Fancy the dear Prince playing all those men by
himself ! ’ ‘Yes, awfully clever, isn’t he ? ’ Then
of the umpire : ‘ Oh, that poor man in the white
coat; he has been fielding all day.’ ‘ I wish the
Prince would do some of his tricks.’ There was a
continuous prattle of artless gabble more or less
akin to the above all day and every day, and on
the third day tired Man went with his ears bulging
wadding.
“ ‘Cricket spreads ’ were a feature of the meet-
ing. Promoters seat scores of acquaintances side
by side so as to be on hand later on to discuss
befrilled poultry and strawberries and cream. But
to pic-nickers in full war-paint, what is lunch
without a table? Not a bad line, sometimes, to
lounge on the grass in a washing frock eating
sandwiches and drinking Billy tea. It needs
nerve, however, to sit bolt upright on a tight-
packed bench wrestling with jointless ehicken-
bones, stiff sugar-tongs, blunt fruit-knives, Sec.,
swept fore and aft by the critical glances of
peckish outsiders, while spilt fruit-salad and ex-
plosive ginger-beer go hugger-muggering all over
your best things.
“ Among the many ‘Naicest’ left out of the
Brassey ball, the maddest probably is an all-im-
portant dowager who has lately enlivened her
home by the society of thickly-gilt young persons
anxious to be introduced into the Inner Circle.
Her first charge speedily bestowed herself and
nuggets on a scion of a tony local clan. A suc-
cessor was promptly placed in the bride-elect’s
vacated shoes by trustees able and willing to pay
for the chaperone’s good offices, and boundlessly
trustful of her social omnipotence. Then
descended that bolt from the blue on the heiress
and her surprised clan.
t “ Elocutioniste Nellie Veitch, lately back from
’Ome primed with new professional and dress
notions, tripped round the Salon on last re-
ception day, pretty as a picture, in white Eton
costume, blue vest, and headpiece of bright
turquoise straw. Miss Louie Morell, smart
in her Flemington get-up of pink and black
muslin with Toreador to match, gave samples
of her vocal quality, and the Summerhayes
sisters duetted agreeably as usual on violin
and piano. The young Bananalander, 1 Syd-
ney M. Sharwood,’ now Cambridge fresh-man
whose prize long-jump and hammer-throwing
were recently chronicled by cable, is ‘MacShar-
wood,’ old Brisbane Grammar-school boy and only
brother of pretty Mrs. Steve Fairbairn, of Melb.
Young Sharwood and his sister, the ethereal belle
aforesaid, who looks literally sylph-like by con-
trast with her tall, sinewy, swarthy husband (one
of the ‘ handsome Fairbairns ’ and himself a Can-
tab athlete in his day), are natives of the hottest
district of Central Queensland.
“The Southern dailies which predicted a mar-
vellous meteoric shower for Saturday last, are re-
sponsible for the shocking midnight row between
two St. Kilda families, whose respective sons’
and daughters’ extraordinary method of viewing
the heavens after 12 o’clock was misunderstood
by the parents.
“The Age is annoyed with Lord Richard Neville.
That long, lean streak of aristocratic good gracious
wouldn’t let Syme’s weekly Leader do what it
liked in the way of illustrating the fancy dress
ball, so it is writ of him : ‘Lord Richard Neville,
it seems, entertains extraordinary ideas as to
the duties pertaining to the position of the
Governor’s private secretary.’ But Dicky is
never tired of telling people that he was once
secretary to Lord Cranbrooke what time that
nonentity was Lord President of the Council,
so (he thinks) he knows all about it. Besides,
if Richard Plantagenet Neville, with a pedigree
as long as his legs, is to be talked to by a man
with an inky finger, what is the world coming
to ?
“ A local paper describes the Government House
costume-ball as ‘ a well-bred crush ’! It certainly
was a crush.
“Dr. Hilda Gamble, M. 8., 8.5., of
Melbourne University, will, on 17th Nov., be
wedded to Dr. James B. Buchanan, of Collins-
street, Melbourne. Miss Freda was the first
woman resident medical officer in Melb. Hospital,
likewise foundress of the Queen’s Hospital for
Women, where women are doctored by women,
and base bipeds in trousers are anathema.
“ The guns of the disbanded Rupertswood bat-
tery are now placed one on each side of ‘ Cliveden’
street-door. ‘ Our bruised arms hung up for monu-
ments.’ How the new wine of sassiety loves to
pour itself into the old bottles of aristocratic
customs—and it keeps tame spiders to spin cob-
webs round the bottles too, as a guarantee of good
faith.
“ Melb. City Council is going to by-law the
flower-selling youths off the streets. These have
been numerous, but little or no nuisance this
spring ; and the cause of the crusade is, of course,
the reasonableness of their tariff compared with
that of the swell ‘By Appointment ’ shops. It’s
all the difference between Id. and 6d. for a button-
hole, 3d. and Is. or Is. 6d. for a bunch, with
quantity and quality in favor of the kerb.
“ A successful actor, at present in our midst,
ascribes his nether loveliness to a youthful diet of
brose and haggis. Yes, like nearly everyone who
noses his way to the front, Adonis turns out to
be one of Rab Tamson’s bairns. Years ago he
tried his prentice hand on Australian audiences,
but he was less brawboned then and, small as was
his r6le, he failei to find favor with local lessees
and managers. Now his ex-employers, the planets
of other times, have paled, and the star of the
glum Scotch lad of yesteryear ‘ twinkles like a
diamond in the sky. ’
“ Gov. Brassey edits the paste-cutting-book
dept, at Government House. References to him-
self are marked by a menial. The Old Man
of the Sea goes through them, and puts in a
heap those he desires to have preserved. A
glance at this book gives the impression that
people love and adore His Ex., for the shy
references to vice-regal ‘ closeness ’ and prosiness
which find their way into certain papers have no
place there : such things form an apocrypha ; the
true gospels are the gushy and obsequious bits.
Gov. Hopetoun used to put all sorts into his book.
He rather liked stories—and particularly pictures
—against himself, being a good-humored chappie.
“ The present inmates of Govt. House (includ-
ing Capt. Wallington, gazing aloofly from the
background) have souvenired the current gala by
a huge group photo, as libellous, in print at all
events, as pictorial productions of the wholesale
type usually are. Lady Brassey in the near dis-
tance bends over her child, as broad as it’s long,
in a white smock. His Ex., cross-eyed for the
time being, also guards the innocent, while
amiable, peaee-at-any-price Gov. Hampden, dan-
dles Freeman-Thomas, junr., on his fatherly knee
hard by. Most of the other ladies involved are
represented as minus waist, and the men’s
moustaches and eyebrows (but not their ears) have
got lost somewhere in the process.
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.
Chief Justice Sam Griffith has just com-
pleted his codification of Q. Criminal Law. A big
job—and performed, they say, well. Griffith, in
his preface, professes himself surprised that this
great branch of law “ has not been reduced to
writing in such a form that any intelligent person
can understand it.” What a sentiment to emanate
from lips forensic!

An ex-police constable has become proprie-
tor and editor of the Blayney (N.S.W.) paper.
The Bulletin hopes he will succeed, but “ run-
ning in” advts. is a very different matter to
“ running in ” drunks.
Protectionist-leader Lyne represents the
Hume in N.S.W. Parliament. His mother’s
maiden name was Hume. Now someone named
Hume, down Albury way, proposes to stand
against him at next election.
The tapping of the Reid Ministry in sup-
Eb of a successor to J. P. Abbott in the N.S.W.
akership has begun. Already the names of
Id, Farnell, Copeland, M‘Court, Ewing, and
M‘Millan tap, tap at the Ministerial door.
Amiable and modest B. E. Minns is meet-
ing with deserved success in the big smoke. He
has just illustrated a story of Marie Corelli’s, and
is so satisfied with things in general that he has a
good word to say even for the London fog.
Admiral Pearson, who succeeds Admiral
Bridge in a month or so, has family friends in
Sydney. Their reminiscences of him, so far, take
the direction of setting him up as an all-round
sport, of colossal physical proportions—only 44
years of age.
Primate Smith, who returns to Sydney
with the rank of Archbishop, will, it is mooted,
deserve an addition to his income to balance the
new dignity. As it is, his income just touches
.£3OOO per annum, but close on .£lOOO of this goes
in more or less voluntary diocesan gifts.
There was much anticipatory licking of
lips in Sydney Hebrew circles when it was
rumored that a certain undischarged bankrupt,
who had “ got into ” them for a round sum, was
Mi the list of beneficiaries in a very “big” will.
But all the gladness passed out of Judah when it
transpired that it was the bankrupt’s wife who
was the legatee.

petitive feeling in local
newsgetting is a point in
connection with the Syd-
ney press which strikes
journalists acquainted with
the fierce press rivalries
elsewhere. For instance,
it is on record that a new
leporter not long from
Maoriland happened on a
piece of specially i m portant
information whilst doing
“Government rounds, ” and
published it. Next morn-
ing his editor, noticing that
the item was not in the
rival paper, sent for the er-
porter and said in a tone
that contained more of sor-
row than of anger : “ Mr.
, how comes it that
this does not appear in the
Graphosnooze ?”

Williams, of N.S.W. Per-
manent Force, was so-
journing with Learmonth,
of Groongal, he deter-
mined to take the tusks
of a huge wild boar that
was known to be on Old
Br ingagee, and sallied
forth one morning to the
fray, with his host, both
armed with freshly-shar.
pened harpoons mounted
mi poles. While engaged
in sticking less important
pigs, they got separated,
and Dr. Williams sud-
denly found himself con-
fronted by the big beast
he was in search of, which
went for him vigorously
on the spot. The fright-
ened horse jumped about
•ill the burly surgeon
dropped his lance, and
things were beginning to
look very serious when Mr.
Learmonth dashed up and
fclled the brute with a thrust behind the shoulder.

Many years ago a leading Melb. morning
paper imported an editor—clever, but impetuous.
And he was not an abstainer from the Fiend. He
closed up a brief but brilliant career by writing an
article in which he dealt with the views of Dr.
Cairns, head of the Presbyterian kirk, in relation
to Cremorne Gardens, then run by George Coppin.
It was hot. And it wound up thus: “For our-
selves, we would sooner go to hell with George
Coppin than go to glory with Dr. Cairns.” He
went ’Ome shortly after.
The rich man Payne, who recently died at
Melbourne, was once travelling in a railway-
carriage in which Aspinall, Ireland and Dawson,
barristers, were also passengers. Payne carried in
his hand a bag of shrimps. He fell asleep.
Aspinall annexed the shrimps, and he and his
companions ate them—putting the heads and tails
back into the bag. At the end of the journey
Pay ne awoke, and took up the bag, looked into it,
and said, with a glance around : “Lawyersagain !
Nothing left but the shells ! ”

King O’Malley has been giving S.A. jour-
nalists a spell of late, but he woke up the other
day. Said the “Lightnin’ Streak” in S.A.
Assembly:—“ I ask the House not to act like
midnight, skulking, famished, Texas prairie dogs,
barking at the imaginary ghosts of their departed
ancestors.” Which recalls Paddy Coglin, a long-
since deceased S.A. orator, and very fond of jaw-
breakers. One hot night while S.A. Assembly
-was debating a dry-hash Bill, Coglin suddenly
rose. “Mr. Speaker ! This noble measure needs
seriously meditative cogitation ! We must place
all the comprehensive arguments in juxtaposi
position ! ” Members sat amazed.

the claim of William Cresswell, so-called, to the
Tichborne baronetcy, is said to derive its going
power and justification from the fact that a will of
Cresswell’s, antedating his lunatic period, is in
existence and favors several influential private
persons. Aha!
Efforts are at work to induce Cardinal
Moran to accept an official position in N.S.W.
Local Option League. Some years ago he was
formally offered the vice-presidency of that body,
but now even the presidency is at his option.

G. Gibson Millar, the Australian million-
aire contractor, who revisits Australia per
“ Ophir ” has had two cabins knocked into one so
that his voyage may be more agreeable (writes
a Bulletin correspondent from London). He
says he has been suffering dreadfully from aortal
aneurism. His friends say it’s imagination, but,
anyhow, he looks very ill.
The late Johnny Sheehan, of Maoriland,
was credited with a sincere and Catholic (but not
Roman Catholic) admiration for the ladies of the
Maori race. At one of his meetings he was
heckled about his supposititious amours. “Turn
out the dug,” yelled Chairman Brodie, pointing to
the interrupter. “ He’s not a dog,” said Sheehan
with twinkling eye, “ he’s only a bit of a wag.”
Reading Gavan Duffy’s
articles in the Dublin Free
man’s Journal, the writer
is reminded that he was
once sent to Creswick to re-
port a speech by O’Shanassy
—then in Duffy’s Ministry.
It was a time of great politi-
cal agitation. The young
Democracy was feeling its
feet, and kicking—much to
the enragement and indig-
nation of the oligarchy of
the day. The speech in
question was of the highest
importance ; so that, when
the reporter got to Creswick
and found that the meeting
was over, he began to think
out the philosophy of suicide.
The other scribes had got
down their reports, and
grinned, of course. Reporters had
no mercy on each other in those
days. However, there was “ a show
for it.” Drinking down a soul-stiffener
of brandy, the scribe walked boldly
into big John’s bedroom. The great man
was asleep and snoring. The reporter awoke
him—by prodding the bed with a stick. He
sat up suddenly, and revealed a broad bosom of
long black hair that would not have shamed
a gorilla. Then the explanation and appeal.
“ Well, now, if that isn’t cool! ” said John ;
“but go ahead.” And then and there he re-
delivered his speech with such emphasis and energy
that he had an audience in the passage outside
drawn from every part of the hotel. “There ye
are, ye parseverin’ young divil,” said he ; “ be off
wid ye.” And that speech, when published, was
the boss speech—longer, better argued and con-
structed, and bristling with points that the other
fellows hadn’t got hold of. Big John explained
this by pointing out that he had “ slept on it.”
G.A.C. : Ernest Giles was the best pastoral
prospector that ever tramped round Australia,
but he never was a mining prospector or fossicker ;
hence the failure of his W. A. expedition.
One night “ Old Jack ” Robertson entered
Sam. Bennett’s office and slated the journalist in
characteristic style because of some remarks that
had appeared in the Empire that day. After
about five minutes of snort and scorching phrases
he turned on his heel and walked away. “ Well!”
exclaimed Bennett, taking off his spectacles and
turning to the writer, “ would anybody suppose
that man owes me a hundred pounds ! ” He lived
to get over his astonishment.
“ During the Autumn Newmarket meet-
ing,” writes The Bulletin’s London correspond-
ent, “ Mrs. Langtry was not permitted to enter
the club enclosure, thanks to steward Weatherby’s
influence, until Albert Edward interfered for an
explanation of her absence. The reply of the
stewards did not satisfy this staunch sticker-up
for his old friends, and he insisted on the old-time
Lily being invited to enter at once. The belle,
however, rang out a note of scorn and declined to
do so until requested in person by Lord Marsh,
when she, gracious in triumph, consented—and
the wigs of the female wire-workers shivered on
the green. ”
During last general election in Maoriland
a fierce battle was waged in one of the Southern
electorates. It was fought between an old Parlia-
mentary hand who had the support of Bung and
the Pagans generally, and a pious young man of
Highland ancestry, the bright, particular star of
the Cold Water and Bible - thumping cranks.
“Mac,” though blessed with many “saxpences,”
worthily upholds the economical traditions of his
forbears. His enemies say he would go to a pub.,
order a room, have a wash, take his lunch from
his bag and eat it, drink some water, and leave
the publican— his blessing. And that good young
man still wonders why he didn’t get in. The
aggregate beer-curse that was thrown after him
by the publicans of his electorate would have sunk
an apostle.
Geoffrey Eagar, at one time a figure in
N.S.W. Assembly, once referred to old Jack
Robertson’s taste for whisky. “We all have our
likes and dislikes in these matters,” retorted
Jack. “/ like a glass of grog—the hon. member
doesn’t. He finds his solace in sucking-pig and
cabbage.” Eagar, a teetotaller, had the reputation
(like so many of the “ temperance ” brigade) of
being a huge eater.
At the recent celebration of the golden
wedding of the Prussian chief executioner (heads-
man) there was a festive gathering of man-
butchers from all parts of Germany, and the
Emperor was on hand, as usual, not only with the
inevitable congratulations but with a “Jubilee
marriage medal.” Which surely ought to cause a
bad slump in the value of William the Mounte-
bank’s felicitations.
John Charles Crook Strange, who died at
Arncliffe (N.S.W.), the other day, was the
younger son of Frederick Strange, the Australian
explorer and naturalist of some 50 years ago.
Frederick Strange christened Mt. Stanley and
Mt. Acheron (M.L.) in 1849, the former after the
Bishop of Norwich, a friend of his. Soon after he
was killed by the blacks off the North Queensland
coast. His widow still lives and is a resident of
Sydney.
Kangaroo: “Met H. G. Wells, novelist
of the weird, the other day. A small, quiet man,
with spectacles; full of questions, incessant,
irritating questions on all sorts of Australian
subjects ; an insatiable, ‘ rapaciouS greed for in-
formation. Amongst his friends he is known for
his caricatures, and, had he failed as a novelist,
could easily have made a reputation as a ‘ black-
and-white ’ man. His drawing is admirable, and
his subjects as various as his ideas for novels.”

“ A Christian policeman does more real
good than any other member of the community,”
says Superintendent Read, of N.S.W. Police.
The Super, files his own bill for piety and police-
manship. He was the religious man of the 40
Londoners who emigrated from England to be a
nucleus for the N.S.W. force, and for close on 40
years he has supported his consistently pious repu-
tation.
Many jingles of PhilipD. Lorinier, who died
at Rook wood Asylum last week, are sung in shear-
ing-sheds and other way-back parts of Australia.
One reason for this is that he liked to pick some
melody in use and write his words into it. Lori-
mer really loved Nature. At one time he took up
his abode in a cave in the Blue Mountains so that
he might uninterruptedly commune with her, but
hunger and rheumatism drove him out.
Tom Garrett, old Jack Robertson’s hench-
man and once N.S.W. Minister for Lands, was an
effective speaker. But he sometimes fell into
inept illustration. “1 don’t value the slander a
hair of the Speaker’s wig ! ” he once exclaimed
with a fist-bang on the table. “ Ordah ! Ordah ! ”
cried Arnold, the Speaker. “I desiah to remind
the honorable membah that the Speakah does not
wear a wig, but his own haiah. At the same, time,
the honorable membah must know that he refers
to a symbol of authority and dignity in a very
offensive and derogatory mannah ! ” Arnold was
drowned in a flood of the Hunter. He was a fine
fellow.

[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
The first man lynched in California was
an ex-convict constable from Tasmania. I re-
cently spent an evening with an old Queenslander
who knew him, and was present when he was
handed. His name was Jenkins, and before
leaving Tasmania he induced a ticket-of-leave
man to give him his money to invest. This man
was to be removed as a stowaway on board a brig
bound for ’Frisco. Jenkins got rid of the stow-
away by showing him to the water-police when
they came on board to make the final search ! A
large part of the trust-money was spent by
Jenkins on opossum-rugs, which my informant
saw in Jenkins’s shop in ’Frisco. Jenkins was
caught “red-handed” robbing a warehouse, and
the mob hoisted him two hours afterwards. There
was a Rhadamanthine spirit of justice in that
rough tribunal of diggers. They offered to value
his property, and give him the price of it and a pas-
sage anywhere he pleased, if he would reveal his
accomplices. Jenkins thought they were joking,
and said they “ could have his property for hang-
ing him ! ” The mob then offered him a chance
to make a will, and undertook to see it faithfully
administered. This offer was also declined, and
the order being given to “run him up,” he was
hoisted accordingly on or abou<b the 14th of March,
1850.

Some time ago a citizen of a Q. coast-town
entered the shop of a local bookseller, who showed
him some old books that had been stowed away
on one of the top shelves. One of these books was
devoted to “Prominent American Criminals,”
and contained a lot of photographs—among others
a faithful picture of the local doctor ! Here,
indeed, was a discovery. An investigation by the
Medical Board followed, with the discovery that
the “ doctor ” had got in on a dead man’s diploma.
When the Board confronted him with the photo-
graph he coolly observed, “ Oh, ye 3 ; a cousin of
mine who closely resembles me! Damn that
fellow, he’s always getting me into trouble ! ”
Later on he disappeared trackless into the blue
immensity, to be finally heard of in the South as
“ medical adviser” to a firm of quacks.

History repeats itself in a thousand ways :
The new is old, the old is new,
The cycle of a Change sublime
Still sweeping through.
The popular Assembly in ancient Rome was called
Comitia Curiata, or “Congress of Curias.” To-
day, in Australia, each unpopular Assembly or
Legislative Council is a “ Congress of Curios.”

The prevalent advent in Queensland of
unexpected surprise-party babies is easily ex-
plained by ascientific factor of which the long-
eared multitude seems to live in Bceotian ignor-
ance. I refer to parthenogenesis. In partheno-
genesis we behold a brief return of the uni-sexual
period, or the union of both sexes in one, a pheno-
menon still witnessed in snails, worms, ascidians,
and many plants. Hence innocent virgins be-
coming innocent mothers to the astonishment of
the credulous ignorant, and the scandal of malig-
nant prudery. Any person who ignores this ex-
planation must be classed amongst those one-eyed
sceptics who are a blighting curse in all ages of
mankind. t t t 9

I n the days when Lieut. Bligh s native
police were hunting the blacks in the streets of
Maryborough, Q., and shooting them in the river
from the principal wharf, a half-dozen of the
fugitives took refuge in a loft over Norton and
Brown’s store. They crawled in there unseen by
anybody, and remained until near daylight. In
that loft at the time were ten barrels of blasting-
powder ! Next morning the storekeeper saw
where the blacks had been striking matches and
knocking the'Ved-hot ashes out of their pipes on
the ends of the powder-barrels. When this bo-
came known all the residents who lived close tc
the store retired to wipe off a cold perspiration !

Scene, a town west of Rockhampton. A
j a dy who had successfully run a shanty for about
two years was captured at last and brought into
town on a spring-cart, accompanied by all her
confiscated liquor. The case was tried before two
local squatters to whom the priestess of Bacchus
had been like “ Spain’s dark glancing daughters—
angelically kind.” The Bench sternly observed
that justice must be done though the heavens fell;
also, that sly-grog selling was assuming gigantic
proportions and must be stamped out with a strong
loot. To emphasise the Rhadamanthine severity
of their intention the Bench fined the lady two
dozen of beer, and acquitted her without any extra
stain on her character. At nine o’clock that night
one 0 f the two magistrates rapped gently at the
alab walls of the lady’s boudoir according to
special invitation. The lady whispered through
a crevice asking him to “call a little later when
the other visitor was gone ! ” The “other visitor ”
wa s the other half of the Bench ! The second
gentlemen retired to meditate on the deplorable
uncertainty of all human calculations where a
woman is concerned, and firmly resolved also to
make the fine not two dozen of beer but three
months’ hard on the next occasion. As I have
this narrative from the favored visitor himself its
authenticity is guaranteed.

he’s a little compos mentis 1 ”
MAROOGALINE.
A Smile across
the Shoulder
FOR THE BULLETIN
I strike my flag to Maud
When the mashing spirit moves her
A fickle little fraud
Her treatment of me proves her.
The one day blowing hot
The next day blowing colder,
But she lands me on the spot
With that smile across the shoulder —
Deadly Maud,
With her smile across the shoulder.
Her form lacks supple grace,
Nor is stately as Diana’s ;
She’s freckled on the face
And vulgar in her manners;
No syren of the town
Hath an eye that’s any bolder,
Yet she brings her pigeon down
With a smile across the shoulder—•
Tricky Maud,
With a smile across the shoulder.
The earnest, honest gaze
Of young and artless Beauty
Whioh walks in Virtue’s ways
Obedient to duty,
Stirs not my passion-fire
Whose embers merely smoulder,
Till I’m quickened to desire
By that smile across the shoulder —
Sad to say,
Just a smile across the shoulder.
Though freely I assert,
In stern, emphatic diction,
Disliking for a flirt
The statement is a fiction.
I look at Maud, and long
In tight embrace to hold her,
When she comes it very strong
With her smile across her shoulder—
I’m undone
By that smile across the shoulder.
I used to like them coy
In days, ah, long departed,
When I was but a boy,
And somewhat simple-hearted ;
But now I’m tough and stout—
Turned fifty, maybe older —
The girl who’d lay me out
Needs to fire aoross her shoulder.
That’s a fact —
She must smile across her shoulder.
Melbourne.

Chairman Krichauff, of S.A. Agricultural
Bureau, finds that the best trap for codlin moths
is a pickle-bottle containing a quarter of a pint of
syrup and a tablespoonful of boiled apples or apple
jelly, suspended on the slant on trees. The moths,
attracted by the smell of the apple pulp, get stuck
in the syrup, and there depart this life.

lately waited on the Premier to demand the
abolition of gambling would have refused a ticket
in Tattersall’s sweep if it were offered to her free.”
Which is probably true, if the ticket was offered
to her when the other W.C.T.U. female wasn’t
looking.
A recent investigation shows that of 335
girls, with degrees from Girton College, 123 are
teachers, 2 are missionaries, 4 are medical
students, 6 are dead, and the rest are living at
home, dependent on either parents or husbands.
Again, from the matrimonial point of view, out of
79 Girton girls who graduated with honors in
mathematics, only 6 are married; of 97 with
classical honors, only 10 are married ; of 47 with
honors in natural science, only 7 are married.
The numbers at Newnham have very similar pro-
portions. Evidently, the base British male taketh
not the blue-stocking to his bosom with much
fervor.
Mr. Frank Senior, who has been chemist
to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cures instantaneously, is
very palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is perfectly
effectual, no matter what the cause of the head-
ache may be. This cure, the result of years of
experience, can be had in 3 sizes, by post 1/4, 2/6,
3/8. Senior, chemist, George-street, Sydney.*
The same old story. A young Melbourne
woman came home to her mother, the other day,
with a damaged countenance and a wound in the
back of her head, and a graphic account of how
she had been outraged by three young men in a
right-of-way. The police were appealed to, and
the “ victim ” conducted them to the scene of the
assault. Subsequently she furnished a full de-
scription of the Missing Three, and yet the police
were not astonished w'hen a Melb. Hospital
doctor, having examined the “victim,” declared
she was telling lies. The doctor’s report appears
to have scared the lady, for she presently admitted
that she had been telling lies. The excuse of vir-
ginal hysteria couldn’t be put forward in this ease,
seeing that the fair tarradiddler was a divorced
wife.
Be Moonblindness : Dr. Bull (Norsk
Magazin for Lagevidenskaben, No. 6, 1896 ;
Deutsoh Medizinal Zeitung, Aug. 19, 1897)
relates the case of a sailor, previously healthy,
who, together with two comrades, had to sleep all
night on the ship’s deck, off the coast of Hon-
duras, in bright moonlight. When they awoke
early in the morning not one of them could dis-
tinguish the ship’s yards, and they could hardly
make out her bridge. This state of things con-
tinued for two months without interruption, when
the man who was the subject of Dr. Bull’s
observation lost sight of the other two sailors. On
his return to Europe the man consulted a
physician in Kronstadt, who prescribed cod-liver
oil. He thought his vision improved a little after
that. It was not until seven years later that Bull
saw him. There was then slight conjunctivitis,
and the whole visible surface of each lens showed
numerous little points that were bluish-white by
reflected light. Besides, there were fine stripes
radiating from the centre of the lens. Bull is con-
vinced that the impairment of vision was occa-
sioned by the moonlight.—X.Z.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

A Miner ;
Let me draw your attention to the fact that prisoners
have to pay their own fares from Coolgardie to Fremantle;
distance a trifle short of 400 miles. Case in point: A
few weeks ago a young man who had been sentenced at
Coolgardie to three months’ imprisonment, was informed
by the police sergeant that the cost of his conveyance to
Fremantle would be deducted from the moneys belonging
to him, then in police possession, although, as far as I am
aware, there had been no order made to that effect by
the court. The necessary £1 17s. 6d. was accordingly
deducted from the money which the prisoner had directed
to be handed over to a friend. Perhaps it was right that
he should pay for his transit, but that he should have to
do so at the option of the police is another beautiful
sample of Westralian justice.
In Paris, any suspected food or drink-
article can be analysed at the expense of the
municipality, which will further, if necessary,
prosecute the offender, who, upon conviction, is
not only liable to imprisonment, but may be com-
Eelled thereafter to exhibit in his window and on
is shop-door for a longer or shorter period, a sign
“ Convicted of Adulteration.” The huckster sym-
pathies are too strong in British communities to
admit of so salutary and logical a style of dealing
with dishonest tradesmen. “A nation of shop-
keepers.”
If some of the Bismarckian anecdotes are
true, the “man of blood and iron” will be noted
in history for the things he said, rather than the
things ho did. One yarn has it that old Emperor
William, taking a long ride with his faithful
Chancellor, was somewhat irritated at his com-
plaining of fatigue. “How is it, Prince, that I,
an older man than you, am not nearly so soon
knocked up ?” asked the Kaiser. And Bismarck
answered, “ Ah, sire, the rider lasts longer than
the horse.” The sole authority for this story
would be Bismarck, anyway, and he probably
didn’t get off the epigram on the spur of the
moment. He only thought of it afterwards. Or
perhaps another fellow had the idea. A Chan-
cellor who has made history gets many plums of
speech put into his mouth by newspaper para-
graphists.
A Parmer’s Plain Letter.
North Laen, via Minyip, V.,
July 27, 1897.
“It gives me pleasure to testify to the efficacy
of Warner’s Safe Rheumatic Cure. Some years
ago I was afflicted with rheumatics in the legs,
which rendered me totally unable to do my work.
For six years I suffered in this way. during which
time I was under seven different doctors, but
without receiving any benefit. The flesh wasted
away from the legs; while the toes and ankles
were swollen to a most abnormal size. In my
extremity I was induced to try Warner’s Safe
Rheumatic Cure. I used in all twenty-one
bottles, but long before I had taken that quantity
I was greatly relieved, and that amount of medi-
cine did what all else I had tried had failed to do,
and lam cured as a result. I can now do my
work, and am a wonder to those who knew me
during my years of suffering.”
[Farmer) MICHAEL PENTLAND.
WITNESS C. B. Eastward, J. P.

Japs and North Australian Pearls.
A w*LL-informed N. Queenslander Bends The
Bulletin some loose-talk about pearl-shelling :
According to Govt. Resident Douglas, the diving fatal-
ities up at Thursday Island occur principally among
Japanese boat-owners.” This means that the Japs, form
syndicates, who rent boats from white storekeepers, who
call themselves shellers, but stay ashore, and every man-
Jap. on board each boat dives, whether he is a practised
diver or not. As each boat takes out only one diver s
license, the Japs, thus have a big pull on the whites who
dive for themselves. A diver can t keep below water for
very long, but has to come up to rest; much also depend-
ing on the state of the tide and weather. The Japs,
simply work all round ; as soon as one diver is pulled up,
another jumps into the dress and goes down They have
a corslet with a double arrangement to fit differently-
sized helmets, and they thus continually cheat the reve-
nue by working with unlicensed drvers.
What makes detection of this evasion difficult is that
each Jap. is more like every other Jap. thi J n “ e 18 “f 6
himself. They are worse than the collectively undis-
tinguishable Chinaman in this respect. An aiteration is
proposed to be made in the law, under whioh each diver s
license will contain a personal description of the licensee
—“ color,” “ color of hair,” apparent age to date, height,
distinguishing marks, eto., but the versatile dap. will
“ get there just the same.” Every Jap. is of the same
color as every other ; every Jap. has black hair, etc.
* * * *
Jap. divers risk their lives carelessly, partly because of
their fatalistic temperament and partly because they are
paid by results. The vogue is to ship these boat-renters
as “ shilling-a-month ” seamen, and give them a percent-
age of the value of the yield. Japs, will go down into SO
fathoms (!), and say nothing about it. So will Manilla
men, but these only stay down a few minutes and are very
careful, few deaths occurring among them. If a Jap. is
taxed with having been deep diving, he will strenuously
deny it, unless caucfht on the spot, and will Bwe ®£ by
his ‘gods that he has not been down more than 18
fathoms or so.
* * * *
Should the inspector of fisheries raid a boat while at
work, the diver who is hauled up will swear until his
brown face becomes white that he is the very Yokohama
to whom the inspector issued the license, and “ the devil
a bit of know will the inspector know, you know. Be-
sides, they are not so easily caught. For instance, when
they are on the raid for pearl-stealing—everybody who
gets the chance steals pearls in Torres Straits they have
a look-out man perched at the masthead, and if any
strange sail comes in sight they up-anchor and away.
* * * *
Pearl-stealing, in some of its phases, is a fine art. The
ordinary method is for the diver to raise small shell, that
is, shell under the legal size-limit, which used to be 6m.
but is now Bin. across the nacre—that generally gives an
outside measurement of 6Jrf>in. These shells are ripped
open and fossicked for pearls and then thrown over-
board, as they would be condemned by the Customs in-
spector ashore. White divers tell how they find lots
of “dead shell” thus destroyed at the bottom
on the pearl-oyster beds. Fine pearls are got m
small shells, though how many is kept dark for obvious
reasons. Stephen Pack, a scientific expert, who has been
engaged by James Clark and Co. to try and “cultivate
the pearl oyster, tells how he has found a good-sized
pearl in a shell only an inch in diameter ! G. Smith, a
practical sLJller in the same firm, declares that he found
a pearl worth £lOO in a shell under the 5-inch limit, and
a £2OO pearl in one a little over six inches. These
instances illustrate the incentive there is on the part of
wages-men to look for illicit perquisites. Pace says that
the pearl grows with the growth of the shell ; on the other
hand, a practical diver declares that oysters vomit the
pearls that grow too big and annoy them. It is remark-
able how dense is the ignorance, even among scientists,
of the habits and life of the pearl-oyster, but Jambs Clark
and Co. have agreed to give Pace £lOOO a year for three
years to try and succeed in cultivation.
Every pearling boat, and every man on board, and his
traps, are searched for illicitly-obtained pearls directly
they come ashore. But the searching is a farce. There
is nothing easier to be done than to hide a pearl except,
perhaps, the ease of selling it, although pearl-buyers have
to be licensed. Everybody on Thursday Island buys
pearls; everybody sells them; therefore everybody is a
wilful law-breaker. An agitation is now afoot that pearl-
dealing licenses should be abolished, as the law is held in
utter contempt. A license is £lO, and runs for a year
only. The futility of searching on shore suspected pearl-
stealers was illustrated by a witness, Hudson, before the
Q. Pearl Fisheries Commission a month or two ago ; he
told the Commission of a man who was “ known to have
pearls with him n being searched at the landing-place,
and how all his traps were ransacked, without result. The
E earls were all the time concealed in the soap with which
e was washing his hands !
• * * *
One of the dodges of pearl-shelling wages-men to get at
pearls without destroying the shell in cases where there
are no pearls, is to leave the shell exposed to the sun for
an hour or two. Then the oyster gapes and they examine
him. If he has a pearl, he has to be ripped and cast
overboard; if not, a bucket of water is thrown over him
In company with his mates, and he closes up again all
right. There is also said to be a practice among the more
artistic pearl-seekers, of not only exposing the shell to
the sun, but, if the oyster is obstinate in respect of his
calipers faculty, of tickling him at the mouth with a
stick, which seldom fails to make him yawn ! This state-
ment is, probably, somewhat of an exaggeration.
• • * *
It is, however, a curious fact, as regards Torres Straits
pearl-shelling, that few or no pearls are found in shell
raised by wages-men—that is, by Japs, and other colored
crews who go out alone ; but that pearls are plentifully
found in shell raised by white divers who do their own
diving ! If the boat-owner goes out by accident with his
diver, it is an observed phenomenon that the shell raised
contains many and fine pearls. As a fact in submarine
natural history, this deserves to be noted by scientists !
* * * *
The Japs, are getting the pearl-shelling industry of
Torres Straits pretty well into their own hands. There
are now only three or four white divers on Thursday
Island. Colored men superseded them, and Japs. squeezed
out the rest of the colored men. Three large firms do
nothing else in the shelling line beyond renting boats to
Japs., and getting round the law by shipping the crews
on the nominal wage of a shilling a month. They thus
run no risk and make big profits, but are surely giving
away the industry to the “ little brown man.” An attempt
is to be made to knock on the head this Ailling-a-month
artifice, and to conserve the fisheries to Queensland’s own
people, by legislating that no aliens shall be permitted to
own licensed boats or rent them, and that whites who
hire out boats to aliens shall be liable to penalties ; also,
that the wages paid to crews shall be set down in the
shipping articles. Aboriginal natives are to be allowed to
fish, license free, in their own boats and in their own
waters, but are to be restricted from working without
licenses for white employers. The Queensland Govt, has
recently made a treaty with Japan, under which the Jap.
Govt, has promised to discourage emigration to Queens-
land, and is trying to turn it towards Brazil and Mexico.
* * * *
The slave-trade shoots out a tentacle from the pearl-
fishing entity. Polynesians are actually indented—boat-
owners deny that their system is “ indenting,” but it is
practically nothing else—from Sydney and “ the islands,”
under no obligation on the part of their employers to
return them to the port of shipment or anywhere else.
There is no restraint on this, as the “boys” are not
actually “ engaged ” until they arrive at Thursday Island
—a somewhat barefaced method of cheating the law.
Thursday Island is now a pestiferous distributing centre
for Japs, who drift in crowds along the Queensland coast,
and bob up serenely in all parts of Australia. There is at
present not one naturalised Jap. on the Island; there
have been two—one returned to Japan and the other
went to Port Darwin, the other open back-door of Aus-
tralia for colored aliens. Not a Jap., therefore, has a
vote; yet the pearl-shelling industry is now being run
chiefly for the Jap.’s benefit, except in the cases of the
few big firms who make the “ little brown man " their
wealth-finder.

quiet, soporific little village in the county of Kent, sat
Rose Starling, a strikingly handsome woman of the
peasant class, gifted with a splendidly symmetrical, if not
altogether graceful, figure and a pair of brilliant black
eyes which seemed capable of either good or evil in no
Bmalle degree ; she appeared to be waiting for some one
whom she is more than anxious to see as is soon attested
by the sound of a horse’s steps pulling up at the very door
and the appearance of a pale, blast-looking man with an
habitually languid air, who, however, appears slightly
perturbed at the present moment as he advances and ;
clasps the woman is his slight, sinewy arms.” We now go :
out for a well-earned drink .T.C.D. : Received ;
Cornstalk : Thanks Mendo : The oft-repeated cry that
Supreme Court Judges are badly-paid because successful
barristers made more than they do in certain years, is
largely traceable to the unconsidered fact that the barris-
ters’ guild, out of which Judges are taken, holds its doors
so tightly closed against what should be legitimate com-
petition for the doing of legal work. The large incomes
certain barristers make would not fall to them with an
open or fairly-open Bar. Many of the best-paying cases
would receive, truly and in popular estimation, as good
treatment in the hands not now allowed to compete. But
it is alongside the incomes of these best-paid barristers
that Judges’ salaries are placed by those who cry out on
the latter’s behalf.

SOME SYDNEY MATTERS.


Apropos Col. Home’s report on the unre-
liableness of the western (N.S.W.) rivers,
ties indubitably show that reliableness has
been increasing in late years. The stocking
of the country first by cattle and then by sheep
has solidified the porous surface; sheep-pads
leading generally down to water, have served
as the beginnings of small streams, and cart-tracks
(also generally leading down to water and espeoi-
ally towards the sometimes navigable rivers} have
been, in course of time, turned into larger streams,
all adding to the flow of water from the country.
The average height of the Australian Rifles
(N S W.) is sft. as against the Imperial In-
fantry average of sft. 6Jin. The tallest N.S.W.
warriors are the Irish Rifles, who average-like
the Royal Irish Constabulary—sft. BJm.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Two politicians that the Vic. Bible-in-
schools crowd tried their hardest to wreck at the
recent election were Higgins and Gillies, and
their return was an awful smite on the nose for the
godly.
Ed. Bulletin, —The “ interesting prayer ”
you quote from Q. Wokkeb as being used by
Speaker Cowley to open every sitting of Q. Beg.
Assembly, is the prayer always used in the
Anglican Church all over the world, while Parlia-
ment is sitting. But Cowley should leave it
alone. —D.
A N.Q. town —democratic, of course.
Prominent residents (also democrats) lease their
f round to Chows for fruit and vegetable gardens.
! very body deals with the Chows, until an anti-
alien fury seizes the place, and the residents show
their disgust by dealing with John as before —but
refusing to pay him.
THE WAY TO GO.
STRANGER (to Cocky): “ Could you tell me the way to Thargorumbeburrah"?
COOKY : “ I can that 1 Yer go beyant there, thro’ the fince end down the lane
till yer come to where a house wus; but there's no sign there now.
Then through the scrub and over the ridge and you’ll hear a dawg
bark, and you’re there—if yer don’t yer lost, by jabers!”
Premier Kingston’s Gaining Suppression
Bill will probably be killed by its friends in S.A.
Assembly. Some holy members want to include
the suppression of the totalisator, and other drastic
provisions. 36 members —out of 54 —spoke on the
second reading !
Apparently the Tasmanian Ministry has
struggled through its troubles, and will go on
gridironing the West with syndicate railways a
little longer. Still, the defection of Attorney-
General Clark is a heavy loss, and if Clark—the
brightest man of a dull team —goes definitely into
Opposition with a vigorous policy the days of the
Braddon-Fysh Govt, will probably be few and
troubled.
The Glenelg railway, the only private line
in S.A., will probably be shortly acquired by the
Government. A. 3. \V. Government is beginning
to contemplate the purchase of the Moama-Demli-
quin line, and has just acquired the Rosehiil line.
The Great Southern, in Westralia, has also just
become State property, and, in one way and
another, private railway biz. is fast becoming
extinct in Australia.
An interesting law-point cropped up re a
forfeited deposit at the East Gippsland (Vic.)
election. Four garnishee claims to the ±5O were
put in by creditors of the unhappy candidate, and
one claimant argued that he was entitled to first
cut at the cake, inasmuch as he had a Supreme
Court judgment to go upon, whilst the other
creditors were merely third-rate applicants from
Petty Sessions. Whichever way they settled it,
there was not so much as a crumb of the fatty
left for the hungry Turner Govt.
Victoria was kind to Premier Turner.
Observe that, immediately upon returning to their
provinces with their new honors, Whiteway (New-
foundland) and Escombe (Natal) were defeated.
A high southern official to The Bulletin
from London : ,
I have had much consideration shown me here by
reason of my official position, but, speaking generally,
Australians are somewhat at a discount. It is a curious
thing, but I have not met more than two persons here
who profess liberal opinions, nor did I meet more than
three persons in Ireland who professed to be Home-rulers.
This does not mean that there are no Liberals or Home-
rulers, but it is not the lot of the ordinary visitor to find
them out. The Jingo feeling is excessively strong and
will lead to war one of these fine days.
Down in Albury (N.S.W.) district land
which the squatter bought or dummied not so
long ago from an easy-going Government at 20s.
or 255. an acre is now being let out by him to
agriculturists at as much as 10s. an acre per
annum. It was the huge Riverina pastoral estates
which Reid’s Land for Closer Settlement Bill was
allegedly intended to break up, and a decent
measure, without the fish-like odour of corruption
which hung about that bygone scheme, might
have done a lot of good down there.
N.S.W. country paper :
Currently reported in Sydney that Mr. Want will re-
sign the office of Attorney-General and that Lands
Minister Carruthers will take his place. If so, it will be
the first time in the history of N.S.W. that a solicitor has
been permanently appointed to the position of Attorney-
General, an office which, as it confers the leadership of
the Bar, is usually held by a member of the long-robed
branch of the legal profession.
Which is an error. James Martin (afterwards
Chief Justice) was a solicitor when first appointed
Attorney-General by Cowper in 1856.
Billy Maloney,
Yic. M.L.A., threa-
tens the introduction
of a Referendum Bill
at an early date, and
already is heard the
Tory cry against the
measure, instancing
the recent decision of
the people of Greece
to make war against
Turkey. Well, the
object of the referen-
dum is to give the
people of a country
what they want. The
Greeks wanted war,
and they got it.
Melbourne : Let
us rip out this word
Charity, and substi-
tute something like
Public Duty. Sweep
away all the humbug
of the religious side-
show, the pretence
under which the fats
of the Cloth get ob-
liquely subsidised.
The Rev. Mr. Treble-
chin gets his .£lO a
week because of the
gre-a-t work he does
among the poor, and
that same moan
about the poor is al-
ways the most effec-
tive mode of raising
the shekels. Now is

to show that Maori-

Although the erst-
while stalwart John
M‘Elhone is sadly
fain to admit that his
body isn’t strong
enough to let him again contest Fitzroy (Sydney)
electorate, warrior Chapman has the temerity to
think that his mind is equal to the task.
Premier Seddon made a wise departure
this session by simply dumping his printed
financial statement on the table of Maoriland
Parliament without reading it, and supplying
each member with a copy to digest at his leisure.
It saved two hours of dreary, hoarse stumbling
through a document which every member has to
read over for himself in any case before he can
remember what it is about.
The rumours of a big loan to be floated in
Maoriland this year have faded away. Still,
Seddon is dabbling in a small way with the un-
olean thing, and proposes to float a little quarter-
million—±2oo,ooo for railways and £50,000 for
education. The railway idea might possibly be
excused as a “ permanent and reproductive work,
but the idea of charging the education of this
generation to posterity is unvarnished swindle.
Australian papers are quoting, with the
remnants of their Jubilee enthusiasm, the latest
almighty say-so of cock-sure Kipling :
For the white man to explain that he is loyal is about
as unnecessary as for a respectable woman to volunteer
the fact that she is chaste.
Without a hard-and-fast definition of loyalty this
assertion is about as lucid as a sneeze. If Kip.
means by loyalty the first dictionary meaning,
“ devotion to a sovereign,” he is simply cheeky,
with the impudence of a boss prophet—he is a
self-constituted Moses slithering down the moun-
tain of his own conceit with the eleventh com-
mandment under his arm.
AT HER WORD.
“ Gam now, Bull Head, ma sea yer ’ave ter gimme
'arf yer bread."
BULL HEAD: “ Naw, won't. Yer jest said yer
wished yer was dead—and bread's ther staff
er life.”
Fitzgerald, M.L.C. (Vic.), who has been
’Ome, says Premier Turner has broken with the
Labor-party, because he finds after his Jubilee
visit that he has now two reputations to maintain
—one in England and one in Australia. But no
Australian politician has ever succeeded in main-
taining two reputations. It is much more than
enough for the average parliamentarian to main-
tain one. .
Queensland proposes to go into the r ede-
ration movement with the proviso that the
Kanaka Parliament shall have power to refuse to
submit the proposed Constitution to the people, if
it is one of which the Nelson-Tozer majority dis-
approves. The people who tried so hard to drag
the Piebald Land into the movement have done
Federation a bad turn. That province, with its
strictly Fat Man franchise, will almost certainly
send a crowd of Tories to the Melbourne Conven-
tion, to combine with the Westralian and Tas-
manian fossils in voting down every progressive
idea. Then having helped to spoil the prospects
of the other provinces, it will probably stand out
itself and cackle sardonically in the distance.
In 1894 Maoriland had 25,851 persons em
ployed in factories; in 1895, 29,879 ; in 1896
32,387; in 1897, 36,918. This is by far the largest
and steadiest increase in the Seven Provinces, and
Maoriland, in proportion to population, is now
the boss manufacturing state of Australasia. It
has attained this position almost at one jump
since it began to go in for “ advanced and
“grandmotherly” legislation—to regulate hours
of labor, enforce weekly half-holidays, look after
the sanitary condition of factories and workshops,
and generally harry the miserable employer off his
head and drive capital out of the country. _ Also,
it has got there under one of the most rigid pro-
tective systems in the Seven 7 /ovinces.
NO WASTE AND A GREAT WANT .
MAOK STIGGINS: “I gave you money yesterday,
my friend. Waste not, want not.”
TRAVELLER : “ But I swear I didn’t waste a drop,
mister, an’ no mam could want a drink more’n
I do this morning.”
Ed. Bulletin, —Speaker Cowley’s daily
prayer in Bananaland Parliament is a practical
bar to acceptance of the Speakership by scrupulous
persons outside the Anglican pale. Its arrogant
reference to “ Thy Church ” might, of course, be
construed by each sect (bar the R.C.’s) into a
special mention of its particular Holy of Holies,
but how are we to manage when we come down to
the sparse Swedenborgians, “ Beardies,” and the
“ other denominations ” of about one adherent or
less? Mention of “Our most blessed Lord and
Saviour, ” would surely stick in the
gullet of a Jew or Unitarian Speaker. Yet we
find persons of all creeds daily content to overlook
these insults so long as they are able to draw the
perquisites. —T.D.
The fact that a man like Graham Berry
worked close on 20 years without payment as a
member of Parliament, and took the risk of work-
ing all his life without payment, while fighting for
payment for his successors, should count for some-
thing in the discussion of his claims to a Parlia-
mentary allowance in his sere and yellow-leaf.
‘ ‘ There will be children in Hell for the
loss of whose souls this House will be responsible,”
Labor-member Price told S.A. Assembly the other
day in re the recent decision to charge all children
Is. a week for their schooling after they had
passed the compulsory standard, even though they
get there at 10 years old. Those whose parents
cannot afford to pay are forced on to the streets.
Dear Bulletin, The prevailing mis-
sionary zeal demonstrates that, though the
orthodox have practically long since abandoned
Christianity, they still consider it good enough for
the Chinese, Hindus, South Sea islanders and so
forth. No doubt there is wisdom in teaching
these inferior races to turn the other cheek ; also,
to lay up their treasure in heaven only, to forgive
their European enemies, to hand over their goods
after thev have been despoiled of their land, to
lend without interest to the European borrower,
and, in fact, to become good “ Christians ” all
round. The missionaries also have served their
purpose in facilitating the European conquest of
these countries, just as the overthrow of Rome by
the barbarians was materially aided by the pre-
vious introduction of Christianity to Italy.—
Democrat.
Britishah : “ Mr. Ranjitsinhji properly
sticks up for his fellow-subjects of Her Imperial
Majesty the Kaiser-i-Hind, in Australiah. He does
not bully, but takes the lofty stand which every
advocate of the English connexion is bound to
assume. Houng Eat and Sam Kee are aliens, who
may be legislated against anyhow, but Ghollah
and Chundah, as Imperial Britons, possess as muoh
right to have free course and be glorified in Aus-
traliah as anyone from England —that is to say, on
the assumption that Australiah belongs to England.
My gorge rises at the disgraceful way in which
Beak Panton, of Melb., hectors the hawker
Afghan, Sikh, or Pathan. Lo, the poor Indian !
Our flesh and blood. Anyone would think he was
Bill Sikhs, instead of a glorious tawny Tommy
Atkins, who has borne our magnificent colors in
the Panjab. Ranji will do incalculable good by
sticking to his new text. ”
Premier Kingston’s S.A. Gambling Sup-
pression Bill is a beautifully comprehensive slab
of moral snuffle. Penalty for betting with a
person under 21, fine of from =£lo to .£lOO or
imprisonment up to six months. Receiving money
for betting purposes from persons under 21, fine
of from £2O to ,£lOO, or same term of imprison-
ment. Promoting sweeps, =£loo or six months.
Being a private totalisator-agenr, .£2O to £lOO or
six months. And the Bill also gives the police
Eower to forcibly enter and search suspected
ouses and arrest suspected persons without war-
rant, and they may impound any articles sup-
posed to be used for gambling purposes, the onus
being laid on the accused to prove that they are not
so used. Which tremendous clause would pro-
bably justify them in seizing the table and leaving
the family to eat its meals on the mantelpiece,
till it could satisfactorily demonstrate that the
impounded piece of furniture was not habitually
used for playing penny-nap upon.
S.S. : “Sickening to bear the yapping
and snarling of some of the Melb. ’trades
Hall councillors and certain Labor mem-
bers at the heels of Trenwith. Bilious
spite is apparently the one motive —spite
that is the outcome of envy, selfishness,
and narrow political creeds. Trenwith
has climbed patiently and laboriously,
he has been true to the principles he
has professed, but because he can find
no policy in fish-market vituperation
and melodramatic posing for the delec-
tation of the ‘ push,’ he is hissed as a
traitor, and hounded as an enemy to
the Labor cause. It is counted
against him with fine scorn that
he aspires to office. How could he
serve his party better ? What could
Ballance have done for Democracy
in Maoriland as the leader of a mere
faction ? It is Trenwith’s game to
play for office, and whilst he re-
mains what he is in heart every
Radical should help him on.
Meanwhile, his enemies evinoe
their loyalty to the working-man by pulling
hideous faces at the bourgeoisie. Labor may have
a score of men who will abuse the enemy for
one who will use him.”
Premier Reid’s straight and honest and
strictly truthful statement that N.S.W. doesn’t
need foreign mining-capital has brought down a
perfect shriek of obloquy on his capacious head.
About 47 people rose simultaneously to ask how,
in the name of heaven, N.S.W. could work its
mines unless they were owned and managed by J.
Bull and the dividends went to England, and
what might not happen to the helpless Cornstalk
mining infant unless he was held up by the British
investor. The Bulletin opines that the Corn-
stalk, unless he is a hopeless fool, or a cretin, or
something of the kind, could struggle through on
his own, just as the mining men of Bendigo, Bal-
larat, Gympie, Charters Towers, and other places
did for so many years with good results. If the
people of N.S.W., after 60 years’ experience, don t
know how to run their own mines, then it is time
they were compelled to learn, and so long as they
lean in abject weakness on Bull or any other out-
sider they never will learn.

Public Notices.
PUBLIC SERVICE BOARD.
50 Young-street,
Sydney, 29th October, 1897.
A COMPETITIVE EXAMINATION of Candidates for
JUNIOR CLERKSHIPS in the Public Service of the
Colony will be held in Sydney and at various country
centres, which will be fixed as far as possible to suit the
convenience of candidates, on the 20th DECEMBER next,
and four following days. The estimated number of
appointments to be made is 25. Candidates must be not
less than 16 nor more than 21 years of age on the first day
of examination ; of good moral character, and in sound
health. Persons already employed in the General Division
of the Public Service will be admitted to the examination,
provided their age does not exceed thirty years.
The subjects in which the candidates will be examined
are :—Handwriting, English (dictation precis writing,
grammar, and composition), Arithmetic (full course),
Geometry (Euclid, First Book), outlines of Physical and
Political Geography, and the Geography of the Austral-
asian colonies in moderate detail; English History from
the middle of the eighteenth century to the present time,
especially with reference to the colonies and depen-
dencies. Candidates will also be allowed to take any
two, but not more than two, of the following subjects :
Mathematics, English—a prescribed author —(the same as
that prescribed for the Junior Examination of the current
year—Tennyson’s “ Gareth and Lynette ” (MacMillan),
Latin, French, German, Elementary Chemistry, and
Elementary Geology. Application to attend the examin-
ation must be made on a form for the purpose, which can
be obtained at the office of the Board, and must reach
the undersigned not later than the Ist December, 1897.
Candidates will be required to pay an examination fee
of 10s., which must be lodged with a clerk of petty sessions
or the Treasury, and the receipt therefor forwarded to
the Secretary of the Board with the form of application.
A list of registered candidates, with centres of examina-
tion, will be published in the Government Gazette after
the time for the receipt of applications has expired, and
candidates will also be advised direct of the place at which
they will be examined. The regulations and copies of the
papers set at the last examination can be obtained at the
Government Printing Office and the principal booksellers.
By order of the Board.
9a3i J. W. HOLLIMAN, Secretary.
