Philip D. Lorimer, the well-known writer
of homely bush songs who died in Rookwood
(Sydney) Asylum on sth Nov., was a brother-in-
law of P. N. Russell who lately gave .£50,000 to
Sydney University.
The projected re-issue of the Antipodean
magazine was nearly falling through, the other
day, for want of advts. to the publishing guaran-
tor s minimum ; whereupon Geo. Essex Evans
one of the editors, whirled round Brisbane and
collected over <£loo worth in a few hours. The
first recorded Business Poet ?
One of the things that make the shriek-
sister take herself seriously is the poodle-parson—
like him who recently told a W.A. woman’s fran-
chise meeting that as he had eliminated the
obedience-clause from the marriage-service, no
woman married at his church was bound to obey
her husband.
Attention is drawn in the advertising
columns to the fact that the financial year of the
A.M.P. Society is drawing to a close, and that the
date when the usual yearly investigation of its
affairs and distribution of its surplus will be made
is at hand. Intending assurers are reminded that
a bonus for the year 1897 will be allotted to every
participating policy effected not later than the 31st
December next. The amount of cash bonus dis-
tributed amongst the participating policy-holders
for the single year 1896 was no less a sum than
.£428,797. Persons who wish to avail themselves
of the benefits which membership of this well-
known society confers, and who desire to become
participants in the profit earned in the current
year, will do well to make application at the Head
Office, or any of the District Offices, or consult
one of the agents of the society without delay.*
Australian Meat.— The only permanent
hope for Australian beef and mutton values is the
export trade, and to maintain this the world’s
markets must be vigilantly scrutinised. Pitt, Son,
and Badgery, Ltd., are endeavoring to keep this
colony’s end up by the Cape and Westralian trade,
without losing sight of other channels, both better
and less known. Their assiduity may also dis-
cover new practical outlets. *
Minerva Tonic, which has been introduced
to Australia by a London and New York com-
pany, comprises in its formula the expert medical
knowledge of the world. This tonic enables a

doctor would charge guineas for. The value of

doctors of Australia, and it is being testimonialised
by people whose social character and reputation
make their testimony invaluable as a guide to
others. The Minerva Company does not give talk;
it gives proofs. And it offers £lOO to any person
who can prove that any of its numerous testi-

no more.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The result of the Sandhurst Cup race, run Nov.
24, is likely to be very much under the influence of Tatt’s
sweep on the event.
r • • • •
W. R. Wilson has won a number of big races,
but his bad luck with good horses is proverbial. They
seem to go wrong in the feet, or the temper, and the only
St. Alban’s “ crack ” that has “ trained on ” of late years
is the expatriated Norman. At the present time backers
are cursing the waywardness of Manfred and Majestic,
and wondering whether Aurum’s cracked hoof will soon
go wrong altogether.
* ■ * *
At a recent Australian race-meeting widespread
surprise was expressed at the continual companionship
throughout the day of the judge and a big owner, inside
the box as well as outside. Some of the decisions also
caused much astonishment. It was a nice, bright day,
and clearer still was the fact that the judge enjoyed
being in the sun. The big owner and his particular pals
didn’t do too badly on that day, either.
* « * *
Bookmakers are honest, in that they scorn to
dissemble their awful prosperity. The impoverished
punter has the real state of affairs thrust under his notice.
The fingers that grab his “quid” are heavy with expen-
sive rings ; the voice that calls the odds is lubricated with
champagne in public places. The blessed Truth stares
the punter in the face. On the Monday afternoon pre-
vious to Melb. Cup, bookmakers Sam Allen and Charley
Westbrook rode around the Collins-street Block on a
couple of tall horses, the observed of all observers.
* • * •
Bathurst, last week, gave trotting one of the
few shows it gets in N.S.W. Mr. Buekland was repre-
sented by Cling and Burlington, Junior; Dr. Slate by
Narragarisett and Silver Cloud ; Mr. Fitzgerald by Huon,
Junior, and Lady Nutwood; and Mr. Knight had as sole
representative Harold 8., a rather slab-sided Childe
Harold horse, who has been raced for a couple of seasons
without success, being looked upon as a bit of an outlaw.
The fine little track on the Bathurst Show Ground was in
tip-top order, and Harold B. won the three minutes trot,
the 2.45 trot, and the exhibition for speed, style and
action ; in the latter rattling off half-a-mile in lntin.
18sec., and fairly distancing Dr. Side's Silver Cloud.
Harold B. moves more like Fritz than any other horse in N.S.
Wales, and Knight deserves much credit for his handling
of the horse, which, before it fell into his hands, had
beaten every man who had sat behind it. The Handicap
was won by Lady Nutwood, but the handicapping was so
bad that it robbed the event of all interest. The Maiden
Trot fell to Burlington, Junior, an enormous pacing
stallion by Burlington 8., and he ran the final heat in
2min. 47sec. » -
*%* * *
At a recent race-meeting for the benefit of
Kiama R.C. Church, Father Heydon’s horse, Turko, fell
and badly injured his jockey, hight O’Dwyer. Lot more
give-and-take about R.C.-ism, after all, than about any
other sect. Imagination can’t possibly run to “ the Rev.
Duncan M‘Parritch’s horse, Tonald, ridden by Sandy
Buchanan.” at,
* * * *
It was understood, at time of writing, that a
“ further enquiry” into the Parthenopseus scandal would
end in bookmaker Sam Allen giving tha V.R.C. a sworn
declaration that he didn’t back old Par. for the Final
Handicap, but merely saved it in his volume. Why not
call upon the Ring in general to state who did pile the
money on the horse when he won ? And how about the
Spring Handicap, for which Parthenopseus started at an
outside price and ran “ nowhere ” —did Samuel and
partner Arnfield, save their horse on that occasion ?
• * * *
The public should be grieved to hear that Mr.
Forrester was laid £3OOO to nothing against Gaulus by the
drawer of the horse in Tatt.’s big sweep. Any one of us
might happen to draw a horse in a Cup sweep, and the
horse might win—and why, in the name of Turf Morality,
should we be expected to pay Bill Forrester, or anybody
else, £3OOO in consideration of his good luck ? The con-
ditions of these big handicaps are not properly stated in
the Racing Calendar. It ought to be duly set forth that
the Melb. Cup has £3OOO added by the V.R.C., and several
other thousands added, under pressure of fear, by holders
of sweep tickets.
* * Vf * *
An impromptu set-to between a couple of pugs
took place in a Melb. pub. last week, consequent upon one
splitting the other’s lip open while his hands were in his
pockets. An adjournment was made to the nearest ring,
where a sharp knuckle-fight under otherwise Queensberry
rules occurred. The battle consisted principally of the
man with the split lip belting hunks off the aggressor—
who has by farthebiggerreputation.andwhoultimatelygot
out of a nasty hole by remarking that it wasn’t his game
to fight for fame. The other reminded him that he
should have thought of that when he started the row, and
proceeded to give it to him “ lying down,” the upshot
being an agreement to settle the question with gloves at
an early date. The prospect of an honest go, with bad
blood between the parties, ought to raise a crowd big
enough to fill the Exhibition.
* * * *
On the handicaps Walne and Kellow should
have splendid chances in this year’s Austral, and it would
be no surprise if the first and second prizes of ’97 went to
the same men as those of ’96. The men have reversed
marks this time, Kellow having 30yds. and Walne 20yds.
start from Englishman Barden on scratch. Ahead there
are such men as Tebbutt, Reynolds, Porta, Middleton,
and Walker on 40yds., so that there is first-class pacing
at their disposal. The enormous number of entries also
have scattered a number of riders on every mark, so that
in no heat will there be a break of more than 15yds.
Barden’s chance on a flat grass track is very uncertain,
but on recent form Walne holds Englishman Green safe
on the same mark. Judging by the finish in the five-mile
race at Melb. Hibernian sports last week, it is doubtful if
he does Kellow, and of the cracks entered these two seem
to have the best chance.
Both the five and ten-mile scratch races at the
Hibernian sports aforesaid fell to Walne, though Kellow
was beaten by only a tire in the shorter distance, in addi-
tion to which he protested vainly against interference on
the part of the Bananalander. The ten-mile race was
noticeable for the run-away conduct of Johnstone
“ No. 1,” who headed the field by 100yds. on two or three
occasions. In the final rush Megson came round.on the
outside to smother the field, but Walne carried too many
guns, and finished a length ahead, with Kellow third.
The cycle racing at St. Kilda,;iast Saturday,
was remarkable principally for the return to form of Ken.
Lewis, who, keeping to the training and dodging the
asthma, smothered Walne, Megson and Kellow in the
run home of the Five Mile scratch. In the mile event he
also ran well, finishing third to Walne and Kellow s first
and second. Walne seemed wanting in strength, and
was all out in the finishes of both races as well as in the
Pursuit Race, where he failed to come any nearer than
third. Megson and Kellow' are riding splendidly, but
they seem to have lost their old trackmanship, both
allowing themselves to get into execrable positions at
critical moments. Jackson, the Newhaven of the last
Austral, rode very well, coming second in the Pursuit
Race.
The Pursuit Race was the first, and is probably
the last, of the sort Melb. will ever see, for it was duller
than a yacht-race. Eight men (all the tip-toppers, with
the exception of the Englishmen, who were in Adelaide)
started at intervals around the track, and dropped out
one by one when overhauled, until only Hunt, Jackson,
and Walne were left in. Jackson was coming strongly on
Hunt when the bell rang for the expiration of the 7min.,
leaving the Maorilander the winner with 2 miles 1648yd5.,
and Jackson 198yds. behind.
The handicap races were notable for the splen-
did finishes, eight men finishing within a blanket’s length
in one heat, and the times put up. The middle-markers
did best, and the big race fell to O. Lober —over 200 in
the two-mile event, which he turned off in 4min. 29sec.,
or equal time to Kellow'’s Austral.
* * * *
Wally Kux, the Melb. road-cyclist, broke the
Adelaide-to-Melb. record, last week, in a characteristically
German manner. Kux made out a schedule of all the
townships on the route, with Snell’s time at each,
knocking a few minutes off every one, and timed him-
self to reach Melb. in 52 hours. So carefully did this
stolid Teuton adhere to his arrangement that he arrived
at the G.P.O. in 51hrs. 48min. and some odd secs., and
that after taking tw'O hours’ sleep he had not provided
for. Kux, on arrival, pedalled straight to the German
Club, where a select crowd of fellow-countrymen toasted
him with lager and sang “In der Schmertz” in chorus
until breakfast-time. Snell's previous record stood at
61hrs., and Kux’s before that at 69hrs. Snell is now pre-
paring t.o vanquish his vanquisher, and apparently the
game will continue ad infinitum.
n • * *
“Oarsman” to The Bulletin The N.S.W.
Rowing Association, in presenting the “ Pennant ” with
the “ flag” (indicating the premiership of ’97), decided to
hold a dinner in celebration of the affair. Not a single
man was invited to attend this function—the winners of
the championships being required to pay in order to re-
ceive the trophy which they had won I This strikes me
as an absurdity previously unheard-of in any branch
of sport. The “crew” had resolved, in order to dis-
courage such paltriness, not to attend the dinner, and
only on the advice of the Sydney Rowing Club Committee,
which held a meeting on the subject, did they buy tickets
for the affair, which took place at Aarons’ Exchange
Hotel last Saturdav evening.
* " * * »
Stoddart, in the match with Vic., was not even
a suggestion of the batsman he used to be.
A nauseating feature of the England v. Victoria
match was the tendency of a section of the crowd to
yell itself hoarse every time Ranjitsinhji touched the
ball in the fipld. Soft ’uns that the average small boy,
playing with a kerosene-tin wicket, could take with ease
were made the subject of fulsome applause and cries of
“ What a wrist he has I” “ Wonderful 1” &c.
* * * *
The crowd can’t complain of Storer as a show-
man. He appears to revel in unnecessary work behind
the wickets, and he chases the ball whenever he has a
chance to get there half a second sooner than another
man. Also, in batting he is ever on the alert to steal a
run. A fearless, aggressive-nosed, quick-witted fellow,
who will probably “ come off ” more frequently than any
of his mates. The new men among the English pros, are
mostly fair-haired, Hirst being quite the typical Anglo-
Saxon —short and thick, with a pair of “ beautiful blue
eyes,” and the covert glances that, as a rule, fetch
responsive dittos from womankind.
* * » •
Acritioof the English men’s Vic. “order of going”
might want to know why shouldn’t the sturdily self-confi-
dent Storer be a first-man in—that is, when Stoddart wins
the toss? Storer is a rugged, rapid, and fairly safe batsman
who puts the ball in all sorts of places, and, to an outsider,
it seems that a run-getting wicket-keeper should always
open the proceedings, in order that he may have all pos-
sible chance of a long rest before putting the gloves on.
By the same token, he should be reserved for a middle
place in the second innings. Hayward and Stoddart,
judging from their form at Adelaide and Melbourne, will
seldom prove useful batsmen “ on the pinch.”
If the general opinion of Hinglish barrackers
were taken.it would be found in favor of reserving Stoddy
and the Surrey man—cramped beginners both of them—
for the time when the attack has lost some of its original
keenness. A Bulletin correspondent from Adelaide
classed Hayward as the soundest bat in the team, although
he made only 6 runs. His Melbourne results were 7 and 5,
made in an unhappy, over-anxious manner which pre-
pared the crowd for his downfall on each oc-
casion. Cricket prophecies re new men on strange
wickets are dangerous things to attempt, any-
way. The present writer merely predicts a huge
financial success for the Stoddartian tour, weather
permitting. The Englishmen-cam-Ranji promise to
play a lively game, and when they happen to get
plenty of luck they will make a fine scoring display.
}* * * *
Bruce, 88, in his old form—“the Australian
Ranji ” —wrist cuts and leg glances, and lightning leg
pulls. The Indian Ranji watched William with evident
interest.
* * * *
Worrall’s 83 runs, in the second innings of Vic.
v. Stoddarc’s Eleven, were scored at the rate of somewhat
less than 20 to the hour. It’s a poor show for the multi-
tude when a batsman gets “ set ” to such an awfully slow
tune. They might as well have Alick Bannerinan back
again.
* * • *
Scraps of feminine cricketology in M.C.G. re-
serve at England-Vic. match. “ That man in the white
coat and dark trousers is Stoddart, dear.” It was
Crockett, the umpire. “ You see they have to hit the
ball up and down between the goal-posts.” “ Why do
they say a man is ‘ run out ’ when he walks out ?” “ Oh,
the batsman gets them (the bowling-screens) moved to
show where he wants to hit to, and then the fielders have
to stand away from them.” “ Isn’t Storer cunning 1 He
sits right down on his heels so that the wicket will protect
his face.”
* * • »
Dear Bulletin, — lie bowler Jones, you say
“ nearly every bowler whose action has been questioned
in past days had a slow delivery.” True. And many
cricket-lovers in Melbourne are asking “ What about
‘Micky’ Roche’s action, especially when he’s putting in a
slow one?” Several times during the recent match with
Stoddart’s team it was, to put it mildly, suspicious.
There was a final jerk of a bent arm which made the de-
livery, at least, open to doubt. We saw it from many
angles, and with and without glasses. Roche is again an
M.C.C. pro. while here, umpire Phillips an ex-M.C.C.
pro., and umpire Crockett a present M.C.C. pro. Did
any of the visiting team think in silence ? Has anyone
an opinion to offer? Isthe “ clever throw” creeping in?—
Bails.
* * * *
When Giffen’s Australian team went to the
West last year the players—most of them Australian
Eleven men—agreed upon a fixed amount they were each
to demand this year in the Stoddart matches, but it is
stated that some of the men, when separately interviewed
by Wardill, went back on their confreres. This is one of
the items in George Giffen’s comprehensive grievance.
* * * *
The glorious uncertainty of cricket—and um-
pires: In the England v. N.S.W. match Iredale was
caught behind wickets off a ball which he afterwards
admitted having just touched, but was given “ not out.”
His dismissal was from an l.b.w. appeal, confirmed by the
umpire, when he really played the ball on to his pad.
• • • *
The N.S.W. men should undoubtedly have made
more of their chance on the first day of the match with
the Stoddartians. They had the bowling beaten, the
wicket was very fast, and boundary hits came easily, and
yet they made only 303 runs. The visitors’rate of scoring
next day was nearly 30 per cent, faster.
• • • •
The fact of young Trumper being sent first to
the wickets in the Sydney match against the Englishmen
caused much argument in cricketing circles. Many
thought it a grave error of judgment to send the lad in
first; others were of opinion that it was better for him
to go in and have it over than to sit and wait for his turn.
A vexed question is—Should veteran Garrett
now stand down for younger men, despite his fine second
innings? Unfortunately, Garrett is one of the selection
committee, and his playing only bears out The Bulletin’s
old contention that no probable player should have a
place on a selection committee. “No man is a competent
judge in his own cause.”
From all accounts, it was touch and go as to the
inclusion of Donnan, whose 104 pulled N.S.W.’s first
innings out of the fire.
The spectacle of the crowd cheering Ranjit-
sinhji is deplorable. The unfortunate “ prince ” cannot
show himself, or do anything, or do nothing without the
crowd applauding. Really, does not the Australian
crowd deserve the contempt that the Englishmen feel in
their hearts for it ?
Not alone in the Trumper incident was Gar-
rett’s captainship provocative of endless criticism. Some
people said his captaining was as bad as his catching ; but
this probably goes too far. The biggest howls certainly
arose from duffers who couldn’t have done half as well;
but a knowledge of that fact—if they know it —made no
difference to the crowd. There’s no medium in a cricket
critic who has backed his judgment with a shilling at the
gates ; and he makes no allowance for weather, or luck,
or the chapter of accidents. Play is either “ brilliant ”or
“ rotten "—mostly rotten. And one miss wipes out the
memory of a hundred well-fought matches.
Friday’s play left N.S.W. at 5 wickets for 303.
By this time every man in Australia but the lonely bush-
man sitting down to damper and spotted ‘‘goanna”
knows that the next 5 wickets added only 8
runs. For the collapse the weather—and the bats-
men—accounted. The rained -on pitch was tricky ;
the bowlers were fresh ; and down went the wickets.
Richardson’s third ball delivered Kelly into Storer’s
hands ; Coningham—O Pyjamaland I—retired for 0 with
a gentle tip to the bowler; Mackenzie’s wicket suddenly
lay down, and refused to get up again ; a black arm ex-
tended like a semaphore and clutched Garrett; and
Howell allowed M’Kibbin to carry out his bat. Half the
Eleven lay peacefully under wet turf, with a neat little
tablet erected to their memory by Hearn e and Richard-
son.
The Englishmen, who had fielded anyhow and
nohow, and bowled for the most part merely somehow,
did rather better with the bat. Their innings totalled
335, or 24 more than the local men made. M’Laren’s
slashing 142 was its feature, and when Iredale at last held
him relief shone visibly on every perspiring N.S.W. figure-
head. McKibbin’s bowling was worse than Noble’s, and
Noble’s was none too good ; Howell and colt Trumper—
who dismissed Hayward with a splendid catch —got little
show and none respectively. Storer played a good 84.
N.S.W. did worse second innings than first, and
could only tot up 260; whereupon the Englishmen came
with a rush and carried off the match by eight wickets.
Ranji. gave a taste of his quality for the first time in Aus-
tralia and made 112 (not out). The wicket played more
like a billiard-table than a cricket-pitch, and when Ranji.
and Maclaren got together they simply beat the bowling,
Maclaren running up his even hundred before Noble
knocked his wicket down. Mason joined the Indian,
but only scored four when Noble found his weak spot.
Hayward then took a hand with Ranji. and the pair
played out the game in the interests of the durned
Britisher as aforesaid. Ranji. played a faultless innings,
and justified the hatsful of gate-money he has drawn to
the ground.
* * * •
The Sydney Wheel Race, value £3OO, was won
by W. J. E. Elliott, on a Beeston-Humber fitted with
Dunlop tyres, and the 10 miles international scratch race
was captured by J. Megson mounted on a similar machine
supplied by the Austral Cycle Agency. The Humber
machines also scored Ist, 2nd, and sth places in the
League’s annual road race, Parramatta to Penrith.

Vic. Government has decreed that wit-

Apropos night-watchmen, it came out quite
accidentally in a recent night robbery case
that the jute goods in a certain free store had for
months back been nightly utilized as beds by
numbers of night birds, to whom the thrifty
watchman charged 2d. a night.

The Centennial Park, Sydney, a legacy
from the Centenary celebration period, has cost
N.S. Wales to date £224,902 3s. 4d. The sales of
land and other returns in connection with the
Park from 1891 to 1897 have realised £O3O 3s. 7d.
Old man Parkes promised a handsome profit in
three years ! Still, the noble park is worth all the
money spent on it.

From S.M. Herald :
Ballina, Thursday.—An inquest was held yesterday at
Rous on the body of a man named Johnson, who commit-
ted suicide by cutting his throat to-day.
When it was proposed, in a certain N.S. W.
town, to buy a chandelier for the Town Hall, the
Teutonic Mayor said : “ Veil, here ve haf a biano,
und ve can’t afford to keep any von to blay it; zo,
if ve puy von zhantelier, who vill blay dat ? ”
i Dear Bulletin, — lie filthy dairies, etc.
Have you ever seen a cream-separator cleaned
after it has been running an hour or two? On
opening, the plates are covered and the bowl filled
with a horrid greenish-grey slimy mass which the
creamery-manager usually feeds to hiss poultry,
who eat it greedily. Under the private dairy
system of non-separated cream, most of this filth
goes into the butter ; which doubtless accounts for
so many bucolics preferring dairy-made to factory
butter. —F.C.
Panton, P.M., in declaring that a wife’s
adultery does not exempt a husband from pay-
ment of maintenance, has raised an outburst of
dissent among Melbourne lawyers. Yet he is
right, and has the Vic. Full Court with him. By
a decision not a year old the tribunal named de-
cided that a man is liable to a maintenance order
until divorced, but, at the same time, it reduced
an order for 7s. 6d. a week, made at North Melb.
police court, to a farthing a week. The gleeful
defendant rolled up to court and, planking down
a shilling on the clerk’s desk, said “Here’s just
about a year in advance.”
Premier Kingston, as the result of recent
deputations from the W.C.T.U., &e., recently in-
troduced a drastic liquor bill into S. A. Assembly.
Any person now found in an hotel on Sunday un-
less a lodger or a traveller is “ deemed to have
committed an offence,” so that the pious Adelaide
citizen who steps into the vestry of a pub. to
ascertain the whereabouts of the nearest church is
deemed to be intoxicated and riotous, and may be
arrested by the nearest policeman. The police
can also enter a pub., search it, and arrest every
person therein at any time without a warrant.
Apparently the only device left to the Adelaide
man with a dark-brown Sunday-morning taste in
his mouth is to enter a pub. and search it with a
policeman, and arrest the nearest bottle of beer on
suspicion of felony.
“ 6 x 8 ” on an industrial subject :
Dear Bulletin, —Do you know what “ etc.” stands for
in the following sort of daily-paper advts. : “ Wanted,
youth for station, to milk, ride, etc., ss. a week.” For
the edification of Westward-bound Sydney youths, I’ll
tell you. This kind of much-advertised-for youth does
little riding or milking on a station. He might have to
milk an ancient cow or two, and once a day ride a more
ancient station crock to run ’em in. His main five-
shillings’ worth consists of “ etc.,” viz., getting up at day-
break (4 a.m. in the summer-time), cutting kindle-wood
for house and kitchen, lighting two or three fires, shining
from six to a dozen pairs of boots, peeling potatoes, clean-
ing knives and forks and all brass-work about the home-
stead, and feeding fowls, pigs, dogs, and pet sheep. After
breakfast, his day’s work of “ etc.” begins in real earnest.
He has to clean the verandah and yards and lawns ; then
wash a buggy or two, grease a few sets of harness and two
or three saddles ; and, if he’s smart, he might get a spell
before dinner, i.e., nursing a baby or wheeling a peram-
bulator about. After dinner, he helps the cook washing
up, cleans some more knives and forks, and passes time
till evening by cutting and carting wood, weeding the
garden, watering flowers, hunting for hen-eggs, grooming
ladies’ hacks, unpacking stores, killing sheep, and pump-
ing and carting water for the laundress. After tea, more
washing-up, taking clothes off the line, or perhaps turning
a mangle for an hour or two.

Mr. Button writes later:-“The Pills I had from you
were of great benefit to me and others. In one case,

you that, having to pass their camp next day, I found

THIS Company is being formed for the purpose
of acquiring the property of the Cobar
Mining Company, No Liability, comprising the
following leases on the Cobar Goldfield :
aggregating a total of
98 acres O rood 38 perches,
also, a Freehold grant of 100 acres, adjoining the
above-mentioned leases, favorable for a mill-site,
and containing a dam capable of holding about
three million gallons of water. The purchase also
embraces the plant and machinery now on the
mine, including three batteries, each of five head
of stampers, a Huntingdon mill, together with
pumps, boilers, engines, &c.
The object of the Company will be to work the
large auriferous and cupriferous deposits contained
in the leases on an extensive and scientific system,
to which purpose the works and machinery neces-
sary will be erected. The property has been care-
fully examined by a number of the most competent
experts in Australia, who are unanimous in their
appreciation of the intrinsic merit of the mine, as
will be seen from their reports hereto appended.
The main lode of the mine, which has been well
described in the past by no less an authority than
Mr. H. C. Callahan, manager of the Lake View
Consols Mine at Kalgoorlie, as “ the Mother Lode
of New South Wales,” is one of the largest in
Australia. Under the old company it was worked
for gold, no copper having appeared on the sur-
face, whilst the gossany slates were richly
auriferous.
With depth, however, occurred a change in the
character of the ore, the gold becoming asso-
ciated with copper and other metals, from which
it could not be readily separated and extracted by
amalgamation. The New Company will, there-
fore, treat the ore on an entirely different prin-
ciple, and, under advice of the most capable
metallurgists, the whole system of extraction will
be altered. The ore will be smelted ; and the
working capital has been calculated with a view
to the erection of electrolytic works, should the
management deem them advisable.
The success of another property upon the same
lode, the Great Cobar Copper Mine, is a fitting
standard for comparison. That mine has a
remarkable historical, as well as geological, simi-
larity to the Cobar Chesney. Both on the same
lode, in one of the most gifted of the mining dis-
tricts of the colony, they had previously been
closed down, after years of comparatively ineffec-
tive working, for identical reasons—the heavy cost
of treatment, the paucity of general facilities, and
the enormous expense of transporation prior to
the construction of the railway. The loss of the
gold values in the Great Cobar, owing to the high
proportion of copper, and the refractory nature of
the ore in the Cobar Chesney, were the immediate
causes for suspending work in the mines. The
former has since been reopened on behalf of the
tributors with immense success.
The reports which, with plans and sections,
accompany the prospectus, are the expression of
seven of the most careful and capable mining
managers, engineers, and metallurgists available
in Australia, and that they are unanimous in
their appreciation places beyond doubt the in-
trinsic value of the mine. The authors of the re-
ports are :
Mr. William H. Trewenack, General Manager
of the Lake George United Mines, New South
Wales.
Mr. Louis Janin, Junior, author of “ Amal-
gamation of Free Milling Gold Ores,” and other
mining works, one of the discoverers of the
Cyanide Process, and whose metallurgical and
mining work in America has placed him in the
same rank as Dr. Peters, Junior, to whom the
present position of Mt. Lyell mine is largely due.
Mr. Thomas Longworth, General Manager of
the Great Cobar Copper Mine, New South Wales.
Mr. Randolph Adams, late Manager of the>
Broken Hill Central Mine, and well-known Copper
Mining Expert of America.
Mu. J. E. O’Gorman, formerly of the State
Mining Bureau of California, and General
Manager of the Cobar Gold Mines, Limited.
Mr. G. A. Stockfkld, one of the pioneers of
Western Australia, and recently leader of a
mining expedition through South Africa, in con-
nection with the British South Africa Chartered
Company.
Captain Henry Cock, formerly manager of
copper mines in Cornwall, and of gold mines in
Bendigo.
Reports.
Mr. WM. H. TREWENACK, Manager of the
Lake George Mines, Limited, writes: —Some months
back I made an exhaustive examination of the
property, not with a view of making a report for
flotation purposes, but to assure myself of the
value or otherwise of the mineral deposits within
the Company’s property. Its surface indications
are strongly marked, inasmuch as they are gos-
sany, having a long length and great width. The
gossany ores were worked for free gold formerly
by the Company, then by tributors, and the
average recovered value from data furnished me
was equal to 7dwts. gold per ton. This, however,
did not represent the true value of the ore won
from the several shoots of ore followed down, for
I made a complete sampling of the tailings at the
mill, the assays from which were certainly astonish-
ing. Apparently the recovery had not been more
than CO to 65 per cent, of the absolute value of the
ore. I consider there are large quantities of this
class of ore, and immense reserves of lower grade
still left standing in the upper workings of the
mine. Since no copper or excess iron are shown
in the upper workings, the most suitable means of
recovery should be —(Ist) by milling the crude
stone through a modern American type mill;
(2nd) the tailings should then be subjected to
Cyanidation.
At 150 ft. from the surface I noticed stains of
copper shown in the form of carbonates. Seeing
this led me still further in my pursuits, and red
oxides were then noticed. At 260 ft., or there-
abouts, ohalcopyrite, coated with rich black sul-
phides, were seen, assaying high for both copper
and gold. At 360 ft. a crosscut was run west ex-
posing a vein 32ft. to 34ft. in width, carrying on
an an average 5 per cent, copper and 3rlwts. to
4dwts. gold per ton. A large portion of this vein
matter can be easily and cheaply dressed by con-
centration. The more massive sulphide portion
can be broken down at the point of the pick, and
so be sent to the blast furnaces direct, thereby
saving handling and further surface costs. The
concentrated portions can be put in such a form
that no one need have fear of meeting with any
blast furnace difficulties, and the ore can be so
regulated by concentration, that—after some little
experience has been gained by the millmen —suffi-
cient silica shall be left in the ore to form its own
silicious flux for the furnace work.
Taking into consideration the enormous width
of the lode formation, length of ore body shown,
and the important change that has taken place in
the deep workings of the property, copper ore
having been developed in payable quantities asso-
ciated with gold, one could come to no other con-
clusion than that the Chesney Mine will become
one of the largest and constant producers within
the Cobar district. To gain this object, however,
shaft-sinking and general exploration work should
be pushed ahead as fast as possible. The owners
need have no fear of the ore bodies in the deeper
workings. The gold will carry down in the
copper ore, and in all probability these ores will
become more massive as depth is attained. With
good economical management, and with skill
shown in the metallurgical treatment of the ore,
this mine can be made to pay handsomely from
the start of operations.
The Mine, 3rd November, 1897.
Mr. LOUIS JANIN, Jun., Mining Engineer
•and Metallurgist, reports as follows :—I may pre-
face my report by saying that I am highly pleased
with the prospects of this mine, particularly with
its resources as a copper-mine. I am fully of
opinion that this property will be one of the first
three Copper-Gold Mines of New South Wales, if
taken over by a Company which cah supply the
necessary large working capital and worked under
intelligent management.
That the Chesney Mine was not recognised to
be a copper mine at its start is not difficult to see.
In many mining districts, to which the Cobar
District is no exception, several classes of ore
occur, each on its own horizontal plane, and each
to a certain more or less defined depth. In the
case of the Great Cobar Copper Mine, which is
nearly 200 ft. lower than the Chesney Mine, the
soft gossans were eroded, leaving the rich car-
bonate ore belonging to the second zone exposed
on the surface. The Chesney Mine still has these
gossans, which, as in the case of the Cobar Gold
Mines Limited, Fort Bourke Mine, show no
copper for a considerable depth.
As in the Great Cobar Mine, the vein in the
Chesney is wide, but of varying width. These
walls will average over 100 ft. apart. At the No.
3 Shaft the width between these limits is over
200 ft., with auriferous gossan exposed on the sur-
face, over 150 ft. in width. These gossans have
been cut at the 100 ft. level 115 ft. wide. At the
No. 1 Main Shaft the lode is 87ft. wide. Of
course, in such large ore bodies masses of mullock
or waste rock are bound to occur, but, with due
allowance for this, these ore bodies remain among
the greatest I have seen in copper-bearing lodes.
The Great Cobar Mine shows over 75ft. of solid
ore in its 540 ft. level, and, judging from the sur-
face and underground developments of the
Chesney, there will be a greater width of lode at
the same level in the latter mine. I have no
doubt that these deposits will be worked eventu-
ally to great depths.
The treatment of this class of ore is simple, but
requires intelligent and trained management.
If we assume that one ton of each of the samples,
most carefully taken by myself, and assayed under
my direction, were smelted together without re-
gard to fluxes, the results would be as follows -.

and results at a minimum.
At the present market price of copper, if the
mine is worked on a scale of 2000 tons a week, as
is proposed, these figures mean an annual profit of
=£90,000. With skilled management and the large
working capital which will be supplied, I feel
quite confident that my estimate will prove not to
be exaggerated.
Having all these facts under consideration, I
have no hesitation in recommending the Cobar
Chesney Mine as a most valuable property, with
very great potentialities.
Mr. RANDOLPH ADAMS reports :—Viewing
the property in general, I should say the future
policy should be to treat the mine as a sulphide
copper mine carrying gold, the latter metal being
extracted with copper as a base. This appears
clearly proven by the smelting work on the Cobar
Copper Mine, as now carried out. In this mine,
below the sulphide zone, the ore bodies are open
from 40 to 100 ft. in width, in clean, massive sul-
phide of iron, carrying sulphide of copper and a
little gold throughout. The presence of iron in
large quantities allows a general blending of
gossans, carbonate ore, and sulphides, forming a
mixture most readily and cheajjly reduced by
ordinary shaft furnace smelting.
The alterations shown in the Cobar mine from
gossans to carbonate ore, and finally to massive
sulphide, are precisely duplicated in the Chesney
mine.
The mine, even as at present developed, I con-
sider a most valuable and legitimate mining pro-
position, although the past work is really only
superficial so far as proving the mine is concerned.
If the deeper work is carried out there is an ■
assurance of adding quickly to the value of the
property, not only by proving sulphide deposits,
but by furnishing a general combination of sul-
phide and oxidised ores, more economically treated
than could be done with the present exposed
reserves.
The great width of the deposit is proven both at
the surface and at all the levels so far opened.
The enclosing slate country is very regular in
formation, and carries the lode with a very slight
deviation from the vertical, so there is little ques-
tion of it being permanent in depth.
Mr. Thomas Longworth, manager of the
Great Cobar Company, asserts the opinion that
the Chesney lode “is the same lode as that run-
ning through the Great Cobar Copper Mining
Company, which is known as the eastern lode.
The probabilities are that at a greater depth this
ore body will make into a solid body of iron
pyrites, carrying good copper.”
Mr J. F. O’Gorman, manager of the Cobar
Gold Mines, Ltd., says :—The width of vein at
the 350 ft. level is 35ft., all payable, and a first-
class concentrating and smelting ore of higher
grade than in the Cobar mine. Below the mill, in
a flat, are 30,000 tons of railings that will average
Bdwts. These are a valuable asset to the mine.
Mr. Gerhard A. Stockfeld points out, “as a
fact worth serious consideration, that the Cobar
Chesney Mine stands at present in a very similar
position to that occupied within the last few years
by several of the most prominent copper mines of
Australia, such as the Cobar Copper Mining Com-
pany, the Lake George, and others, which, by
dint of the application of superior knowledge of
their requirements, combined with the expendi-
ture of sufficient capital to properly develop and
equip them in such a manner as to render
them capable of yielding an enormous output,
have, from being almost unpayable and abandoned
properties, taken a place among the copper mines
of the world, which, to the public at large, has
come as a gigantic surprise. That the Chesney
Mine is fit to rank with those above-mentioned is
a question long settled in the minds of those who
have thoroughly studied it, and that it will even-
tually do so is also equally certain, and I can con-
fidently recommend the Mine as offering every
chance of success. ”
Mr. Henry Cock asserts :—“ The ore bodies are
simply enormous. It is the largest gold and
copper lode I have seen in Australia. In size and
continuity it much resembles the great silver lode
of Broken Hill. Its size is subject to considerable
variation, but, comparatively speaking, it is a big
lode even in its smallest part. I have no doubt
whatever that it is the same lode as that worked
by the Great Cobar Mine, and that the point of
dislocation will be found by later developments/ 1

Tales of the convict system, by
Pbic® Wabung ; Is. 3d. posted.
Writes “ Gumleaf ” :—
Ed. Bulletin, —The parsonic grip on the throat of civi-
lisation relaxes slowly but surely. I have been taking-
notes at various religious gatherings in Melbourne lately,
and at every one of them heard some pitiful wail about
decreasing attendance and the diminishing amounts
which the threat of everlasting damnation can wring out
of those who do attend. Those anfflinic creatures, the
Christian Endeavorer creatures, bemoan with sickly voice
the loss of the male element from the matrimonial agency
known to them as the “ C.E.” Same lament ran through
proceedings of Anglican Synod and Congregational Union.
All afraid of Marcus Clarke’s “Civilisation without
Delusion.” But education will be too strong for the par-
sonic delusion, as it has been for the witch and wizard
delusion. Dr. Bevan, in his address on “ Religious
thought in England,” altogether burked this question,
except insofar as he admitted that everywhere gatherings
of a religious character have a larger proportion of the
feminine element than the masculine, which fact he
attempted to account for by the astounding crammer
that such is always the case in “gatherings of a more
intellectual character.”
It looks as if success at last awaits the
ceaseless efforts of devoted Mme. Dreyfus to estab-
lish her husband’s innocence of vending military
secrets to Germany. An impression prevails
that the unfortunate artillery-captain was made a
scapegoat for some influential person ; but, whether
or not, it is certain that if his wife hadn’t
; gallantly tackled on his behalf Tsar, Kaiser, and
Pope, Dreyfus would have been allowed to rot out
the short remainder of bis life in the cruellest !
durance on earth—the Cayenne convict-hell.
They were two gallant Permanent Artil'
j lerymen who had been spending a pleasant after-
! noon in Sydney and were now on their way back
to the defence station at Middle Head. They
walked unsteadily on, and sat down obtrusively
at the extreme end of the boat. One was a small
chap, with a warlike air and a very red nose.
He had just had enough grog to make him argu-
mentati ve, and was becoming quite eloquent on the
subject of defence. His mate, a long, sleepy-look-
ing individual, was in a different stage of drunken-
ness, and he sat down in a hog-like manner and
refused to be drawn into any argument. The
little fellow started about the Russians. “Sup-
pose,” said he, “that I wus the General and that
you wus a captain under me, and the Russians
wus coming up the coast in big ships to take
Sydney. Suppose I sed to you ‘ Captain, there’s
2000 men. You’ve got to lick those beggars—fire
low and knock ’em out or any other way ’—-What
would yer do? Would yer let ’em come into the
’arbour ’ere, or would yer let ’em go down to
Bot’ny and land there ? Now which ’d yer do ? ”
“ I’d let ’em do what they damn well pleased,”
growled the Captain. “ Well! Yes ! that’s not
’er bad move,” said the little ’un, loftily. “But
suppose yer was Captain again, and I wus over
yer.” The Captain grunted. “Suppose I wus
the General,” continued the diminutive would-be
commander, “and I sed to yer, ‘Look ’ere, Cap-
tain. There’s the Russians out there and you’ve
got to keep them outside those heads—I’ old you
responsible for the safety of this ’ere ’arbour.’
Wot would you do then ? ” The Captain thumped
the seat savagely with his fist. “I’d tell yer to go
and get d” he yelled. The General suddenly
lost confidence in himself as a commander.
A Boon to Sufferers.— A well-known stock-
dealer and storekeeper announces that, if written
to confidentially, he will reply and state the plan
he pursued by which he was restored to health
and vigor after years of suffering from Nervous
and Physical Weakness, Loss of Vigor, etc. He
writes : “I have no scheme to extort money from
anyone whomsoever. I was victimised by quacks
and empirics until I lost faith in mankind ; but,
thank heaven, I am now well, vigorous, and
strong, and anxious to make this certain means of
cure known to all. Having nothing to sell, I
want no money.” Address—J. KILIAN, Store-
keeper, Daveyston, S.A. #

The Klondike Miner’s Evensong.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
* “Old Man” Eli’s claim (No. 17, or “Dollar-to-the-
Pan ”) on Hunker’s Creek, in the Klondike region. Six
o’clock of an evening, last August, when the sun—con-
cealed for all the rest of the day behind encircling snow-
capped hills —is at last seen in the western outlet of the
canon—the signal for the miners to knock off for supper,
which is generally digested—and it takes a lot of that —
over a game of poker or pedro.
We rustle an’ cuss an’ shiver
Twelve hours of a cheerless day,
Till the sun has struck the river,
And we sling our picks away ;
And up from the shafts we tumble
An’ rub from our han’s the dirt ;
I never hear no man grumble
When it’s time to dry his shirt!
Why—talk o’ the Forty-niner !
His work, alongside this,
Was Sambo a-kissin’ Dinah,
When Dinah calls the kiss !
If his leads ran topsy-turvy,
Our gravel’s fruz’ right through !
We’ve to wrestle with the scurvy—
He’d wholesome greens to chew !
But set the fire a-crackle,
An’ bile tea-leaves fer tea !
Weak tea and cussed hard tack’ll
Still make a meal fer me 1
But gosh ! If I ain’t mistaken,
Pard’s down to something there;
Ain’t that the smell o’ bacon,
That perfumes all the air ?
An’ now, as the ole camp-kettle
Is coolin’ —l git warm !
Them sizzlin’ rashers settle
On my “ rock ” in fust-class form.
Here’s Jake from the deep-down level—
How do ? Jake winks an eye—
Holds up his han’—the devil 1
If it ain’t a bottle o’ rye I
So kick the fire up, hearty !
An’ Joe, pull out the deck !
What I drop to this card-party
Won’t be paid with no blamed cheque !
Fer back o’ the ole man’s bunker
There’s a bean-bag full o’ gold—
Gold ! Here’s good health to Hunker—
There’s more down there in the cold !
This camp is an Eldorado,
But—what’s in a golden name ?
’Tis a valley still o’ the shadow,
If God don’t mark our game ;
Fer I tell ye straight an’ truly
(An’ face it, boys, we must!)
If the boat is late nex’ July,
We’ll have to grub on dust!
An’ then, where we worked, a-shiver,
Deep down from the eye o’ day,
The moon ’ll wink on the river,
An’ the lispin’ waves ’ll say :
“ Them boys is a-sleepin’ steady ;
They are millionaires, but they,
Havin’ banked their piles already,
Can’t tote their banks away ! ”
So put up the cards, you sinners !
An’ down on yer knees, jest so !
An’ pray fer yer gol’-darned dinners,
A-squattin’ there in a row !
Fer I tell ye straight an’ truly,
If the ice shud walk out slow,
An’ the boats ain’t up in July,
’Frisco’s not where you will go !
Canada, N.W.T.
E. B. OSBORN.
Woman’s Ways.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Db. Gallipot, the other day, wrote what he reck-
lessly considered a snappy article, and sent it
along to the Byalong Free and Independent.
The editor saw at a glance it would make up
about three columns, looked at the signature,
thankfully put his scissors down and pushed the
paste-pot further out of his way. He didn’t have
time to read it, unless he sacrificed his customary
afternoon doze in the office chair. In next issue
appeared the long rigmarole under the headingof—
Grave and Gay.
SARCASM AND SATIRE LET LOOSE.
By Charles Gallipot, M.B.
People couldn’t well help noticing the huge slab of
waste ink, occupying nearly half of the back page.
Some imagined it about one thing and others took
it for something else, but no one read it, and
everyone congratulated the doctor so warmly that
he really wished he had taken up literature in-
stead of medicine as a profession. There were a
few who waded in and wrestled with the first
paragraph or two, but an unfortunate impression
existed that the article was a medical treatise or
something of the sort. Consequently the pearls
of satire, which occurred in the third column,
were entirely lost.
Jonks, the banker, whose eye that morning
caught the word “grave” on top of the page,
jumped to the conclusion that it must be a protest
against the disgraceful sanitary condition of the
town. He met the doctor in the street and con-
gratulated him on his “public spiritedness.”
“ Give it ’em hot, Doc. ; wake ’em up. The town’s
worse than a blooming stinkpot! ” The doctor
smiled inanely and wondered what the devil Jonks
meant, anyhow ; but thought it some slangy ex-
pression of approval.
After dinner the doctor awaited some comment
on his literary efforts from Mrs. Gallipot, but the
difficulty of making the waistband of the doctor’s
evening trousers ever meet again was troubling her
sorely, and her thoughts were far away. So he
mentioned the subject himself.
“ What did you think of it, Annie ? ” he asked.
“It was very interesting indeed, dear; but
what was it about ? ”
“What was it about?” he scowled; “didn’t
you read it ? ”
“ Yes, dear, of course ; but I wanted you to ex-
plain it,” she said pleasantly, with a kindly smile.
“ ‘ Explain it ’ ! Devils and thunderstorms 1
There were three whole columns of explanation,
and you ask a silly question like that! ”
“ There you go, losing your temper again because
I only wanted to know what you meant.”
“ What I meant! ”
“ Yes, dear, why did you write it ? It was very
clever; but you know how silly I am. I didn’t
quite follow it, you know. You said something
about hotels and" barmaids and ballet-girls, and in
the next column you mentioned Mr. Gladstone.
What was that for? I didn’t know he was a
publican or an actor. I got quite mixed. I’m
awfully silly, I know. You never trouble to
explain things to me ”
The doctor, on the border-land of insanity, was
plucking his hair out by the roots, when the
patient’s bell rung, and he bounded out of the room
with a demoniac expression on his face.
Mrs. Gallipot thinks the doctor is becoming
more irritable every day.”
Harper’s Weekly depicts Bull with a
Jubilee nightmare of an Indian cobra.
The Salvation Army gets madder as it
gets further West. Out Barcoo way red-shirted
dervishes have taken to masquerading in the
names of prominent politicians and others. Thus
you will be noisily invited by a “ Lord Caring-
ton ” or a “ Chicago Dibbs ” to “do a volley,” or
will be regaled with the life history—the crimes
especially—of “His Nibs Jersey.” Poke-bonneted
women strive to similarly entertain under. the
assumed names of the female leaders of society,
and the most plain and ordinary and yellow poke-
bonnetted female of the lot would as soon call
herself Sarah Bernhardt as anything else. There
is no sense of incongruity about the woman who
struggles heavenward with a straw coal-scuttle on
her head and a discordant top-note in her mouth.
Whom the Gods Love.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Once, when canvassing for a sewing-machinefirm.
I called at a house in a Melbourne suburb. The
lady of the house—a pretty girl of 22 or 23
answered the door.
No, she didn’t want a sewing-machine ; she had
one already. But—it was out of order; did Ido
repairs? I would be glad to have a look at it and
let her know what could be done.
And as I took the machine to pieces I was all
the while watching the girl’s face, fascinated by
its look of pure, complete happiness.
I mentioned her husband, asking if he were a
mechanic, and the lovelight in her eyes was
glorious to see ; then she blushed a rosy red, and
replied, “0 yes, Dick can do almost anything.”
Finally, when I could find no more excuse for
watching the happy face, I took away a broken
cogwheel, promising to bring a duplicate the
following week.
That day week, looking over the morning paper,
I was surprised to see that “Dick” —whose full
name and address corresponded with that in my
book —had been arrested for bigamy, at the
instance of his first wife. Curious, and somewhat
anxious, to see how the little woman would take
it, I approached the house slowly ; but I need not
have worried, for I found all the blinds down, and
neighbors whispering volubly about the villainy of
men.
“ Pretty ” was dead !
Some people have all the luck.
F. CROSON.
Viator : “A bona-fide game at German
picnics in Australia. A rooster is put under a big
crockery basin. Women throw sticks, and who-
ever breaks the basin gets the rooster.”
The Commercial Travellers’ Association of
N.S.W. has an organ, the Commercial, which,
under the literary guidance of P. J. Holdsworth,
heroically endeavors to etherealize, by means of
learned dissertations on Shakespeare and so on,
the drummer’s sordid and unlovely soul. Let not
Holdsworth cast thus his pearls. Wanted —a
Travellers’ ideal organ, bound in deepest blue and
styled (with a double apropos) The Drummek.
L. : Another delusion concerning the Chow
—that he is usually mean. Now, he isn’t. Writer
has known a Chow fruit-vendor, to whom half a
Western town was indebted for supplies, and
John never sued a man. T’other day, Shing Kee,
of Townsville, instructed solicitor Jarvis to take
proceedings, on behalf of Whay Kong, against a
certain Caucasian, for assault. Shing Kee not
only paid counsel’s fee, but also handed final costs
of J 62 Bs. Bd. to hospital. And Townsville identi-
ties swear such a thing hasn’t been done by a
European in the memory of man.

The King of Carabas.
[FOR THE BOLLETIN.]
■‘Wanted—a truthful epitaph for defunct royalty.”—
Morrison Davidson.
And so the King of Carabas is dead,
Bewept by other kings and queens in dozens;
In grief profound the nation bows its head,
And pensions off his five-and-forty cousins,
While Sorrow slowly beats her muffled drum,
And bids the people weep in slimy lane and slum.
He loved not much, but many. In his day,
I rom high-born damsels down to Sue and Sally,
He ran the gamut in his princely way,
And knew each shapely ankle in the ballet ;
His judgment was superb on waists and wine,
Tiie dainty “ fatted calf ” he deemed a thing divine.
He loved the turf, and all the band of Sport
Revered him as of festive life the patron ;
For much he lingered round each gay resort
And scandalised each pious ancient matron.
The Course and all its crew he made a Regal Ring,
Among the jocks he rose—aye, every inch a king !
He laughed at learning, but he lived for Art;
There was no man in all that mighty nation
Could better judge how tailor did his part,
Or fold a tie so sweetly on occasion ;
Was none could half so deftly wield a cue,
Or read in Beauty’s blush if Beauty painted true.
How free he was from pride ! He once confessed
To break the bars of rank was his ambition,
And therefore he preferred plebeians blest
With surplus cash to any poor patrician;
“ I owe my people love ! ” his polished accents
fell—
(How much he owed in coin no soul could ever
tell).
And so the King of Carabas is dead
And grief has rent the great heart of the nation,
And sundry butchers bitter tears have shed,
And paupers weep in endless desolation ;
While fellow Regal Vultures wail the Thing
That one time wore a crown and called itself a
King.
Q-
P. LUFTIG.
“ Werrimee,” to The Bulletin :
A few days before Onepah (N.S.W.) shed cut-out, the
Ooss went round collecting money for Tibooburra hospital.
Everybody gave something except the three men at the
wool-table. They had subscribed at Whittabrunah, and
refused to part a second time within the month. Where-
upon the boss told ’em straight that he intended to join
those squatters who were organising to make it compul-
sory for every man in work to subscribe a certain amount
to the local hospital. This cuts two ways. A man may
be in a shed only long enough to earn 'his subscription,
and be sent on the track the worse for his labor in vain,
or he may work a week in one shed, a week in another,
and so on ; to fork out at each place for the local institu-
tion. The practice is fast becoming universal ; and the
“ reference ” or “ character ” of the future, to be of value,
must set forth in effect the important essential—“ Liberal
subscriber to the local hospital” (“as enforced by P.U.
regulations ” need not be added).
The Bond.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Into the deserted waiting-room, where the
evening shadows were deepening, a woman came
with a slow, uneven footstep. She was the last
patient but one, and even as she crossed the
threshold she heard the door of the doctor’s con-
sulting-room close behind the final case for the
day, and knew that she had already faded out of
the busy man’s mind.
She was only one of many, and her case was not
even an individual one. Daily such conditions as
hers came under the doctor’s notice, and he for-
got her before she had passed out into the hall.
Her fine grey eyes were dazed ; her beautiful
mouth, made to curve so graciously, drooped with
the flabby movement with which great misery
alters the firmest, sweetest lips.
In that little room down the passage a hard
blow had fallen on her, and she sank trembling
into a chair and held her hands to her eyes.
Dream-buds, so tenderly, so carefully loved and
nurtured, had been shrivelled to death in a bitter
wind. Fair-winged hopes were dashed to the
ground, their wings broken, and all flying stopped
for ever. In a word, she had been told she could
never be a mother.
And at home —that fair home of hers —was the
husband, who had often dreamed away to her long-
iupfly °f the little fair-faced child with shining
hair, and eyes of the purest blue who was to walk
beside him some day, and whisper to him tender
child-things with her hand in his.
“ It must be a little girl.”
How often he had said it !
“A little girl, because she will be like you.”
And they had even talked about her name—this
little being whose coming had never been heralded.
The mother called her Star-Baby; the father, little
Annie, for that was the mother’s name, and there
was none better.
And, had there been anyone to listen, he would
have thought their child was already with them, a
real, living little one. Their lips were always
lingering round its name. . . . “I must bring the
little one something in my pocket to-night.” . . .
“ We must never let little Annie pull the cat’s
whiskers, as these children are doing.” . . . “I
should like to buy baby a doll’s perambulator,
like that little girl’s.”
And sometimes the mother would go on tiptoe
to the bedside, and bend down and whisper, “ She
is fast asleep.” And the father, not to be outdone
in this fond, foolish, primeval tenderness, would
say, “I think I hear her calling ‘Daddy,’” and
he would come back presently and say, “ The little
girl was frightened of the dark.”
Father ! Mother ! Never, never could it be.
Her heart sickened at the thought of her hus-
band. To her a grief, a sadness that would
shadow all her life. To him it would be an
anguish that would turn all his future into dead-
sea fruit.
He was a man born to be a father. Every
child loved him and came slowly, hesitatingly as
children will, but always surely, to his side. Ho
had the indescribable art of making conversation
with children. He talked to them as if they were
as old as he, and that is what wins the little
hearts.
They had been married now two years, and for
one the world had held no one but themselves—
he for her only, she for God in him. It was in
the second year she learned of his longing for a
httle life he would love to join to theirs.
Then all the latent motherhood in her sprang to
flame, and burnt with a bright, ever-deepening
glow that sent a warmth over heart and brain,
and changed the.whole aspect of life. Self began
to dwindle, dwindle. The rush of love flowed
over everything that had life—her plants and
flowers, and birds and dogs;—the mystery of
their birth awoke strange thrills in her heart,
and their helplessness brought the wonderful
hud of maternity into fuller, lovelier bloom. Out
of love sprang love, and all the waifs and strays of
the world were touched with this tide of mother
tenderness. She could hardly believe it was she,
herself, who felt so. She had never known that
she would care for children. Every woman loves
to hold a baby and kiss the helpless hands and
feet, and that was all the extent of her love for
children until now.
And now it was all over.
* * * *
“ I saw a tiny thing to-day just like little
Annie,” he was saying.
They were sitting alone on their verandah that
looked over a rose-garden towards the sea. Dusk
was falling, and here and there the light of a
coasting steamer gleamed far out on the Pacific.
Dkl you, dear ? ” she forced herself to answer.
A week, and she had not told him. A subtle
change had come over the brightness of her face ;
depths of her eyes lurked weariness.
( l Her father was a working man.”
You were glad of that; you were able to give
her one of your endless pennies.”
“ You’re a witch. I own I never see these
little things without wanting to give them some-
thing, and she was so tiny, and had just her fair
curls.
For they had often looked, in dreams, on the
face of their little one ; they knew every line and
curve, every light and shade of the little features.
He came and flung himself at her side, and
leaned his head against her knee. He reached up
for her hand, and brought it to lay on his head.
“Big Annie,” he whispered.
“Yes,” she whispered back to him, and tried to
keep the pain out of her voice.
“Do you know what I was thinking? . . . Hove
you—truest, dearest woman—l love you. ... You
seem to me to be more and more beautiful every
day. . . . But sometimes, girl, sometimes we are
a little lonely. . . . We are not enough for each
other. I look forward and see a time when we
might—might care for each other a little less, and
then a little less. . . . But the time will never
come. . . . That is why I want the little one so
much. Did I hurt you?”
“ A little. . . . You might tire of me—might
love me less.”
“Never, never. It is impossible. But it must
be born in me to love a child, and I am stunted
until then. Then I shall love you doubly, trebly,
a hundred thousand times more dearly.”
“But they say,” she murmured—and this was
the poor little one straw she had been clinging to
so desperately the whole week past—“ they say
that a childless man and woman love each other
more dearly than those who are fathers and
mothers. Their love retains its early freshness—
is a truer comradeship. They are all in all to
each other, for there is no one to divide their
hearts with.”
“ That may be true of some, not of us—not of
you, not of me. She will - be the great, enduring,
eternal bond, the only bond that can never
weaken.”
Like a child himself, he whispered against her
knee that he longed so for the little arms to be
round his neck, for the little cheek to be against
his ; that he yearned to carry her about from room
to room in his arms, to buy her dollies, and
little baby toys. . . .
She thought her heart would break.
* * * *
To the old pain, the week-old pain, that
seemed to have begun whole centuries before, there
was now a new one added. He could contemplate
the time when he might tire of her.
Next, the pain would come when he had tired of
her.
V Tired of her ” was an ugly phrase. It was not
quite what he meant. She knew he was hers in
some stronger bond than charm or beauty.
But the atmosphere of his love would soon be
too still and silent for him ; the beauty of it would
jar when the longing for the little arm was strong
on him. Then he would look at her critically,
coldly, and wonder why her presence there no
longer filled the rooms with radiance and a sense
of rest.
Day by day she lost her brightness, her quick
capacity for laughter and warm responsiveness.
Thought ' grew leaden with turning round and
round in the one small place. There were cruel
moments when her secret bit her like the Spartan’s
fox, but she smiled and forced herself to play her
part—-the part that must inevitably be disclosed
some day as hollow.
She forced herself to wander with him through
all his tender imaginings; she made herself respond
to every scheme for the little one’s happiness.
“When she. is old enough we will take her to
Italy, to Paris. How old ought she to be?”
“We will take her when she is eight.”
Life altered again. A new revolution, and she
saw that for herself she could bow to this blow,
and some day even kiss the hand that dealt it.
Her eyes had been opened, and selfishness, which
is blindness, never could return. The mother-love
that had been given to her so lavishly need never
be wasted while the world stretched out around
her, hungering everywhere for love.
But her husband had gathered the little one in
his arms so often that he almost believed she was
there, blue-eved and sunny-haired, with a mouth
like a clover for sweetness. To know that such a
child was never to be born to them would be more
bitter than to have Death take her from his arms.
Down she went to the brink of the grave, p’ay-
ing the part gaily, tenderly all the while. Won-
dering with him how soon the good God would let
them know of little Annie’s coming, whispering to
his whisper that the cling of the little arms was
the sweetest thing in all the world.
And only on the grave’s brink, with Death
staring like a hungry wolf into her face, and her
heart too torn and wounded almost to resist, she
told him.
“There can never be any little Annie.”
And she told him she had known it many
months. Told him the knowledge, and the
thought of the chasm that was to divide their
lives, had been slowly killing her. Everything she
laid bare to him, all her pitiful little mockeries,
her tender, sad deceits.
Then she was ready to turn away and die. But
the hungry wolf had seen the agony on the man’s
face, and fled, knowing its time was not yet.
There, at her side, was the child for whom her
mother-love might flow, this broken-hearted man,
her husband !
N.S.W.
